Agency in Litigation: The Dispute Over Binding Effect
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the binding effect of agency in litigation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the binding effect of agency in litigation, identify the evidence for each position, and explain the practical consequence.

Core Idea
Agency for litigation is valid by consensus, but the dispute is over its binding effect: Abu Hanifa requires opponent's consent; the Sahibayn say it is binding regardless.

Agency in litigation raises a critical question: can you force someone to face your agent in court? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents the internal Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two illustrious students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Understanding this dispute is essential for knowing when an opponent must accept representation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus on validity
Note that all agree agency for litigation is permissible (ja'iz) in itself. The disagreement is not about jawaz but about luzum (binding effect).
Al-Hidayah explicitly states this.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and his evidence
Abu Hanifa holds that agency is valid but not binding on the opponent without his consent. His evidence: the opponent owes a response specifically with his own tongue; delegating it transfers a right, and people vary in skill, so consent is needed.
Also attributed to Ibn Abi Layla.
3
State the position of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and their evidence
They hold that agency is binding even without the opponent's consent, because litigation is an act in one's exclusive right; one may delegate it freely.
This is also the view preferred in al-Nihayah.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under Abu Hanifa, if the opponent refuses to accept the agent, he is not obliged to attend. Under the Sahibayn, he must attend. This has direct implications in court.
Later scholars introduce exceptions and judge's discretion, which are covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالخصومة: [سواء كان مدعيا أو مدعى عليه أي وصح التوكيل بالخصومة، أي بالدعوى الصحيحة أو الجواب الصريح. (م، ط] هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: يجوز التوكيل بالخصومة وإن لم يرض الخصم، وإن لم يكن به عذر؛ لأنه تصرف في خالص حقه، فإن شاء باشره بنفسه وإن شاء فوض إلى غيره، وله أن الجواب مستحق على الخصم بلسانه عيناً، وفي التوكيل نقل هذا الحق إلى غيره، والناس تتفاوت في الخصومة فلا يجوز بغير رضاه، وقال صاحب "الهداية": لا خلاف في الجواز، وإنما الخلاف في اللزوم، فعنده توكيل بالخصومة صحيح غير لازم - حتى لا يلزم الخصم الحضور والجواب بخصومة الوكيل، وهو قول ابن أبي ليلى، وعندهما يلزم حكمه، ويسقط حقه في إحضاره ودعواه وجوابه بنفسه، وفي النهاية الصحيح قولهما. [رمز الحقائق: ١٦٧/٢] إلا أن إلخ استثناء من قوله: برضاء الخصم، تقديره : يشترط رضاء الخصم إلا أن يكون الموكل مريضاً بحيث لا يقدر على المشي إلى مجلس القضاء، ولو أمكن ركوب الدابة والحمل على أيدي الناس يكون التوكيل منه لازماً بلا رضاه، أو يكون غائباً مدة السفر، أو يكون مريداً للسفر ولو قال بأنه يحتال ولا يريد السفر، قيل: يحلفه القاضي، وقيل: لا يحلفه بل ينظر إلى حاله؛ فإن هيئة من يسافر لا يخفى على أحد، فإن كان عليه عدة السفر، يلزمه التوكيل، وإلا لا، أو تكون الموكلة امرأة مخدرة، وهي التي لا يراها غير المحارم من الرجال، ولم تجر عادتها بحضور مجلس الحاكم، ففي هذه الأعذار يجوز التوكيل بلا رضاء الخصم بالإجماع، ومن الأعذار الحيض من المدعى عليها إذا كان الحكم في المسجد، وهو مقيد بما إذا كان الطالب لا يرضى بالتأخير. وأما حيض الطالبة، فهو عذر مطلقاً، والنفاس كالحيض، ومن الأعذار أيضاً الحبس إذا كان من غير القاضي الذي ترافعوا إليه والمتأخرون اختاروا للفتوى أن القاضي إذا علم من الخصم التعنت في إباء التوكيل لا يمكنه من ذلك، ويقبل التوكيل من الموكل بغير رضاه، فإن علم من الموكل القصد إلى الإضرار لصاحبه في التوكيل لا يقبل منه التوكيل إلا برضاء صاحبه وهو اختيار الشمس الأئمة السرخسي. (عيني، فتح)
And for litigation: [whether he is plaintiff or defendant] meaning agency for litigation is valid, i.e., for a valid lawsuit or explicit response. (M, T) This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two companions: agency for litigation is permissible even if the opponent does not consent, even if there is no excuse; because it is an act in his exclusive right; if he wishes he may perform it himself, and if he wishes he may delegate it to another. But he [Abu Hanifa] held that the response is owed by the opponent with his own tongue specifically, and in agency this right is transferred to another, and people vary in litigation, so it is not permissible without his consent. The author of al-Hidayah said: There is no dispute about permissibility, only about binding effect. According to him (Abu Hanifa), agency for litigation is valid but not binding – such that the opponent is not required to attend and respond to the agent's litigation; this is the saying of Ibn Abi Layla. According to them (the two companions), its ruling is binding, and his right to bring him, his lawsuit, and his response by himself is dropped. In al-Nihayah, the correct is their view. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/167] Except that etc. is an exception to his saying: with the opponent's consent. Its implied meaning: the opponent's consent is required except when the principal is so ill that he cannot walk to the court, even if he could ride an animal or be carried by people; then his agency is binding without his consent; or he is absent for the duration of travel; or he intends to travel – even if he says he is plotting and does not intend to travel, it is said the judge should administer an oath, and it is said he should not but rather look at his condition, for the appearance of one who is traveling is not hidden; if he has the equipment for travel, the agency is binding, otherwise not; or the principal is a woman in seclusion (mukhaddarah), i.e., one whom only mahram men see, and she is not accustomed to attending the judge's court; in these excuses, agency is permissible without the opponent's consent by consensus. Among the excuses also is menstruation of the female defendant if the hearing is in the mosque – this is conditioned on the claimant not consenting to delay. As for menstruation of the female claimant, it is an excuse absolutely; postpartum bleeding is like menstruation. Among the excuses also is imprisonment if it is by a judge other than the one before whom they are litigating. The later scholars chose for fatwa that if the judge knows the opponent is being obstinate in refusing agency, he does not allow him that, and accepts agency from the principal without his consent. If he knows the principal intends to harm his companion by appointing an agent, he does not accept the agency except with his companion's consent. This is the choice of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (between Abu Hanifa and the two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the validity and binding nature of agency in litigation (wakalah bi al-khusumah). The passage begins by stating the ruling: agency for litigation is valid according to Abu Hanifa. The text clarifies that litigation includes both being a plaintiff (mudda'i) or defendant (mudda 'alayh). The core dispute: does the opponent's consent (rida al-khasm) affect the binding nature of the agency? According to Abu Hanifa, the opponent's consent is required for the agency to be binding (lazim) on the opponent; otherwise, the opponent is not obliged to attend or respond to the agent. The two companions (Sahibayn) hold that agency for litigation is permissible even without the opponent's consent and even if there is no excuse, because it is the principal's exclusive right to delegate. The text cites al-Hidayah: the disagreement is not about permissibility (jawaz) but about binding effect (luzum). Abu Hanifa's view is that the agency is valid but non-binding, similar to the view of Ibn Abi Layla. The Sahibayn hold it is binding, and the principal's right to personally attend drops. According to al-Nihayah, the correct view is that of the two companions. The passage then discusses exceptions (istithna') to the requirement of opponent's consent: illness (inability to walk), travel (absence during travel or intent to travel), woman in seclusion (mukhaddarah), menstruation (for defendant if hearing in mosque and claimant does not agree to delay, and for claimant absolutely), postpartum bleeding, and imprisonment by a different judge. The later scholars (mutakhkhirin) add that if the opponent is obstinate in refusing to consent, the judge may accept the agency without his consent. Conversely, if the principal intends to harm the opponent by appointing an agent, the agency is not accepted without the opponent's consent. This is the choice of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi, mentioned by Ayni and Fath. The passage thus provides a detailed practical guideline for the permissibility and binding nature of agency in litigation.

Key Terms
خصومة (khuṣūmah): Lawsuit or litigation, including being plaintiff or defendant, presenting claims and responses in court.
لزوم (luzūm): Binding effect; here refers to whether the opponent is obligated to accept the agent's representation.
مخدرة (mukhaddarah): A woman in seclusion, who does not appear before non-mahram men and is not accustomed to attending court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the point of disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the two companions?
A) Validity of agency
B) Binding effect
C) Whether a principal can appoint an agent
D) The definition of litigation

Key Takeaway
Agency for litigation is valid by consensus; the intra-Hanafi dispute is over whether it binds the opponent, with the preferred view being that it is binding without consent.

Exceptions and the Fatwa Practice: When Agency in Litigation Is Binding Without Consent
Exceptions to the requirement of opponent's consent and later Hanafi fatwa practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the excuses that make agency for litigation binding without the opponent's consent and apply the later Hanafi fatwa regarding obstinate parties.

Core Idea
Even where consent is normally required, illness, travel, seclusion, and other valid excuses make agency binding; later scholars also empower the judge to override obstinate refusals.

The previous lesson established the general rule: under the preferred view, agency for litigation is binding even without consent. But what if the principal is sick, traveling, or a woman in seclusion? This lesson examines the exceptions where consent is waived by consensus, and how later Hanafi jurists refined the rule to prevent abuse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle and the exception clause
The text states 'opponent's consent is required except when...' The exceptions are circumstances that excuse the principal from personal attendance.
The exception is introduced by 'illa an'.
2
List the excuses mentioned in the text
The excuses are: illness preventing walking, absence on travel, intention to travel (with judicial verification), woman in seclusion, menstruation/postpartum, imprisonment by a different judge.
Some excuses apply only to defendants or claimants.
3
Understand the later jurists' refinement
The mutakhkhirin (later scholars, like al-Sarakhsi) added that if the opponent is obstinate, the judge can accept the agency without consent; if the principal intends harm, the agency is not accepted.
This balances the rights of both parties.

Exceptions to Requiring Opponent's Consent for Agency
Exceptions to opponent consent in agency and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exceptions to the requirement of opponent's consent for agency and apply the Hanafi position that the opponent is bound by the agent's lawsuit.

Core Idea
Agency without opponent's consent is valid for sick, absent, traveling, or veiled principals.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explores the exceptions to the general rule that agency requires the opponent's consent. The Hanafi school recognizes several valid excuses, and the commentary resolves an intra-Hanafi dispute between Ibn Abi Layla and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). You will learn the relied-upon position and the practical consequences for litigation, including later refinements regarding obstinacy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The base rule is that agency requires the opponent's consent. The passage begins with an exception ('illa) to this rule.
The matn states 'three days' as a default period, but the focus is on exceptions.
2
List the recognized excuses
Sickness, absence for travel duration, intention to travel, veiled woman (mukhadara), menstruation (if defendant and court in mosque), and imprisonment.
These are agreed upon (bi al-ijma) per the text.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
Ibn Abi Layla held that the opponent is not compelled; the two companions held that the opponent is bound and the principal forfeits his right to sue.
The text declares the latter as the correct view (al-sahih).
4
Apply the later fatwa on obstinacy
Later scholars (mutakhkhirun) ruled that if the opponent is obstinate in refusing agency, the judge may compel him. Conversely, if the principal intends harm, agency is not accepted without the opponent's consent.
This is the choice of Imam al-Sarakhsi.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the relied-upon view, the opponent cannot refuse to deal with the agent, and the principal cannot disrupt the proceedings.
This ensures efficiency in litigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو ثلاثة أيام إلا أن يكون الموكل مريضاً أو غائباً مدة السفر، أو مريداً للسفر، أو مخدرة، وبإيفائها واستيفائها إلا في حد وقود حتى لا يلزم الخصم الحضور والجواب بخصومة الوكيل، وهو قول ابن أبي ليلى، وعندهما يلزم حكمه، ويسقط حقه في إحضاره ودعواه وجوابه بنفسه، وفي النهاية الصحيح قولهما. [رمز الحقائق: ١٦٧/٢] إلا أن إلخ استثناء من قوله: برضاء الخصم، تقديره : يشترط رضاء الخصم إلا أن يكون الموكل مريضاً بحيث لا يقدر على المشي إلى مجلس القضاء، ولو أمكن ركوب الدابة والحمل على أيدي الناس يكون التوكيل منه لازماً بلا رضاه، أو يكون غائباً مدة السفر، أو يكون مريداً للسفر ولو قال بأنه يحتال ولا يريد السفر، قيل: يحلفه القاضي، وقيل: لا يحلفه بل ينظر إلى حاله؛ فإن هيئة من يسافر لا يخفى على أحد، فإن كان عليه عدة السفر، يلزمه التوكيل، وإلا لا، أو تكون الموكلة امرأة مخدرة، وهي التي لا يراها غير المحارم من الرجال، ولم تجر عادتها بحضور مجلس الحاكم، ففي هذه الأعذار يجوز التوكيل بلا رضاء الخصم بالإجماع، ومن الأعذار الحيض من المدعى عليها إذا كان الحكم في المسجد، وهو مقيد بما إذا كان الطالب لا يرضى بالتأخير. وأما حيض الطالبة، فهو عذر مطلقاً، والنفاس كالحيض، ومن الأعذار أيضاً الحبس إذا كان من غير القاضي الذي ترافعوا إليه والمتأخرون اختاروا للفتوى أن القاضي إذا علم من الخصم التعنت في إباء التوكيل لا يمكنه من ذلك، ويقبل التوكيل من الموكل بغير رضاه، فإن علم من الموكل القصد إلى الإضرار لصاحبه في التوكيل لا يقبل منه التوكيل إلا برضاء صاحبه وهو اختيار الشمس الأئمة السرخسي. (عيني، فتح)
And it is three days unless the principal is sick or absent for the duration of travel, or intending to travel, or a veiled woman. And by fulfilling and collecting rights except in hadd and qisas. So that the opponent is not obligated to attend and answer the agent's lawsuit. This is the saying of Ibn Abi Layla. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), his ruling is binding, and his right to bring him, sue him, and answer in person is forfeited. And in the end, the correct view is theirs. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/167] 'Except' etc. is an exception from his saying: 'with the opponent's consent,' its implication: the opponent's consent is required unless the principal is so ill that he cannot walk to the court, even if he could ride an animal or be carried by people, then his agency is binding without his (the opponent's) consent; or he is absent for the duration of travel; or he intends to travel, and if he says that he is making an excuse and does not intend to travel, it is said: the judge makes him swear, and it is said: he does not make him swear but looks at his situation; for the appearance of a traveler is not hidden from anyone; if he has the equipment of travel, agency is binding on him, otherwise not; or the principal is a veiled woman, who is not seen by non-mahram men, and she is not accustomed to attending the court. In these excuses, agency is allowed without the opponent's consent by consensus. Among the excuses is menstruation of the female defendant if the trial is in the mosque, and this is restricted to when the plaintiff does not agree to delay. As for the menstruation of the plaintiff, it is an excuse absolutely, and postpartum bleeding is like menstruation. Among the excuses also is imprisonment if it is by other than the judge to whom they referred. The later scholars chose for fatwa that if the judge knows that the opponent is being obstinate in refusing agency, he does not allow him that, and he accepts agency from the principal without his (the opponent's) consent. And if he knows that the principal intends to harm his companion by agency, he does not accept agency from him except with his companion's consent. This is the choice of the leading imams, al-Sarakhsi. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn ruling about the duration of agency (three days) and the exceptions to the requirement of the opponent's consent for agency. The matn states that agency is valid for three days unless the principal has an excuse. The commentator explains that the general rule requires the opponent's consent, but there are exceptions: if the principal is sick, absent on travel, intending to travel, or a veiled woman who does not appear in court. Also, menstruation of the female defendant (if court is in the mosque) and imprisonment are excuses. The passage discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute: Ibn Abi Layla held that the opponent is not compelled; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) hold that the opponent is bound by the agent's lawsuit and loses the right to sue in person. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the two companions. Later scholars (mutakhkhirin) added that if the opponent is obstinate, the judge can allow agency without his consent; but if the principal intends harm, agency is not accepted without consent. This was chosen by Imam al-Sarakhsi. The passage is from al-Hidayah but here it's Kanz al-Daqaiq with commentary.

Key Terms
مخدرة (mukhadara): A woman who is veiled and not seen by non-mahram men, and does not usually attend court.
مندوب إليه (mandub ilayhi): An act that is recommended (mustahabb) in Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what happens to the principal's right to sue in person after he appoints an agent?
A) The principal retains the right to sue in person alongside the agent.
B) The principal loses the right to sue in person; the agent's lawsuit is binding.
C) The opponent may choose whether to deal with the agent or the principal.
D) The principal can revoke the agency at any time and resume his own lawsuit.

Key Takeaway
Agency without opponent's consent is valid in specific excuses, and the judge can compel an obstinate opponent, but must also prevent the principal from using agency to cause harm.

Rights Attach to Agent in Self-Added Contracts When Principal Absent
Ruling and proof for rights attaching to agent in contracts added to himself when principal absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who bears rights and obligations when an agent adds a contract to himself and the principal is absent, and identify the exception for the mahjur agent.

Core Idea
When the principal is absent, the agent bears the rights and obligations of a contract he adds to himself, unless he is under interdiction.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi agency law: the allocation of rights when an agent acts in his own name. The source passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq establishes that unless the agent is prohibited from transacting, he personally assumes liability. The proof relies on the concept of legal doubt (shubha) arising from the possibility that the principal may forgive the agent's action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Determine that when the agent adds the contract to himself (e.g., 'I sell') and the principal is absent, rights and obligations attach to the agent.
The agent is personally liable for delivery, payment, etc.
2
Note the exception
If the agent is mahjur (minor or slave), the rule does not apply; rights attach to the principal to protect the other party.
The mahjur agent lacks legal capacity to be bound.
3
Locate the proof
The proof is istihsan based on the presence of shubha (doubt) due to the possibility that the principal might forgive the agent's act, since forgiveness is recommended.
This doubt prevents the rights from attaching to the principal when he is absent.
4
Understand the analogy
Similar shubha is recognized in other cases like a judge writing to another judge, testimony upon testimony, and women's testimony with men.
These analogies illustrate the principle of caution when doubt exists.
5
Distinguish the present principal
When the principal is present, there is no possibility of forgiveness because he can object directly, so rights attach to him.
The absence of doubt changes the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن غاب المؤكل، والحقوق فيما يضيفه الوكيل إلى نفسه كالبيع والإجارة والصلح عن إقرار تتعلق بالوكيل إن لم يكن محجوراً كتسليم المبيع. لما في ذلك من ضرب شبهة كما في كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي والشهادة على الشهادة، وشهادة النساء الرجال؛ لأن في صورة غيبة الموكل احتمال العفو ثابت فإن العفو مندوب إليه، بخلاف ما إذا حضر الموكل؛ لانتفاء هذا الاحتمال
If the principal is absent, the rights in what the agent adds to himself, such as sale, lease, and compromise concerning an admission, attach to the agent provided he is not under interdiction (minor or slave), such as delivery of the sold item. This is because in that there is a kind of doubt, as in the case of a judge writing to a judge, and testifying upon testimony, and women testifying with men; because in the case of the principal's absence, the possibility of forgiveness exists, for forgiveness is recommended, unlike when the principal is present, because this possibility is absent.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage states the general rule in Hanafi law: when an agent acts in a contract that he attributes to himself (i.e., he does not say 'on behalf of my principal'), and the principal is absent, the legal rights and obligations arising from that contract attach to the agent personally. This includes delivery of the sold item, receipt of the purchase price, and lawsuits for defects. The exception is if the agent is under legal interdiction (mahjur), such as being a minor or a slave, in which case the rights attach to the principal to protect the other party. The proof (dalil) for this ruling is the presence of a legal doubt (shubha) arising from the possibility that the principal might forgive the agent's action, since forgiveness is a recommended act. This doubt does not exist when the principal is present, because he can directly object. Similar doubts are recognized in other areas: e.g., a judge writing to another judge (where the second judge may not act due to doubt), testimony upon testimony, and women testifying with men. Therefore, the law is cautious and attaches the rights to the agent.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakīl): The agent who acts on behalf of another (the principal) in legal transactions.
المؤكل (al-muwwakil): The principal who authorizes the agent to act on their behalf.
محجور (maḥjūr): A person under legal interdiction due to minority or slavery, whose transactions may be restricted.
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt that prevents the application of a penal or severe ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, when an agent adds a sale contract to himself and the principal is absent, to whom do the rights and obligations attach?
A) The principal
B) The agent
C) The agent unless he is mahjur
D) Both principal and agent jointly

Key Takeaway
When an agent adds a contract to himself and the principal is absent, rights and obligations attach to the agent unless the agent is under legal interdiction.

Ownership and Procedural Rights in Hanafi Agency
Ruling with proof: ownership and rights in agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that ownership vests in the principal while procedural rights attach to the agent, explain the Hanafi proof using the concept of khilafa and the slave analogy, and apply this distinction to contracts like marriage and compromise.

Core Idea
Ownership belongs to the principal; rights of possession and suit belong to the agent.

In Hanafi law, when an agent acts for a principal in a sale, ownership of the purchased item immediately vests in the principal. However, certain procedural rights—such as taking delivery, collecting the price, suing for defects, or reclaiming if the item is owned by another—attach to the agent. This separation, which the Shafi'i school contests, is justified through the concept of deputyship (khilafa) and analogy with a slave's acts. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and its application to various contracts and disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rights Attaching to the Agent
Read the first line: 'Wa qabduhū wa qabd al-thaman wa al-rujū‘ ‘inda al-istiḥqāq wa al-khuṣūma fī al-‘ayb’ – receipt, price receipt, recourse upon claim of right, and litigation for defects are rights that the agent exercises in his own name, even though ownership belongs to the principal.
These are procedural (wad‘ī) rights, not substantive ownership.
2
State the Ownership Rule and Its Consequence
The text states 'al-mulk yathbutu lil-muwakkil ibtidā’' – ownership is established for the principal from the outset. A concrete consequence: if the agent buys his own relative (e.g., father) by agency, the relative does not become free, because ownership never passes to the agent.
This distinguishes Hanafi agency from a direct purchase.
3
Understand the Shafi‘i Objection
Imam al-Shafi‘i argued that rights follow the ruling of the act; since the entire disposition (taṣarruf) is for the principal, the rights should also go to the principal. This objection is the basis of the ikhtilāf.
The Shafi‘i view treats the agent as a mere extension of the principal.
4
Grasp the Hanafi Response via Dual Capacity and Khilāfa
The Hanafi answer: the agent has two capacities – as author of the contract (qiyās) and as deputy (khilāfa). Both must be honored. Ownership goes to the principal (the purpose of agency), but rights attach to the agent to preserve the efficacy of the act. The analogy of a slave accepting a gift (where the master gets ownership but the slave's action is the cause) supports this.
The slave analogy shows that the cause (sabab) and the ruling (ḥukm) can be split.
5
Apply the Rule to Contracts Added to the Principal
In contracts like marriage, manumission, gift, and compromise over a denied claim, the agent must explicitly add the contract to the principal. If he adds it to himself, he becomes a pure messenger (safīr) and the contract is his own. In sulḥ over an admission, it resembles a sale and rights attach to the agent.
The key is whether the agent's role is that of a deputy (khilāfa) or a messenger (safīr).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقبضه وقبض الثمن والرّجوع عند الاستحقاق والخصومة في العيب، والملك يثبت للموكل ابتداء، حتى لا يعتق قريب الوكيل بشرائه، وفيما يضيفه إلى الموكل كالنكاح لا للوكيل. يثبت للموكل جواب عما قال الشافعي: إن الحقوق تابعة لحكم التصرف، والحكم يتعلق بالموكل، فكذا توابعه، وتقرير الجواب: أن الملك يقع للموكل، ولكن يعقد الوكيل على سبيل الخلافة عنه، ومعنى الخلافة أن يثبت الملك للموكل ابتداء، والسبب انعقد موجباً حكمه للوكيل، فكان قائماً مقامه في ثبوت الملك بالتوكيل السابق، وتحقيق المسألة : أن لتصرف الوكيل جهتين: جهة حصوله بعبارته وجهة حصول صوله نيابة عن الموكل وإعمالهما ولو بوجه أولى من إهمال أحدهما، فلو أثبتنا الملك والحقوق للوكيل على ما هو مقتضى القياس؛ لحصولهما بعبارته وأهليته بطل التوكيل، ولو أثبتناهما للموكل بطل عبارته فأثبتنا الملك للموكل؛ لأنه الغرض من التوكيل. وإليه أشار المصنف بقوله : "ابتداء" فتعين الحقوق للوكيل، ويجوز أن يثبت الحكم بغير من انعقد له السبب كالعبد يقبل الهدية والصدقة ويصطاد فإن مولاه يقوم مقامه في الملك بذلك السبب وجواب عن سؤال مقدر أيضاً، تقديره: إذا كانت الحقوق في هذا الفعل راجعة إلى الوكيل، ينبغي أن يعتق قريبه إذا اشتراه بالوكالة؛ لأن شراء القريب إعتاق، فأجاب عنه بقوله: "والملك يثبت للموكل "ابتداء أي في ابتداء الأمر خلافة عنه بمعنى الوكيل أصل في حق العقد، لكن في حق الحكم يخلفه الموكل، فيقع له من غير أن يكون أصلاً فيه كالعبد يتهب أو يصطاد، فكما أن المولى يثبت الملك له ابتداء خلافة عنه، فكذا الموكل يثبت الملك فيما اشتراه وكيلاً خلافة عنه. (عيني، وعناية بشرائه وكذا لا يفسد نكاحه إذا اشترى زوجته بالوكالة. (عيني) وفيما يضيفه إلى الموكل [ أي والحقوق في العقد الذي يضيفه. (ط عيني] يعني لا يستغني عن إضافته إلى موكله حتى لو أضافه إلى نفسه كان العقد له فيصير كالرسول، وقوله: "كالنكاح" وكذا العتق على مال، والكتابة والهبة والتصدق والإعارة والإيداع والرهن والإقراض والشركة والمضاربة والتوكيل بالقبض، وأما الوكيل بالاستقراض، فإن أضافه إلى الموكل كان للموكل، وإلا كان للوكيل، وقوله: "وعن إنكار" قيد به؛ لأنه إذا كان الصلح عن إقرار يكون كالبيع، فترجع الحقوق إلى الوكيل كما في البيع، وإذا كان عن إنكار فهو فداء يمين في حق المدعى عليه، فالوكيل سفير محض، فلا ترجع إليه الحقوق. (فتح، عناية)
And [the rights include] its receipt, receipt of the price, recourse upon claim of right (istihqaq), and litigation for defects. And ownership is established for the principal from the outset, so that the agent's relative does not become free by his purchase [of that relative]. And in [contracts] that the agent adds to the principal, like marriage, [the rights attach] to the principal, not to the agent. It is established for the principal in answer to what al-Shafi'i said: 'Rights are subsidiary to the ruling of the disposition, and the ruling pertains to the principal, so too its subsidiaries.' The explanation of the answer is that ownership accrues to the principal, but the agent contracts by way of deputyship (khilafa) from him. The meaning of deputyship is that ownership is established for the principal from the outset, while the cause [the contract] is concluded expressing its ruling for the agent, so he stands in his place in the establishment of ownership by virtue of the prior appointment. The reality of the matter is that the agent's disposition has two aspects: one by his own expression, and the other by way of acting on behalf of the principal; we must give effect to both if possible rather than neglecting one. If we established ownership and rights for the agent—as would be required by analogy (qiyas) because they arise from his expression and capacity—the agency would be void; if we established them for the principal, his [the agent's] expression would be nullified. So we establish ownership for the principal because that is the purpose of agency. To this the author pointed with his statement 'from the outset' (ibtida'). Thus the rights are specified for the agent. And it is permissible for a ruling to be established for someone other than the one for whom the cause arose, as in the case of a slave accepting a gift, charity, or hunting: his master takes his place in ownership by that cause. And [this is] also an answer to an implicit question: if the rights in this act revert to the agent, then his relative ought to be freed if he buys him by agency, because buying a relative is [like] freeing. So he answered with his statement 'and ownership is established for the principal from the outset', meaning from the beginning, as deputyship from him. Thus the agent is the originator in the contract, but in terms of the ruling the principal deputizes him. [The ruling] accrues to him without the agent being the originator in it, like the slave receiving a gift or hunting. So just as the master's ownership is established for him from the outset by deputyship, so too the principal's ownership is established in what his agent purchases by deputyship from him. And also it does not invalidate his marriage if he buys his wife by agency. And regarding what the agent adds to the principal, meaning the rights in the contract that he adds [to the principal]—that is, he does not dispense with adding it to his principal; if he adds it to himself, the contract is for him and he becomes like a mere messenger. And his statement 'like marriage' and similarly manumission for money, kitaba, gift, charity, loan, deposit, pledge, loan, partnership, mudaraba, and appointment to receive. As for the agent for borrowing, if he adds it to the principal then it is for the principal, otherwise for the agent. And his statement 'and regarding denial' restricts it; because if the compromise is concerning an admission, it is like a sale, so the rights revert to the agent as in sale; if it is concerning a denial, it is a ransom of oath on behalf of the defendant, so the agent is a pure messenger and rights do not revert to him.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage elaborates on several rights in agency: the agent's right to receive the sold item, receive the price, have recourse against the seller if the item is repossessed (istihqaq), and sue for defects. Despite these rights attaching to the agent, ownership of the purchased item is established for the principal from the commencement of the contract. This is crucial: if ownership went to the agent, then buying his own relative (e.g., father) would free that relative, but since ownership is the principal's, the agent's relative is not freed. The text then responds to Imam al-Shafi'i, who argued that rights follow the ruling of the act, and since the ruling applies to the principal, rights should also go to the principal. The Hanafi answer is that the agent has two capacities: as the contract's direct author and as the principal's deputy. Both must be honored. Ownership is given to the principal because that is the goal of agency, while rights in the contract (like possession and suit) are given to the agent to make the agency effective. This is comparable to a slave who accepts a gift or hunts: his master gets ownership, but the slave's action is the cause. Contracts that the agent must add to the principal (like marriage, manumission, gift, etc.) do not transfer these rights to the agent; the agent is a mere messenger (safir). For compromise (sulh), if it is over an admitted debt, it resembles a sale and rights attach to agent; if over a denied claim, the agent just relays the oath and rights stay with principal. The passage ends by distinguishing cases where the agent adds the contract to himself versus to the principal.

Key Terms
الاستحقاق (al-istiḥqāq): Recourse or reclaiming of property from the buyer when it turns out to be owned by someone else.
الخلافة (al-khilāfa): Deputyship; the agent acts not as original but as deputy, making the principal the real owner.
سفير (safīr): A pure messenger who merely conveys the principal's words and has no personal liability or rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the agent bought his father
B) No, because ownership vests in the principal
C) Yes, because the agent is the direct buyer
D) No, because the father is not a slave

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi agency, ownership always vests in the principal from the beginning, while procedural rights like possession and suit belong to the agent, based on the concept of deputyship (khilāfa) and juristic preference (istihsān).

Property Vesting in the Principal
States the rule of property vesting and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that property vests in the principal in agency and explain two practical consequences: the agent’s relative is not freed and the contract must be attributed to the principal.

Core Idea
Property vests in the principal from the start, not the agent.

In agency, a fundamental question arises: does the agent own the property he acquires? The Hanafi school firmly establishes that ownership belongs to the principal from the moment the contract is made. This rule has immediate practical effects, such as preventing the agent from freeing his relative by purchase and requiring the agent to attribute the contract to the principal. This lesson unpacks these consequences and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Locate in the passage the sentence stating that property is established for the principal from the beginning.
The rule is expressed in the first line after the book title.
2
Note the consequence regarding relatives
Observe that if the agent purchases a close relative, that relative is not freed. Explain why: ownership belongs to the principal, not the agent.
This distinguishes agency from a direct purchase where the buyer’s relative would be freed.
3
Note the requirement of attribution
The agent must attribute the contract to the principal. If he attributes it to himself, the contract becomes his and he is like a messenger.
Attribution is crucial for the contract to be valid as agency.
4
Understand the purpose
The purpose of agency is to benefit the principal. Therefore, ownership goes to the principal. The agent’s capacity is secondary.
This teleological reasoning supports the rule.

(ط عيني] يعني لا يستغني عن إضافته إلى موكله حتى لو أضافه إلى نفسه كان العقد له فيصير كالرسول، وقوله: "كالنكاح" وكذا العتق على مال، والكتابة والهبة والتصدق والإعارة والإيداع والرهن والإقراض والشركة والمضاربة والتوكيل بالقبض، وأما الوكيل بالاستقراض، فإن أضافه إلى الموكل كان للموكل، وإلا كان للوكيل، وقوله: "وعن إنكار" قيد به؛ لأنه إذا كان الصلح عن إقرار يكون كالبيع، فترجع الحقوق إلى الوكيل كما في البيع، وإذا كان عن إنكار فهو فداء يمين في حق المدعى عليه، فالوكيل سفير محض، فلا ترجع إليه الحقوق. (فتح، عناية)
كالنكاح والخلع والصلح عن دم عمد، وعن إنكار يتعلق بالموكل، فلا يطالب أي والصلح عن إنكار وكيله بالمهر ووكيلها بتسليمها وللمشتري منع الموكل عن الثمن، وإن دفع إليه صح، ولا يطالبه الوكيل ثانياً .
أي وكيل الزوج 6 أي دفعه إلى الموكل المشتري : المشتري الثمن يتعلق بالموكل: [لأن الوكيل في هذه الأشياء سفير محض فلا يستغني عن إضافته إلى الموكل. (عيني)] فوكيلها بالنكاح لا يلي قبض مهرها وبالخلع لا يلي قبض البدل، ويصح ضمانه ،مهرها، وتخير بين مطالبته أو الزوج، وإنما تعلقت الحقوق في هذه العقود بالموكل؛ لأن الحكم فيها لا يقبل الفصل عن السبب؛ لأنه إسقاط، فيتلاشى، فلا يتصور صدوره من شخص، ويتوقف حكمه لغيره، فكان سفيراً محضاً؛ ليقترن الحكم بالسبب، بخلاف البيع، فإن حكمه يقبل الفصل كما في البيع بخيار. (عيني، فتح) بالمهر: أي مهر المرأة التي تزوجت بالموكل. (عيني) بتسليمها : أي بتسليم المرأة التي زوجها من الموكل. (عيني) وللمشتري منع الموكل أي إذا باع الوكيل بالبيع، ثم طلب الموكل ثمن المبيع من المشتري، يجوز للمشتري أن يمنع الثمن إياه، ولا يدفعه إليه ؛ لأن الموكل أجنبي عن العقد وحقوقه؛ لأنها تتعلق بالعاقد كما مر ، ولهذا لو حلف المشتري من الوكيل أنه لا شيء للموكل عليه كان باراً، ولو حلف أنه لا شيء عليه للوكيل كان حانثاً. (عيني، فتح) ولا يطالبه الوكيل ثانياً: أي لا يطالب الوكيل المشتري ثانياً؛ لأنه برئ ذمة المشتري بدفع الثمن إلى المستحق وهو الموكل، ولهذا لو كان للمشتري دين على الموكل تقع المقاصة بمجرد العقد، ولو كان له دين عليهما تقع المقاصة بدين الموكل دون دين ،الوكيل ولو كان له دين على الوكيل فقط وقعت المقاصة به، ويضمن الوكيل للموكل؛ لأنه قضى دينه بمال الموكل خلافاً لأبي يوسف بخلاف ما إذا باع مال اليتيم، ودفع المشتري الثمن إلى اليتيم، حيث لا تبرأ ذمته، بل يجب عليه الدفع للوصي ثانياً؛ لأن اليتيم ليس له قبض ماله فكان الدفع إليه تضييعاً، وبخلاف الوكيل في الصرف إذا صارف وقبض الموكل بدل الصرف، حيث يبطل الصرف، ولا يعتد بقبضه؛ لأن جواز الصرف تعلق بالقبض قبل الافتراق فكان القبض فيه بمنزلة الإيجاب والقبول وهما يتعلقان بالمتعاقدين والموكل ليس بعاقد رمز الحقائق: ١٦٩/٢]
Book of Agency. Rulings of Agency. Its taking possession and taking possession of the price and return upon revocation and litigation regarding defect, and property is established for the principal from the beginning, so that the relative of the agent is not freed by his purchase, and in what he attributes to the principal. Not for the agent, like his father or his son (the agent). His taking possession: i.e., taking possession of the sold item if the agent is a buyer. (‘Ayni) At the time of revocation (i.e., recourse against the seller if the agent is a buyer). (‘Ayni) It includes two matters: First: when the agent is a seller and takes possession of the price, then the sold item is revoked, the buyer has recourse for the price against the agent, whether the price remains in his hand or he delivered it to the principal, and he has recourse against his principal. Second: when he is a buyer and the sold item is revoked from his hand, then he has recourse for the price against the seller, but not all? (Maw, ‘Ayni, Fath) And litigation: i.e., the agent’s litigation with the seller regarding purchase. (‘Ayni) It is established for the principal – a response to what al-Shafi‘i said: Verily rights follow the ruling of the act, and the ruling pertains to the principal, so likewise its subsidiaries. The summary of the response: that property belongs to the principal, but the agent contracts by way of substitution (khilafah) on his behalf, and the meaning of substitution is that property is established for the principal from the beginning, while the cause is concluded making its ruling applicable to the agent, so he stands in his place in the establishment of property by the prior authorization. The verification of the issue: that the agent’s act has two aspects: the aspect of its occurrence by his own statement, and the aspect of its occurrence by way of representation of the principal. Acting upon both, even in one way, is better than neglecting either. So if we establish property and rights for the agent, as required by analogy (qiyas), because they occur by his statement and his qualification, the authorization would become void. And if we establish them for the principal, his statement becomes void. So we establish property for the principal, because that is the purpose of the authorization. The author alluded to this by his saying: “from the beginning.” So rights are determined for the agent? (wa qad?) And it is permissible that a ruling be established for someone other than the one for whom the cause is concluded, like a slave who accepts a gift, charity, and hunts – indeed his master takes his place in property by that cause. And it is a response to an implied question: If rights in this action revert to the agent, then his relative should be freed if he purchases him by agency, because the purchase of a relative is manumission. He answered with his saying: “and property is established for the principal from the beginning,” i.e., at the beginning of the affair by substitution on his behalf, meaning the agent is the origin regarding the contract, but regarding the ruling, the principal substitutes him, so it occurs for him without him being the origin in it, like the slave who receives a gift or hunts – just as the master establishes property for himself from the beginning by substitution on his behalf, so too the principal establishes property in what his agent purchased by substitution on his behalf. (‘Ayni and ‘Inayah) And likewise his marriage is not invalidated if he purchases his wife by agency. (‘Ayni) And in what he attributes to the principal (i.e., rights in the contract which he attributes to him) (Ta‘ ‘Ayni) meaning he is not independent of attributing it to his principal, so that if he attributes it to himself, the contract becomes his and he becomes like a messenger. And his saying: “like marriage,” and likewise manumission for compensation, kitabah, gift, charity, lending, deposit, pledge, loan, partnership, mudarabah, and authorization for taking possession. As for the agent for borrowing, if he attributes it to the principal, it is for the principal, otherwise it is for the agent. And his saying: “and regarding denial” is a restriction; because if the compromise is regarding admission, it is like a sale, so rights revert to the agent as in a sale; and if it is regarding denial, it is a ransom for the oath concerning the defendant, so the agent is a pure intermediary, so rights do not revert to him. (Fath, ‘Inayah) Like marriage, khula‘, and compromise regarding intentional killing and regarding denial – it pertains to the principal, so the agent (i.e., the agent of the husband) is not asked for the dower, nor is her agent asked for handing her over (i.e., handing over the woman whom he married to the principal). And the buyer may withhold the price from the principal, and if he pays it to him, it is valid, and the agent does not demand it from him a second time. (i.e., the agent of the husband) (i.e., his payment to the principal buyer) The buyer (the price) pertains to the principal: [because the agent in these things is a pure intermediary, not independent of attributing to the principal. (‘Ayni)] So her agent in marriage is not entitled to receive her dower, and in khula‘ he is not entitled to receive the compensation. And his guarantee of her dower is valid, and he may choose between demanding it from him or the husband. And rights are only attached to the principal in these contracts because the ruling in them is not separable from the cause, because it is a cancellation, so it annihilates, thus it cannot be imagined to originate from one person while its ruling depends on another; so he is a pure intermediary so that the ruling is connected to the cause, unlike sale, for its ruling is separable, as in a sale with an option. (‘Ayni, Fath) Meaning the dower of the woman who married the principal. (‘Ayni) Meaning handing over the woman whom he married from the principal. (‘Ayni) And the buyer may withhold the principal – i.e., if the agent sold, then the principal demands the price of the sold item from the buyer, the buyer may withhold the price from him and not pay it to him; because the principal is a stranger to the contract and its rights, for they pertain to the contractor as previously. Hence, if the buyer swears by the agent that there is nothing for the principal against him, he is truthful; and if he swears that there is nothing for the agent against him, he is false. (‘Ayni, Fath) And the agent does not demand it a second time: i.e., the agent does not demand the buyer a second time, because the buyer’s liability is discharged by paying the price to the rightful owner, i.e., the principal. Hence, if the buyer had a debt against the principal, set-off occurs immediately by the mere contract; and if he had a debt against both, set-off occurs with the principal’s debt but not the agent’s debt; and if he had a debt against the agent only, set-off occurs with it, and the agent becomes liable to the principal because he satisfied his debt with the principal’s property, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Unlike the case where he sells the property of an orphan and the buyer pays the price to the orphan, where his liability is not discharged, rather he must pay it to the guardian a second time because the orphan is not entitled to take possession of his property, so paying him is wasteful. And unlike the agent in currency exchange (sarf) if he exchanges and the principal takes possession of the exchange, where the exchange is invalidated and his taking possession is not considered because the permissibility of exchange is tied to taking possession before separation, so taking possession in it is like the offer and acceptance, and they pertain to the two contractors, and the principal is not a contractor. (Ramz al-Haqa’iq: 2/169)
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa’iq discusses the general principle that in agency contracts, ownership and rights vest in the principal (muwakkil) from the inception of the contract, not in the agent (wakil). The author first states the ruling: property vests in the principal from the beginning. This has practical consequences: if the agent purchases a close relative, that relative is not freed (as would happen if the agent owned the property), because the property belongs to the principal, not the agent. The agent must also attribute the contract to the principal. The passage then provides a response to Imam al-Shafi‘i, who argued that since rights follow the ruling of the act (hukm al-tasarruf), and the ruling pertains to the principal, rights should also vest in the principal. The Hanafi response, as explained in the text, is that ownership is established for the principal, but the agent acts as a substitute (khalifah). The contract is concluded by the agent’s statement, but the legal effect (ownership) is attributed to the principal. This is justified by considering the two aspects of the agent’s action: his own statement and his representation. To give full effect to both, we attribute ownership to the principal (the purpose of the agency) but also give the agent rights related to the contract (like litigation and taking possession) because they arise from his statement. The text then distinguishes between types of agency. In some contracts, like marriage, khula‘, and compromise regarding intentional killing or denial, the agent is a mere intermediary (safir mahd) because the ruling (e.g., dissolution of marriage, cancellation of rights) cannot be separated from the cause. In these cases, all rights pertain exclusively to the principal, and the agent cannot handle the receipt of dower or compensation. In contrast, in sales, rights are separable, so the agent can handle receipt of the price and litigation. Finally, the text discusses the buyer’s right to withhold payment from the principal and the agent’s discharge upon payment to the principal. It provides examples of set-off and distinguishes the case of an orphan (where payment to the orphan is null) and currency exchange (where the principal cannot receive the exchange). The passage ends by noting that Imam Abu Yusuf differs on the liability of the agent when he pays the principal’s debt from the principal’s property.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wakalah): A contract whereby one person (the principal) appoints another (the agent) to perform a legal act on his behalf.
الموكل (al-muwakkil): The principal who appoints the agent.
الوكيل (al-wakil): The agent who acts on behalf of the principal.
الخلافة (al-khilafah): Substitution; the agent stands in the place of the principal for the purpose of the contract.
سفير محض (safir mahd): A pure intermediary; one who merely conveys the contract without any personal rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an agent purchases a house for his principal, who owns the house from the moment of purchase?
A) The agent, because he is the one who contracted.
B) The principal, because property vests in the principal from the beginning.
C) Both jointly.
D) The seller until the price is paid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi agency, ownership vests in the principal from the start, not the agent.

intermediate
8 min
The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Rights in Agency
Presents the Shafi'i objection and the Hanafi response, with detailed reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi response to al-Shafi'i's argument that all rights follow the act, and identify the distinction between agents with rights and pure intermediaries.

Core Idea
Rights split: property goes to principal, litigation stays with agent.

Imam al-Shafi'i argued that since the ruling of the act pertains to the principal, all rights should also pertain to him. The Hanafi response is nuanced: they split the rights. Ownership goes to the principal, but procedural rights like litigation and receipt remain with the agent. This lesson examines that dispute and the Hanafi justification using the dual-aspect theory (two aspects of the agent's act) and the analogy of the slave. It also distinguishes contracts where the agent is a pure intermediary (safir mahd) from those where he retains rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i argument
Locate where the passage says 'al-Shafi'i said: rights follow the ruling of the act, and the ruling pertains to the principal, so likewise its subsidiaries.'
This is the core objection.
2
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafis agree that property goes to the principal, but they argue that the agent's statement generates rights for the agent himself. The concept of substitution (khilafah) and dual aspects (two aspects of the act) are key.
The agent's act has two sides: his own statement and representation.
3
Analyze the dual-aspect theory
If we give all rights to the agent, agency becomes void. If we give all to the principal, the agent's statement is void. So we split: property to principal, litigation to agent.
This preserves both the purpose of agency and the agent's capacity.
4
Examine the slave analogy
The text gives an analogy: a slave accepts a gift, but the master owns it. Similarly, the agent's action results in ownership for the principal.
This shows it is possible for a ruling to be established for someone not performing the act.
5
Distinguish safir mahd contracts
In some contracts (marriage, khula'), the agent is a pure intermediary because the ruling cannot be separated from the cause. In sales, the agent retains rights.
This is a further refinement of the Hanafi position.

كتاب الوكالة
أحكام الوكالة
وقبضه وقبض الثمن والرّجوع عند الاستحقاق والخصومة في العيب، والملك يثبت للموكل ابتداء، حتى لا يعتق قريب الوكيل بشرائه، وفيما يضيفه إلى الموكل . لا للوكيل مثل أبيه أو ابنه الوكيل قبضه أي قبض المبيع إذا كان الوكيل مشتريا. (عيني) عند الاستحقاق [ أي رجوع على البائع إذا كان الوكيل مشتريا. (عيني)] شامل للمسألتين: الأولى : ما إذا كان الوكيل بائعاً وقبض الثمن، ثم استحق المبيع، فإن المشتري يرجع بالثمن على الوكيل، سواء كان الثمن باقياً في يده، أو سلمه إلى الموكل، وهو يرجع على موكله، الثانية: ما إذا كان مشترياً، فاستحق المبيع من يده فإنه يرجع بالثمن على البائع دون كله. مو (عيني، فتح) والخصومة: أي خصومة الوكيل بالشراء من البائع. (عيني) يثبت للموكل جواب عما قال الشافعي: إن الحقوق تابعة لحكم التصرف، والحكم يتعلق بالموكل، فكذا توابعه، وتقرير الجواب: أن الملك يقع للموكل، ولكن يعقد الوكيل على سبيل الخلافة عنه، ومعنى الخلافة أن يثبت الملك للموكل ابتداء، والسبب انعقد موجباً حكمه للوكيل، فكان قائماً مقامه في ثبوت الملك بالتوكيل السابق، وتحقيق المسألة : أن لتصرف الوكيل جهتين: جهة حصوله ،بعبارته وجهة حصول صوله نيابة عن الموكل وإعمالهما ولو بوجه أولى من إهمال أحدهما، فلو أثبتنا الملك والحقوق للوكيل على ما هو مقتضى القياس؛ لحصولهما بعبارته وأهليته بطل التوكيل، ولو أثبتناهما للموكل بطل عبارته فأثبتنا الملك للموكل؛ لأنه الغرض من التوكيل. وإليه أشار المصنف بقوله : "ابتداء" فتعين الحقوق للوكيل، ويجوز أن يثبت الحكم بغير من انعقد له السبب كالعبد يقبل الهدية ،والصدقة ،ويصطاد فإن مولاه يقوم مقامه في الملك بذلك السبب وجواب عن سؤال مقدر أيضاً، تقديره: إذا كانت الحقوق في هذا الفعل راجعة إلى الوكيل، ينبغي أن يعتق قريبه إذا اشتراه بالوكالة؛ لأن شراء القريب إعتاق، فأجاب عنه بقوله: "والملك يثبت للموكل "ابتداء أي في ابتداء الأمر خلافة عنه بمعنى الوكيل أصل في حق العقد، لكن في حق الحكم يخلفه الموكل، فيقع له من . غير أن يكون أصلاً فيه كالعبد يتهب أو يصطاد، فكما أن المولى يثبت الملك له ابتداء خلافة عنه، فكذا الموكل يثبت الملك فيما اشتراه وكيلاً خلافة عنه. (عيني، وعناية بشرائه وكذا لا يفسد نكاحه إذا اشترى زوجته بالوكالة. (عيني) وفيما يضيفه إلى الموكل [ أي والحقوق في العقد الذي يضيفه. (ط عيني] يعني لا يستغني عن إضافته إلى موكله حتى لو أضافه إلى نفسه كان العقد له فيصير كالرسول، وقوله: "كالنكاح" وكذا العتق على مال، والكتابة والهبة والتصدق والإعارة والإيداع والرهن والإقراض والشركة والمضاربة والتوكيل بالقبض، وأما الوكيل بالاستقراض، فإن أضافه إلى الموكل كان للموكل، وإلا كان للوكيل، وقوله: "وعن إنكار" قيد به؛ لأنه إذا كان الصلح عن إقرار يكون كالبيع، فترجع الحقوق إلى الوكيل كما في البيع، وإذا كان عن إنكار فهو فداء يمين في حق المدعى عليه، فالوكيل سفير محض، فلا ترجع إليه الحقوق. (فتح، عناية)
كالنكاح والخلع والصلح عن دم عمد، وعن إنكار يتعلق بالموكل، فلا يطالب أي والصلح عن إنكار وكيله بالمهر ووكيلها بتسليمها وللمشتري منع الموكل عن الثمن، وإن دفع إليه صح، ولا يطالبه الوكيل ثانياً .
أي وكيل الزوج 6 أي دفعه إلى الموكل المشتري : المشتري الثمن يتعلق بالموكل: [لأن الوكيل في هذه الأشياء سفير محض فلا يستغني عن إضافته إلى الموكل. (عيني)] فوكيلها بالنكاح لا يلي قبض مهرها وبالخلع لا يلي قبض البدل، ويصح ضمانه ،مهرها، وتخير بين مطالبته أو الزوج، وإنما تعلقت الحقوق في هذه العقود بالموكل؛ لأن الحكم فيها لا يقبل الفصل عن السبب؛ لأنه إسقاط، فيتلاشى، فلا يتصور صدوره من شخص، ويتوقف حكمه لغيره، فكان سفيراً محضاً؛ ليقترن الحكم بالسبب، بخلاف البيع، فإن حكمه يقبل الفصل كما في البيع بخيار. (عيني، فتح) بالمهر: أي مهر المرأة التي تزوجت بالموكل. (عيني) بتسليمها : أي بتسليم المرأة التي زوجها من الموكل. (عيني) وللمشتري منع الموكل أي إذا باع الوكيل بالبيع، ثم طلب الموكل ثمن المبيع من المشتري، يجوز للمشتري أن يمنع الثمن إياه، ولا يدفعه إليه ؛ لأن الموكل أجنبي عن العقد وحقوقه؛ لأنها تتعلق بالعاقد كما مر ، ولهذا لو حلف المشتري من الوكيل أنه لا شيء للموكل عليه كان باراً، ولو حلف أنه لا شيء عليه للوكيل كان حانثاً. (عيني، فتح) ولا يطالبه الوكيل ثانياً: أي لا يطالب الوكيل المشتري ثانياً؛ لأنه برئ ذمة المشتري بدفع الثمن إلى المستحق وهو الموكل، ولهذا لو كان للمشتري دين على الموكل تقع المقاصة بمجرد العقد، ولو كان له دين عليهما تقع المقاصة بدين الموكل دون دين ،الوكيل ولو كان له دين على الوكيل فقط وقعت المقاصة به، ويضمن الوكيل للموكل؛ لأنه قضى دينه بمال الموكل خلافاً لأبي يوسف بخلاف ما إذا باع مال اليتيم، ودفع المشتري الثمن إلى اليتيم، حيث لا تبرأ ذمته، بل يجب عليه الدفع للوصي ثانياً؛ لأن اليتيم ليس له قبض ماله فكان الدفع إليه تضييعاً، وبخلاف الوكيل في الصرف إذا صارف وقبض الموكل بدل الصرف، حيث يبطل الصرف، ولا يعتد بقبضه؛ لأن جواز الصرف تعلق بالقبض قبل الافتراق فكان القبض فيه بمنزلة الإيجاب والقبول وهما يتعلقان بالمتعاقدين والموكل ليس بعاقد رمز الحقائق: ١٦٩/٢]
Book of Agency. Rulings of Agency. Its taking possession and taking possession of the price and return upon revocation and litigation regarding defect, and property is established for the principal from the beginning, so that the relative of the agent is not freed by his purchase, and in what he attributes to the principal. Not for the agent, like his father or his son (the agent). His taking possession: i.e., taking possession of the sold item if the agent is a buyer. (‘Ayni) At the time of revocation (i.e., recourse against the seller if the agent is a buyer). (‘Ayni) It includes two matters: First: when the agent is a seller and takes possession of the price, then the sold item is revoked, the buyer has recourse for the price against the agent, whether the price remains in his hand or he delivered it to the principal, and he has recourse against his principal. Second: when he is a buyer and the sold item is revoked from his hand, then he has recourse for the price against the seller, but not all? (Maw, ‘Ayni, Fath) And litigation: i.e., the agent’s litigation with the seller regarding purchase. (‘Ayni) It is established for the principal – a response to what al-Shafi‘i said: Verily rights follow the ruling of the act, and the ruling pertains to the principal, so likewise its subsidiaries. The summary of the response: that property belongs to the principal, but the agent contracts by way of substitution (khilafah) on his behalf, and the meaning of substitution is that property is established for the principal from the beginning, while the cause is concluded making its ruling applicable to the agent, so he stands in his place in the establishment of property by the prior authorization. The verification of the issue: that the agent’s act has two aspects: the aspect of its occurrence by his own statement, and the aspect of its occurrence by way of representation of the principal. Acting upon both, even in one way, is better than neglecting either. So if we establish property and rights for the agent, as required by analogy (qiyas), because they occur by his statement and his qualification, the authorization would become void. And if we establish them for the principal, his statement becomes void. So we establish property for the principal, because that is the purpose of the authorization. The author alluded to this by his saying: “from the beginning.” So rights are determined for the agent? (wa qad?) And it is permissible that a ruling be established for someone other than the one for whom the cause is concluded, like a slave who accepts a gift, charity, and hunts – indeed his master takes his place in property by that cause. And it is a response to an implied question: If rights in this action revert to the agent, then his relative should be freed if he purchases him by agency, because the purchase of a relative is manumission. He answered with his saying: “and property is established for the principal from the beginning,” i.e., at the beginning of the affair by substitution on his behalf, meaning the agent is the origin regarding the contract, but regarding the ruling, the principal substitutes him, so it occurs for him without him being the origin in it, like the slave who receives a gift or hunts – just as the master establishes property for himself from the beginning by substitution on his behalf, so too the principal establishes property in what his agent purchased by substitution on his behalf. (‘Ayni and ‘Inayah) And likewise his marriage is not invalidated if he purchases his wife by agency. (‘Ayni) And in what he attributes to the principal (i.e., rights in the contract which he attributes to him) (Ta‘ ‘Ayni) meaning he is not independent of attributing it to his principal, so that if he attributes it to himself, the contract becomes his and he becomes like a messenger. And his saying: “like marriage,” and likewise manumission for compensation, kitabah, gift, charity, lending, deposit, pledge, loan, partnership, mudarabah, and authorization for taking possession. As for the agent for borrowing, if he attributes it to the principal, it is for the principal, otherwise it is for the agent. And his saying: “and regarding denial” is a restriction; because if the compromise is regarding admission, it is like a sale, so rights revert to the agent as in a sale; and if it is regarding denial, it is a ransom for the oath concerning the defendant, so the agent is a pure intermediary, so rights do not revert to him. (Fath, ‘Inayah) Like marriage, khula‘, and compromise regarding intentional killing and regarding denial – it pertains to the principal, so the agent (i.e., the agent of the husband) is not asked for the dower, nor is her agent asked for handing her over (i.e., handing over the woman whom he married to the principal). And the buyer may withhold the price from the principal, and if he pays it to him, it is valid, and the agent does not demand it from him a second time. (i.e., the agent of the husband) (i.e., his payment to the principal buyer) The buyer (the price) pertains to the principal: [because the agent in these things is a pure intermediary, not independent of attributing to the principal. (‘Ayni)] So her agent in marriage is not entitled to receive her dower, and in khula‘ he is not entitled to receive the compensation. And his guarantee of her dower is valid, and he may choose between demanding it from him or the husband. And rights are only attached to the principal in these contracts because the ruling in them is not separable from the cause, because it is a cancellation, so it annihilates, thus it cannot be imagined to originate from one person while its ruling depends on another; so he is a pure intermediary so that the ruling is connected to the cause, unlike sale, for its ruling is separable, as in a sale with an option. (‘Ayni, Fath) Meaning the dower of the woman who married the principal. (‘Ayni) Meaning handing over the woman whom he married from the principal. (‘Ayni) And the buyer may withhold the principal – i.e., if the agent sold, then the principal demands the price of the sold item from the buyer, the buyer may withhold the price from him and not pay it to him; because the principal is a stranger to the contract and its rights, for they pertain to the contractor as previously. Hence, if the buyer swears by the agent that there is nothing for the principal against him, he is truthful; and if he swears that there is nothing for the agent against him, he is false. (‘Ayni, Fath) And the agent does not demand it a second time: i.e., the agent does not demand the buyer a second time, because the buyer’s liability is discharged by paying the price to the rightful owner, i.e., the principal. Hence, if the buyer had a debt against the principal, set-off occurs immediately by the mere contract; and if he had a debt against both, set-off occurs with the principal’s debt but not the agent’s debt; and if he had a debt against the agent only, set-off occurs with it, and the agent becomes liable to the principal because he satisfied his debt with the principal’s property, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Unlike the case where he sells the property of an orphan and the buyer pays the price to the orphan, where his liability is not discharged, rather he must pay it to the guardian a second time because the orphan is not entitled to take possession of his property, so paying him is wasteful. And unlike the agent in currency exchange (sarf) if he exchanges and the principal takes possession of the exchange, where the exchange is invalidated and his taking possession is not considered because the permissibility of exchange is tied to taking possession before separation, so taking possession in it is like the offer and acceptance, and they pertain to the two contractors, and the principal is not a contractor. (Ramz al-Haqa’iq: 2/169)
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa’iq discusses the general principle that in agency contracts, ownership and rights vest in the principal (muwakkil) from the inception of the contract, not in the agent (wakil). The author first states the ruling: property vests in the principal from the beginning. This has practical consequences: if the agent purchases a close relative, that relative is not freed (as would happen if the agent owned the property), because the property belongs to the principal, not the agent. The agent must also attribute the contract to the principal. The passage then provides a response to Imam al-Shafi‘i, who argued that since rights follow the ruling of the act (hukm al-tasarruf), and the ruling pertains to the principal, rights should also vest in the principal. The Hanafi response, as explained in the text, is that ownership is established for the principal, but the agent acts as a substitute (khalifah). The contract is concluded by the agent’s statement, but the legal effect (ownership) is attributed to the principal. This is justified by considering the two aspects of the agent’s action: his own statement and his representation. To give full effect to both, we attribute ownership to the principal (the purpose of the agency) but also give the agent rights related to the contract (like litigation and taking possession) because they arise from his statement. The text then distinguishes between types of agency. In some contracts, like marriage, khula‘, and compromise regarding intentional killing or denial, the agent is a mere intermediary (safir mahd) because the ruling (e.g., dissolution of marriage, cancellation of rights) cannot be separated from the cause. In these cases, all rights pertain exclusively to the principal, and the agent cannot handle the receipt of dower or compensation. In contrast, in sales, rights are separable, so the agent can handle receipt of the price and litigation. Finally, the text discusses the buyer’s right to withhold payment from the principal and the agent’s discharge upon payment to the principal. It provides examples of set-off and distinguishes the case of an orphan (where payment to the orphan is null) and currency exchange (where the principal cannot receive the exchange). The passage ends by noting that Imam Abu Yusuf differs on the liability of the agent when he pays the principal’s debt from the principal’s property.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wakalah): A contract whereby one person (the principal) appoints another (the agent) to perform a legal act on his behalf.
الموكل (al-muwakkil): The principal who appoints the agent.
الوكيل (al-wakil): The agent who acts on behalf of the principal.
الخلافة (al-khilafah): Substitution; the agent stands in the place of the principal for the purpose of the contract.
سفير محض (safir mahd): A pure intermediary; one who merely conveys the contract without any personal rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does the agent retain litigation rights even though the principal owns the property?
A) Because the agent is the one who physically acts.
B) Because the agent's statement creates a capacity for him as the contractor.
C) Because the principal is absent.
D) Because the principal waives his rights.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school splits rights in agency: ownership to the principal, procedural rights to the agent.

Agency Admission and Sale by Ta'ati
Ruling with commentary on agency admission and delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a buyer admits being an agent but later denies authorization, and explain how delivery by ta'ati creates a binding sale.

Core Idea
A buyer's admission of agency cannot be undone by later denial unless the principal repudiates it.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents a nuanced scenario where a buyer says 'sell me this slave for so-and-so.' The ruling on ownership hinges on the buyer's admission of agency and the principal's response. Additionally, delivery of the slave to the principal forms a valid sale by mutual delivery (ta'ati), obligating the principal to pay the price. The commentary by 'Ayni clarifies the distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial act and parties
Consider the scenario: A says to B 'Sell me this slave for C, I am buying it as an agent for C.' B sells the slave to A. Recognize that A attributes the sale to himself but declares it is for C. This is not a fuduli (stranger's) purchase because A says 'sell me' rather than 'sell to C'.
The wording 'sell me' is key to distinguishing from fuduli.
2
Determine the effect of A's admission
A's statement 'for C' is an admission that he is acting as C's agent. This admission is binding and not invalidated by later denial. Therefore, if C claims he authorized A, C takes the slave.
Iqrar (admission) is a strong proof; subsequent denial does not nullify it.
3
Analyze the exceptions
There are two exceptions: (1) If C says 'I did not order him,' A's admission is repudiated and the slave remains with A. (2) If A delivers the slave to C and C takes delivery, the sale becomes binding on C by ta'ati, and C must pay the price.
The first exception shows that the principal's denial overrides the admission. The second exception shows that delivery with intent to transfer ownership creates a valid sale.
4
Understand the basis of ta'ati
The validity of sale by ta'ati is based on custom (urf) and mutual consent. Even without verbal offer and acceptance, the mutual exchange of goods and price suffices because custom recognizes this as a valid transaction.
This is a Hanafi position; other schools may require explicit verbal expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخذه فلان إلا أن يقول لم آمره به إلا أن يسلمه المشتري إليه، أي إذا قال رجل لآخر : بعني هذا العبد لأجل فلان، فإني أشتريه له وكالة عنه، فباعه البائع العبد منه، ثم أنكر المشتري أمر فلان، وقال ما كان أمرني بذلك وقال فلان أنا أمرته أخذ العبد فلان؛ لإقرار المشتري بالوكالة عنه، والإقرار بالشيء لا يبطل بالإنكار اللاحق، فيكون العبد للموكل أي لفلان وقوله : "إلا أن يقول لم آمره به" أي فحينئذ ليس لفلان أن يأخذ العبد؛ لأن إقرار المشتري ارتد برد فلان والإقرار مما يرتد بالرد، فينفذ على المشتري؛ لأن الشراء إذا وجد نفاذاً لا يتوقف، وليس هذا من شراء الفضولي؛ لأنه أضاف البيع إلى نفسه بقوله: "بعني"، وصورة شراء الفضولي: أن يقول: بع عبدك من فلان. وقوله: "إلا أن يسلمه المشتري القائل: بعني هذا لفلان، فإن سلمه إليه، وأخذه الذي اشترى له، صار بيعاً لفلان، فيلزم عليه تسليم الثمن؛ لأنه صار مشترياً بالتعاطي، كالفضولي إذا اشترى الشخص ثم سلمه ودلت المسألة على أن التسليم على وجه البيع يكفي؛ لانعقاد البيع بالتعاطي، وإن لم يوجد نقد الثمن، للعرف ولوجود التراضي، وهو المعتبر في باب المعاوضات المالية. (عيني، فتح) آمره به أي بالشراء، فحينئذ لا يأخذه بل يكون للمشتري؛ لأن إقراره ارتد برد فلان، والإقرار مما يرتد بالرد فينفذ على المشتري (عيني) المشتري إليه أي إلى فلان فيكون لفلان التسليم، وهذا استثناء الاستثناء الأول، من وله حكم النفي، والاستثناء من النفي إثبات. (عيني)
He (the principal) takes it unless he says 'I did not order him to do it' unless the buyer delivers it to him. That is, if a man says to another: 'Sell me this slave for the sake of so-and-so, for I am buying it for him as an agent,' and the seller sells the slave to him, then the buyer denies the order of so-and-so and says 'He did not order me,' but so-and-so says 'I ordered him,' he (the principal) takes the slave because the buyer's admission of agency for him is established, and an admission is not invalidated by subsequent denial. So the slave is for the principal. His statement 'unless he says I did not order him' means that then it is not for so-and-so to take the slave because the buyer's admission is repudiated by the denial of so-and-so, and an admission can be repudiated by repudiation, so it is effective against the buyer because the purchase, once valid, does not stop. This is not a case of a stranger's purchase because he attributed the sale to himself by saying 'sell me.' The case of a stranger's purchase is when he says 'sell your slave to so-and-so.' His statement 'unless the buyer delivers it' to so-and-so, and he takes it, it becomes a sale for so-and-so, and he is obligated to pay the price because he becomes a buyer by mutual delivery (ta'ati), like a stranger who purchases a person then delivers. The matter indicates that delivery on the basis of sale is sufficient for the contract of sale by ta'ati, even if the price is not paid, due to custom and the existence of mutual consent, which is considered in financial transactions. (Ayni, Fath) 'I did not order him' means the purchase, then he does not take it but it belongs to the buyer because his admission is repudiated by the repudiation of so-and-so, and an admission can be repudiated by repudiation, so it is effective against the buyer. (Ayni) 'the buyer delivers it to him' means to so-and-so, so the delivery is for so-and-so. This is an exception to the first exception, which has the ruling of negation, and an exception from negation is affirmation. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a specific scenario in agency for sale. The matn states: 'He (the principal) takes it unless he says "I did not order him" unless the buyer delivers it to him.' The commentary by 'Ayni and Fath al-Qadir explains the details. The scenario: A person says to another, 'Sell me this slave for so-and-so, I am buying it as his agent.' The seller sells. Then the buyer denies that so-and-so authorized him, but so-and-so claims he did authorize. The ruling: the slave goes to the principal (so-and-so) because the buyer's statement 'sell me for so-and-so' is an admission that he is acting as agent, and an admission cannot be undone by later denial. However, if the buyer says 'I did not order him' (i.e., the principal did not order the purchase), then the slave remains with the buyer because the principal's denial repudiates the admission. Additionally, if the buyer delivers the slave to the principal and the principal takes it, then the sale becomes binding on the principal, and he must pay the price. This is a valid sale by mutual delivery (ta'ati), which is recognized by custom and mutual consent. The commentary distinguishes this from a stranger's purchase (shira' al-fuduli), where the agent does not attribute the sale to himself. The passage also discusses that admission can be repudiated by repudiation. The legal reasoning relies on custom and the principle that mutual consent in financial transactions is valid.

Key Terms
التعاطي (al-ta'ati): A sale contract concluded by mutual delivery and acceptance without verbal offer and acceptance.
الفضولي (al-fuduli): A stranger who makes a contract without being authorized by the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where someone says 'Sell me this slave for so-and-so,' and the buyer later denies being authorized by so-and-so, what is the ruling?
A) The slave belongs to the buyer because he denied agency.
B) The slave belongs to so-and-so because the buyer's admission of agency is not invalidated by subsequent denial.
C) The slave belongs to the seller because the sale is invalid.
D) The slave belongs to neither; it must be returned.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a buyer's admission of agency in a sale is binding and cannot be undone by later denial; however, the principal's repudiation can void the admission, and delivery of the goods can create a binding sale by mutual delivery (ta'ati).
