Agency of a Slave to Purchase Himself: Two Outcomes
Ruling on a slave appointing an agent to buy himself: two scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two possible outcomes when a slave's agent buys him from his master and explain the legal basis for each.

Core Idea
A slave's self-purchase is emancipation; the agent's words decide if it's emancipation or a real sale.

This lesson examines a special case of agency where a slave appoints an agent to buy him from his master. The ruling hinges on the precise words used by the agent. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the Hanafi principles of agency and emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal nature
Recognize that a slave cannot literally buy himself; the transaction is legally an emancipation for compensation.
The agent is merely a messenger (safir) in the emancipation scenario.
2
Analyze scenario 1: Agent says 'for himself'
If the agent says 'I bought him for himself', the slave is freed, wala goes to the master, and the price paid belongs to the master.
This is considered acceptance of emancipation, not a sale.
3
Analyze scenario 2: Agent says only 'I bought him'
If the agent does not specify 'for himself', the agent becomes the owner of the slave, and the price goes to the master.
The literal meaning of sale is applied.
4
Understand the agent's role
The agent's capacity: in scenario 1 he is a messenger; in scenario 2 he is a buyer bound by the contract.
If the agent buys for himself, it is a departure from the mandate but valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبشراء نفس الأمر من سيّده بألف ودفع فقال لسيّده اشتريته لنفسه، فباعه على أي العبد هذا، عتق، وولاؤه لسيّده، وإن قال اشتريته فالعبد للمشتري، والألف لسيده، أي المأمور ولم يزد قوله لنفسه هو العبد لأنه كسب عبده منهم من مال أبو منصور الماتريدي إلى الأول، وقال في "النهاية" : وهو الأظهر، وقال في "الكافي": وهو الصحيح.[رمز الحقائق: ١٧٥/٢] ولو تحالفا يلزم المبيع المأمور، ولا يعتبر تصديق البائع في حق الآمر؛ لأنه إن استوفى الثمن، فهو أجنبي عنهما، وإن لم يستوف فهو أجنبي عن الآمر ، فلا مدخل له بينهما، وحاصل اختلاف الفقهاء: أن نظر إلى ظاهره فنفى التحالف، ومنهم من قال مراده التحالف بدليل ما ذكره في موضع آخر، وإنما نص محمد في "الجامع الصغير": أن القول للمأمور مع اليمين؛ لأنه هو المدعي، ولا يمين على المدعي إلا في التحالف، فكان المقصود، رد، والموكل منكر، واليمين عليه ظاهراً، فلم يحتج إلى بيانها.(فتح) وبشراء أي لو أمره عبد أن يشتري نفسه من مولاه (معدن فباعه أي على أن يشتريه لنفس العبد.(معدن) عتق وولاؤه لسيده الأن بيع نفس العبد من نفسه إعتاق على مال، وشراء العبد نفسه قبول الإعتاق ببدل، وكان الوكيل سفيراً.(ط، عيني) لأن اعتباره بيعاً حقيقة غير ممكن، فإذا اشتراه الوكيل، صار البائع معتقاً، فيلزمه الولاء، والوكيل بالقبول سفير محض ومعبر عنه، فلا ترجع الحقوق إليه.[رمز الحقائق: ١٧٥/٢] وإن قال اشتريته أي لم يزد على ذلك لنفسه"، فالعبد للمشتري؛ لأن اللفظ حقيقة للمعاوضة، وأمكن العمل بها إذا لم يبين فيحافظ عليها بخلاف شراء العبد نفسه؛ لأن المجاز فيه متعين وإن كان معاوضة، يثبت الملك له، والألف للمولى؛ لأنه كسب عبده ولقائل أن يقول: قد ذكر فيما تقدم أن الوكيل بشراء شيء بعينه لا يملك شرائه لنفسه، فلا يجوز أن يكون العبد للمشتري، ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأن توكيل العبد بشراء نفسه يكون توكيلا بقبول الإعتاق حقيقة، فشراء الوكيل لنفسه يكون إتيانا بجنس تصرف آخر، والوكيل إذا خالف وأتى بجنس آخر من التصرفات نفذ عليه.(عيني، فتح) فالعبد للمشتري أي يصير ملكا له وهو الوكيل والألف لسيده الذي أخذه من عند العبد ودفعه إلى المولى.(عيني)) راجع للمسألتين، وكان ينبغي أن يقول بعده وعلى العبد ألف أخرى بعد الإعتاق في الأولى، وعلى المشتري في الثانية ألف ثمن العبد؛ البطلان الأداء فيهما؛ لاستحقاق المولى ما أدّاه بجهة أخرى، كسب عبده، فكان مملوكاً له قبل الشراء وقبل العتق. وقوله: "وعلى المشتري ألف مثله" ظاهر فيما إذا وقع الشراء للمشتري، كما في الصورة الثانية، أما إذا وقع الشراء للعبد نفسه حتى عتق، هل يجب على العبد ألف أخرى؟ قال قاضي خان ينبغي أن يجب؛ لأن الألف الأولى مال المولى، فلا يصلح بدلاً عن ملكه، ولزوم الألف على الوكيل في الثانية؛ لأنه عاقد مالك للعبد، وفي المسألة الأولى المال على العبد دين دون الوكيل، وذكر محمد في وكالة الجامع الكبير": أن العبد يعتق، والمال على الوكيل، والصحيح الأول، فالمطالبة بالألف الأخرى على العبد بعد العتق لا على الوكيل.(عيني، فتح) • وهو أنه
And [the case of] buying of the person himself from his master for one thousand, and he gave [the money], so he said to his master: I bought him for himself, so he sold him based on that, i.e., the slave, he is emancipated and his wala is for his master. And if he said: I bought him, then the slave goes to the buyer, and the thousand goes to his master. I.e., the agent did not add the phrase 'for himself'. The slave is his master's earning. Among them, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi inclined to the first opinion, and in al-Nihayah it is said it is the more apparent, and in al-Kafi it is said it is correct. (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 175/2) And if they take oaths, the sold item is binding on the agent, and the confirmation of the seller is not considered regarding the principal; because if he has received the price, he is a stranger to both, and if he has not received, he is a stranger to the principal, so he has no role between them. The summary of the disagreement among jurists: some looked at the apparent meaning and negated mutual oath, and some said his intention is mutual oath based on what he mentioned elsewhere. Indeed, Muhammad al-Shaybani stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'the statement is of the agent with his oath'; because he is the claimant, and there is no oath upon the claimant except in mutual oath, so that was intended. It is rejected. The principal is the denier, and the oath is upon him apparently, so there was no need to explain it. (Fath) 'And buying' i.e., if a slave ordered him to buy himself from his master (Ma'din). 'So he sold him' i.e., with the intention that he buy for the slave himself. (Ma'din). He is freed and his wala is for his master; because the sale of the slave himself is a emancipation for compensation, and the slave's purchase of himself is acceptance of emancipation with a substitute, and the agent is a mere messenger. (T, Ayni) Because considering it a real sale is not possible. So when the agent buys him, the seller becomes an emancipator, so wala is required for him, and the agent in accepting is a pure messenger and spokesperson, so rights do not revert to him. (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 175/2) 'And if he said: I bought it' i.e., he did not add 'for himself', then the slave goes to the buyer; because the word is literally for exchange, and it is possible to act upon it when he does not specify, so it is preserved, contrary to the slave buying himself; because the metaphorical meaning is necessary there. And even if it is an exchange, ownership is established for him, and the thousand goes to the master because it is his slave's earning. And it may be said: it has been mentioned earlier that the agent for buying a specific thing cannot buy it for himself, so it is not permissible for the slave to be for the buyer. And it can be answered that the slave's appointment of an agent to buy himself is actually an appointment to accept emancipation, so the agent buying for himself is an act of a different type, and if the agent violates and does a different type of act, it is binding upon him. (Ayni, Fath) 'So the slave goes to the buyer' i.e., he becomes the property of the agent, and the thousand goes to his master who took it from the slave and gave it to the master. (Ayni)) This refers to the two issues. It would be proper for him to say afterwards: and on the slave another thousand after emancipation in the first, and on the buyer in the second a thousand as the price of the slave; due to the voidness of the payment in both; because the master is entitled to what was paid by another means, it being his slave's earning, so it was his property before the purchase and before emancipation. And his saying: 'and on the buyer a thousand like it' is apparent when the purchase is for the buyer, as in the second scenario. As for when the purchase is for the slave himself such that he is emancipated, is another thousand required from the slave? Qadhi Khan said it should be required; because the first thousand is the master's property, so it is not suitable as a substitute for his ownership, and the thousand is binding on the agent in the second because he is the contracting party owning the slave. In the first issue, the money is a debt on the slave, not on the agent. And Muhammad stated in Wakalah al-Jami' al-Kabir: that the slave is emancipated and the money is on the agent, but the first is correct, so the claim for the other thousand is against the slave after emancipation, not against the agent. (Ayni, Fath) • And it is that.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq deals with a special case of agency: a slave appoints an agent to buy him from his master. The passage is of type 'ruling statement' with commentary. The ruling distinguishes two scenarios based on the agent's words. If the agent says 'I bought him for himself' (i.e., for the slave), then the slave is emancipated and his wala (clientage) goes to his master. This is because the transaction is legally considered an emancipation for compensation (i'taq 'ala mal), and the agent is merely a messenger (safir). The purchase of oneself by a slave is not a real sale but acceptance of emancipation. If the agent simply says 'I bought him' without specifying 'for himself', then the slave becomes the property of the agent (the person who bought him), and the thousand dirhams paid go to the master. The agent 's word 'I bought' is taken literally as a real sale, so the agent becomes the owner. The commentary also discusses a further nuance: whether an additional thousand is owed. In the first scenario (slave freed), the slave owes another thousand because the first thousand was the master's property (being the slave's earning) and cannot serve as the substitute for emancipation; thus the slave must pay another thousand after freedom. In the second scenario (agent buys for himself), the agent owes the thousand. This is the relied-upon position. The passage also includes a discussion about mutual oath (tahalf) in the context of the earlier case, but it is not directly relevant here. The key point is that the slave cannot literally buy himself; the agent's words determine whether it is emancipation or a real sale.

Key Terms
ولاء (walā'): The clientage relationship that arises between an emancipator and a freed slave; the wala goes to the master (emancipator).
عتق (itq): Emancipation; here, the sale of the slave to himself is legally an emancipation for compensation.
كسب (kasb): Earning; the money given by the slave is considered the master's property as it is the slave's earning.
سفير (safīr): Messenger; the agent in this case is merely a conveyor of the offer and acceptance, not a contracting party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave's agent says 'I bought him' (without 'for himself'), what is the legal outcome?
A) The slave is emancipated with compensation.
B) The agent becomes the owner of the slave.
C) The transaction is void.
D) The master keeps the slave and returns the money.

Key Takeaway
The agent's precise wording determines whether the transaction is an emancipation (if 'for himself' is said) or a real sale (if only 'I bought him').

The Additional Payment Dispute: Does the Slave Owe Another Thousand?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on additional payment in slave's self-purchase agency case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether the slave owes another thousand when he is freed, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The slave's payment cannot redeem himself because the money belongs to the master; an extra thousand is required.

After establishing the basic ruling, Hanafi scholars debated a further issue: in the first scenario where the slave is freed, does he owe an additional thousand dirhams? The dispute arises because the initial payment, being the slave's earning, already belongs to the master and cannot serve as the substitute for emancipation. This lesson unpacks the arguments and the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that in the first scenario (emancipation), the thousand paid by the slave is master's property, so it cannot be the substitute for emancipation.
The slave's earning belongs to the master at all times.
2
Understand the opposing views
Qadhi Khan holds that the slave must pay another thousand after freedom. Muhammad al-Shaybani (in al-Jami' al-Kabir) says the money is on the agent. The first (debt on slave) is correct.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi.
3
Analyze the second scenario
In the second scenario (agent buys for himself), the agent owes the thousand because he is the contracting party.
No dispute here.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that the slave owes another thousand after emancipation, not the agent.
This is supported by Qadhi Khan and others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبشراء نفس الأمر من سيّده بألف ودفع فقال لسيّده اشتريته لنفسه، فباعه على أي العبد هذا، عتق، وولاؤه لسيّده، وإن قال اشتريته فالعبد للمشتري، والألف لسيده، أي المأمور ولم يزد قوله لنفسه هو العبد لأنه كسب عبده منهم من مال أبو منصور الماتريدي إلى الأول، وقال في "النهاية" : وهو الأظهر، وقال في "الكافي": وهو الصحيح.[رمز الحقائق: ١٧٥/٢] ولو تحالفا يلزم المبيع المأمور، ولا يعتبر تصديق البائع في حق الآمر؛ لأنه إن استوفى الثمن، فهو أجنبي عنهما، وإن لم يستوف فهو أجنبي عن الآمر ، فلا مدخل له بينهما، وحاصل اختلاف الفقهاء: أن نظر إلى ظاهره فنفى التحالف، ومنهم من قال مراده التحالف بدليل ما ذكره في موضع آخر، وإنما نص محمد في "الجامع الصغير": أن القول للمأمور مع اليمين؛ لأنه هو المدعي، ولا يمين على المدعي إلا في التحالف، فكان المقصود، رد، والموكل منكر، واليمين عليه ظاهراً، فلم يحتج إلى بيانها.(فتح) وبشراء أي لو أمره عبد أن يشتري نفسه من مولاه (معدن فباعه أي على أن يشتريه لنفس العبد.(معدن) عتق وولاؤه لسيده الأن بيع نفس العبد من نفسه إعتاق على مال، وشراء العبد نفسه قبول الإعتاق ببدل، وكان الوكيل سفيراً.(ط، عيني) لأن اعتباره بيعاً حقيقة غير ممكن، فإذا اشتراه الوكيل، صار البائع معتقاً، فيلزمه الولاء، والوكيل بالقبول سفير محض ومعبر عنه، فلا ترجع الحقوق إليه.[رمز الحقائق: ١٧٥/٢] وإن قال اشتريته أي لم يزد على ذلك لنفسه"، فالعبد للمشتري؛ لأن اللفظ حقيقة للمعاوضة، وأمكن العمل بها إذا لم يبين فيحافظ عليها بخلاف شراء العبد نفسه؛ لأن المجاز فيه متعين وإن كان معاوضة، يثبت الملك له، والألف للمولى؛ لأنه كسب عبده ولقائل أن يقول: قد ذكر فيما تقدم أن الوكيل بشراء شيء بعينه لا يملك شرائه لنفسه، فلا يجوز أن يكون العبد للمشتري، ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأن توكيل العبد بشراء نفسه يكون توكيلا بقبول الإعتاق Agent Contracting with Close Relatives: The Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on agent's contracts with close relatives and others whose testimony is rejected

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on an agent's contract with close relatives and apply the Sahibayn's exceptions to determine validity.

Core Idea
An agent's contract with close relatives is suspect; the Sahibayn allow it at market price unless it is effectively a self-contract.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq examines a key dispute within the Hanafi school: can an agent sell to or buy from his own parents, children, or spouse at the market price? The answer depends on whether the contract triggers suspicion of favoritism. Imam Abu Hanifa has two narrations, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, take a more permissive stance with specific exceptions. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the principle of eliminating suspicion (tuhma) in agency contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize that the agent is appointed to sell or buy, and the counterparty is a person whose testimony is rejected for the agent (e.g., father, son, spouse, slave).
Rejection of testimony indicates a close relationship that raises suspicion.
2
State the general rule and Abu Hanifa's two narrations
The text begins by stating that the agent does not contract with such persons. It then clarifies that this is not a single ruling: there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa. One prohibits at market price, the other allows it.
The mudarib is contrasted because he shares profit, reducing suspicion.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's position and exceptions
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) allow contracting at market price except with the agent's own slave, mukatab, minor son, and mufawada partner. They argue that properties are distinct, so no suspicion.
The exceptions involve cases where the agent effectively contracts with himself.
4
Apply the reasoning to similar contracts
The same dispute applies to Ijarah (hire), Sarf (exchange), Salam (advance sale), etc., because benefits are connected, creating suspicion.
Distinctness of properties does not prevent connection in another respect.
5
Determine the practical consequence
If an agent sells to his father at market price, according to Abu Hanifa's one narration the contract is invalid; according to the Sahibayn it is valid unless the father is his slave etc. Most later Hanafi practice follows the Sahibayn.
Thus, most such contracts are valid at market price, except the specific exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوكيل بالبيع والشراء لا يعقد مع من ترد شهادته له، أي للوكيل. لا يعقد: ظاهره أن المنع من البيع أو الشراء مع من ذكر لا يجوز عند الإمام بمثل القيمة رواية واحدة، وليس كذلك؛ لأن فيه روايتين عن الإمام، بخلاف المضارب إذا عقد مع من لا تقبل شهادته له بمثل القيمة، حيث يجوز عند الإمام رواية واحدة. وجه الفرق بين الوكيل والمضارب على إحدى الروايتين أن المضارب كالمتصرف لنفسه من وجه؛ لأنه شريك في الربح، فلا تلحقه التهمة في البيع مثل القيمة وقالوا هذا إذا لم يطلق له الموكل، وأما إذا أطلق بأن قال: بع ممن شئت، فحينئذ يجوز بيعه لهم بمثل القيمة. وكذا لا يجوز للوكيل البيع والشراء من نفسه عندهم جميعاً، كان خيراً أو شراً، للموكل أو الوكيل؛ لأنه إذا لم يملك العقد مع من ترد شهادته له، فأولى أن لا يملكه من نفسه، ومن ترد شهادته للوكيل هم الذين بهم قرابة الولاد، وأحد الزوجين للآخر، وعبيده، فالعقد مع لا يجوز عند أبي حنيفة إلا إذا زاد على ثمن المثل في البيع، ونقص عن ثمن المثل في الشراء. وقالا يجوز بيعه منهم بمثل القيمة إلا من عبده ومكاتبه وابنه الصغير ومفاوضة؛ لأن التوكيل مطلق، ولا تهمة؛ إذ الأملاك بينهم متباينة، والمنافع منقطعة، بخلاف العبد؛ لأنه بيع من نفسه؛ لأن ما في يد العبد للمولى، وكذا للمولى حق في كسب المكاتب، وينقلب حقيقة بالعجز، فصار عاقداً مع نفسه، وذلك لا يجوز، وبه قال أحمد في رواية والشافعي في وجه وله أن مواضع التهمة مستثناة عن الوكالات وهذا موضع التهمة بدليل عدم قبول الشهادة، ولأن المنافع بينهم متصلة، فكان بيعاً من نفسه من وجه، وعلى هذا الخلاف الإجارة والصرف والسلم ونحوها؛ لأن هذه مواضع التهمة؛ إذ المنافع بينهم متصلة، ولهذا لا يقبل شهادتهم، وتباين الأملاك لا يمنع الاتصال من وجه آخر.
The agent for sale and purchase does not contract with one whose testimony is rejected for him, i.e., for the agent. 'Does not contract': its apparent meaning is that the prohibition of selling or buying with those mentioned is not permissible according to the Imam at the equivalent of the value in one narration, but it is not so; because there are two narrations from the Imam, unlike the mudarib if he contracts with one whose testimony is not accepted for him at the equivalent of the value, where it is permissible according to the Imam in one narration. The difference between the agent and the mudarib according to one of the two narrations is that the mudarib is like one acting for himself in some respect; because he is a partner in profit, so suspicion does not attach to him in selling at the equivalent of the value. And they said: this is if the principal did not give him absolute authorization. But if he gave absolute authorization by saying: 'Sell to whomever you wish,' then his selling to them at the equivalent of the value is permissible. Likewise, it is not permissible for the agent to sell to or buy from himself according to all of them, whether it is good or bad, for the principal or the agent; because if he does not have the authority to contract with one whose testimony is rejected for him, then it is more fitting that he does not have authority over himself. And those whose testimony is rejected for the agent are those related by birth, one spouse for the other, and his slaves. So the contract with them is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa unless he increases above the market value in sale, and decreases below the market value in purchase. And the two said: his selling to them at the equivalent of the value is permissible, except from his slave, his mukatab, his minor son, and his partner in mutual surety; because the authorization is absolute, and there is no suspicion; since properties are distinct among them, and benefits are disconnected, unlike the slave; because it is a sale from himself; since what is in the slave's hand belongs to the master, and likewise the master has a right in the earnings of the mukatab, and he returns to being a slave by disability, so he becomes a contractor with himself, and that is not permissible. And Ahmad said in one narration and al-Shafi'i in one view: and he has that places of suspicion are excepted from agencies, and this is a place of suspicion by evidence of the rejection of testimony, and because benefits are connected among them, so it is a sale from himself in some respect. And this difference applies to Ijarah, Sarf, Salam and the like; because these are places of suspicion; since benefits are connected among them, and for this their testimony is not accepted, and the distinctness of properties does not prevent connection in another respect.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the agent's capacity to contract with individuals whose testimony would be rejected if they testified on his behalf—specifically, close relatives (parents, children, spouses, etc.) and slaves. The central issue is whether the agent may sell to or buy from such persons at the market price. The text begins by stating the general rule: the agent does not contract with those whose testimony is rejected for him. However, it clarifies that this is not a single ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa; rather there are two narrations. In one narration, such a contract is prohibited at the market price; in another, it is permitted. The mudarib (speculator in a mudarabah partnership) is distinguished: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the mudarib can contract at market price with such persons in one narration because he has a share in profit, reducing suspicion. If the principal gives the agent absolute authorization (e.g., 'Sell to whomever you wish'), then the agent may sell at market price even to these relatives. The passage then addresses the agent selling to himself: all Hanafi authorities agree it is not allowed, whether beneficial or detrimental. Those whose testimony is rejected for the agent include blood relatives, spouses, and his own slaves. According to Abu Hanifa, contracting with them is invalid unless the price exceeds market value in sale or is below in purchase—essentially eliminating the suspicion of favoritism. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) allow contracting at market price except with the agent's own slave, mukatab (slave contracted for freedom), minor child, and partner in mutual surety (mufawada). They argue that since the authorization is absolute and properties are separate, there is no suspicion. However, the slave and mukatab are exceptions because the agent effectively contracts with himself—the slave's property belongs to the master, and the master has rights over the mukatab's earnings. The passage notes that Ahmad ibn Hanbal and al-Shafi'i agree in one narration/view that places of suspicion are excepted. The same dispute applies to other contracts like Ijarah (hire), Sarf (currency exchange), and Salam (advance sale) because benefits are connected, making them suspect.

Key Terms
ترد شهادته له (turaddu shahadatuhu lahu): A person whose testimony is legally rejected in favor of the agent due to close relationship (blood, marriage, ownership).
مضارب (mudarib): The working partner in a mudarabah contract; he manages capital and shares profits.
مفاوضة (mufawada): A partnership of mutual surety where each partner is a surety for the other and they share all transactions equally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), from which of the following persons may the agent NOT purchase at market price?
A) The agent's father
B) The agent's son who is a minor
C) The agent's brother
D) The agent's wife

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an agent's contract with close relatives is not automatically invalid; the Sahibayn's view, which is widely followed, permits it at market price unless it amounts to contracting with oneself.

Agent Selling to Himself or His Dependents
Ruling on agent's self-dealing and conflict of interest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent contracting with himself or those under his ownership, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding transactions with relatives at market price.

Core Idea
An agent cannot contract with himself; contracts with close relatives are also prohibited unless the price deviates from market value.

An agent is a fiduciary; he cannot benefit from his position. This passage establishes the absolute prohibition on an agent buying from or selling to himself, then extends the principle to those closely connected to him, such as blood relatives, spouses, and slaves. The Hanafi masters differ on whether transactions with such relatives at market price are permissible, with Abu Hanifa requiring a price above or below the market to remove suspicion of bias.

Guided Path
1
Identify the absolute prohibition
Locate the phrase 'لا يجوز للوكيل البيع والشراء من نفسه' – this is the core ruling. Understand that it applies whether the transaction benefits the principal or the agent.
The prohibition is unanimous among the Hanafi imams.
2
Trace the analogy with rejected testimony
Analyze the reasoning: if the agent cannot contract with those whose testimony is rejected due to close relationship (a weaker suspicion), then a fortiori he cannot contract with himself (stronger suspicion). List the categories: blood relatives, spouses, and slaves.
This is a qiyas with a minori ad maius (first awla) argument.
3
Differentiate Abu Hanifa's position
Note that Abu Hanifa permits contracts with these relatives only if the price exceeds market value in a sale or is below market value in a purchase. This condition removes the suspicion of self-dealing.
The exception for relatives only applies when the price deviates from the norm.
4
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani allow contracts at market price with relatives, except with the agent's own slave, mukatab, minor son, and mufawada partner. Understand why these exceptions exist: in these cases the agent is effectively contracting with himself (slave's property belongs to master, mukatab's earnings are subject to master's right, son is under guardianship, mufawada partner is a surety).
The Sahibayn rely on the absolute nature of the agency and absence of suspicion when properties are distinct.
5
Apply the ruling to examples
Given a scenario, determine whether an agency contract is valid. For instance, an agent selling to his own brother at market price: according to Abu Hanifa, invalid unless price is above market; according to Sahibayn, valid. For agent selling to his own slave: invalid according to both, because it is like selling to himself.
Practice identifying which rule applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا لا يجوز للوكيل البيع والشراء من نفسه عندهم جميعاً، كان خيراً أو شراً، للموكل أو الوكيل؛ لأنه إذا لم يملك العقد مع من ترد شهادته له، فأولى أن لا يملكه من نفسه، ومن ترد شهادته للوكيل هم الذين بهم قرابة الولاد، وأحد الزوجين للآخر، وعبيده، فالعقد مع لا يجوز عند أبي حنيفة إلا إذا زاد على ثمن المثل في البيع، ونقص عن ثمن المثل في الشراء. وقالا يجوز بيعه منهم بمثل القيمة إلا من عبده ومكاتبه وابنه الصغير ومفاوضة؛ لأن التوكيل مطلق، ولا تهمة؛ إذ الأملاك بينهم متباينة، والمنافع منقطعة، بخلاف العبد؛ لأنه بيع من نفسه؛ لأن ما في يد العبد للمولى، وكذا للمولى حق في كسب المكاتب، وينقلب حقيقة بالعجز، فصار عاقداً مع نفسه، وذلك لا يجوز، وبه قال أحمد في رواية والشافعي في وجه
Likewise, it is not permissible for the agent to sell to or buy from himself according to all of them, whether it is good or bad, for the principal or the agent; because if he does not have the authority to contract with one whose testimony is rejected for him, then it is more fitting that he does not have authority over himself. And those whose testimony is rejected for the agent are those related by birth, one spouse for the other, and his slaves. So the contract with them is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa unless he increases above the market value in sale, and decreases below the market value in purchase. And the two said: his selling to them at the equivalent of the value is permissible, except from his slave, his mukatab, his minor son, and his partner in mutual surety; because the authorization is absolute, and there is no suspicion; since properties are distinct among them, and benefits are disconnected, unlike the slave; because it is a sale from himself; since what is in the slave's hand belongs to the master, and likewise the master has a right in the earnings of the mukatab, and he returns to being a slave by disability, so he becomes a contractor with himself, and that is not permissible. And Ahmad said in one narration and al-Shafi'i in one view.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that the agent cannot contract with himself. All Hanafi scholars agree that the agent cannot buy from or sell to himself, regardless of whether the result benefits or harms the principal or the agent. The reasoning is a minori ad maius: if the agent cannot contract with persons whose testimony is rejected due to relationship (which is a weaker suspicion), then a fortiori he cannot contract with himself (stronger suspicion). The text then lists those whose testimony is rejected for the agent: blood relatives (parents, children, etc.), spouses, and slaves. The dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding contracting with these persons at market price is reiterated: Abu Hanifa requires a price above or below market to remove suspicion, while the Sahibayn allow market price except with the agent's own slave, mukatab (slave seeking freedom), minor child, and mufawada partner. The exception is because in these cases the agent effectively contracts with himself: the slave's property is the master's, the mukatab's earnings are subject to the master's right, and the minor child is under the agent's guardianship. This view is also supported by Ahmad and al-Shafi'i in one narration.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of manumission with his master, paying in installments.
مفاوضة (mufawada): Partner in a mutual surety partnership; each is a surety for the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Selling to his own slave
B) Selling to his own mukatab
C) Selling to his own adult brother
D) Selling to his own minor son

Key Takeaway
An agent cannot contract with himself, and the prohibition extends to close relatives under some conditions; the Hanafi school's relied-upon view allows market-price transactions with relatives except where the counterparty is legally equivalent to the agent.

Agent Selling to Principal's Close Relatives
Commentary expanding on an exception from the base ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when an agent sells to a close relative of the principal and distinguish it from the case where the relative is of the agent.

Core Idea
An agent may sell to the principal's close relatives because the conflict concerns the principal, not the agent.

The prohibition on an agent selling to those whose testimony is rejected for him is based on avoiding suspicion of collusion. But when the relationship is with the principal, the agent has no personal incentive, so the sale is valid. This distinction is a key principle in Hanafi agency law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the prohibition
Recognize that the base rule (mentioned in previous lessons) prohibits an agent from selling to those whose testimony is rejected for the agent himself, such as his own close relatives.
This is to prevent the agent from favouring his own kin at the principal's expense.
2
Identify the exception for the principal's relatives
The text clarifies that the prohibition does not extend to those whose testimony is rejected for the principal. Understand that the rationale for prohibition (suspicion of collusion) applies to the agent's personal relationships, not the principal's.
The agent has no personal interest in the principal's relatives.
3
Recall the list of persons whose testimony is rejected
The text provides a list: parents and grandparents, children and grandchildren, spouses, master for his slave, mukatab, and partners. Memorize this list as it applies to both agent and principal contexts.
The same list applies for both, but the ruling differs based on whose relationship is involved.
4
Apply the distinction to a new scenario
Practice by considering: an agent selling to the principal's son vs. selling to his own son. The former is valid, the latter is not (unless special conditions).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بقوله: "لا"؛ لأنه لو عقد مع من ترد شهادته للموكل كأبيه وابنه ومكاتبه وعبده المديون جاز، وكذا وكيل العبد إذا باع من مولاه. عيني وفتح وزيلعي) من ترد إلخ: وهم الأبوان والأجداد والجدات وإن علوا، والأولاد وإن سفلوا، والزوجات، والسيد لمملوكه، والمكاتب، والشريك للشريك. (مسكين)
And he restricted by his saying 'La' (no); because if he contracts with one whose testimony is rejected for the principal, like his father, his son, his mukatab, and his indebted slave, it is permissible. And likewise the agent of a slave if he sells from his master. (Ayni, Fath, Zaylai) 'Those whose testimony is rejected' etc.: they are the two parents, grandparents and grandmothers even if ascending, the children even if descending, the spouses, the master for his slave, the mukatab, and the partner for his partner. (Miskin)
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the prohibition discussed earlier (agent not contracting with those whose testimony is rejected for him) does not apply when the testimony is rejected for the principal rather than the agent. The agent may sell to the principal's close relatives (father, son, etc.) because the potential conflict of interest lies with the principal, not the agent. Similarly, the agent of a slave may sell from the slave's master. The text then provides a comprehensive list of those whose testimony is rejected (for the agent or principal): parents and ancestors, children and descendants, spouses, the master for his slave, the mukatab, and partners. This list is derived from the commentaries of Ayni, Fath al-Qadir, and Zaylai.

Key Terms
ترد شهادته للموكل (turaddu shahadatuhu lil-muwakkil): A person whose testimony is legally rejected in favor of the principal due to close relationship.
وكيل (wakil): Agent; one appointed to act on behalf of another.
موكل (muwakkil): Principal; the person who appoints an agent.
شهادة مردودة (shahada marduda): Testimony that is rejected due to close relationship or financial interest.

Key Takeaway
An agent may sell to the principal's close relatives because the conflict of interest concerns the principal's relationships, not the agent's.

Agent's Purchase Authority: Market Value and Slight Overpayment
Ruling on the agent's purchase authority and definition of permissible overpayment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid purchase by an agent with absolute authority and identify the boundaries of permissible overpayment (ghabn yasir vs. ghabn fahish) according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
An agent's purchase is valid only at market value or with slight overpayment that people tolerate; excessive overpayment invalidates due to suspicion of self-dealing.

When a principal appoints an agent to buy without specifying a price, the agent's authority is not unlimited. The Hanafi school restricts the agent to the market price or a slight overpayment that people ordinarily overlook. This lesson explains the rule, its rationale, and how to distinguish slight from excessive overpayment using expert valuation and fixed percentages.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an agent purchasing for a principal with absolute authority. The ruling: valid only if price is market value or within ghabn yasir; invalid if ghabn fahish.
2
Locate the rationale (dalil aqli)
The proof is rational: suspicion of self-dealing (tuhma). The agent might overpay to benefit the seller or buy for himself and later attribute the bad deal to the principal. Hence the restriction prevents abuse.
Consensus (ijma) also supports the restriction to market value.
3
Define ghabn yasir vs. ghabn fahish
Use the text's definition: the range of expert valuators (e.g., 7–10) is yasir; beyond that is fahish. Also note the percentage limits: goods 5%, animals 10%, real estate 20%, dirhams 2.5%.
The percentage limits are given as the 'limit of excessive overpayment' (had al-fahish).
4
Apply the exception for known-price goods
For goods like bread, meat, bananas, cheese, any deviation, even one fils, is not excused (ghabn fahish). This is the mufta bihi position.
Known-price goods have a fixed market; any overpayment indicates self-dealing.
5
Determine the consequence
If the agent buys with ghabn fahish, the purchase is invalid (batil) and the agent is liable. If within ghabn yasir, the purchase is valid and binding on the principal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقيد شراؤه بمثل القيمة، وزيادة الوكيل بالبيع المطلق أي شراء الوكيل ويقيد شراؤه إلخ: أي إذا وكل رجلاً بالشراء المطلق عن التقييد بثمن، يجوز شراء الوكيل شيئاً بمثل القيمة، ويجوز شراؤه بزيادة يتغابن الناس في تلك الزيادة، واحترز به عما إذا اشتراه بزيادة لا يتغابن فيها، فإنه لا يجوز؛ لأن التهمة فيه متحققة، فلعله اشتراه لنفسه، فلما لم يوافقه ألحقه بغيره والتقييد بمثل القيمة بالإجماع، والمراد بالتغابن الخداع، وقولهم: 'لا يتغابن الناس فيه' فمعناه لا يخدع بعضهم بعضاً لفحشه، وقولهم: 'يتغابن الناس فيه' أي يخدع بعضهم بعضاً؛ لقلته. (فتح) وهو ما يدخل إلخ [ أي الغبن اليسير الذي لا يخلو عنه عقود الناس. (عيني)] أي الغبن اليسير: ما يدخل تحت تقويم المقومين بأن قومه عدل بعشرة وآخر بثمانية وآخر بسبعة فما بين العشرة والسبعة داخل تحت تقويم المقومين، وما لا يدخل تحت تقويمهم فهو غبن فاحش، وقيل: حد الفاحش في العروض نصف عشر القيمة، وفي الحيوانات عشر القيمة، وفي العقار خمس القيمة وفي الدراهم ربع عشر القيمة، وهذا إذا كان سعره غير معروف بين الناس، ويحتاج فيه إلى تقويم المقومين، وأما إذا كان معروفاً كالخبز واللحم والموز والجبن، لا يعفى فيه الغبن وإن قل، ولو كان فلساً واحداً وبه يفتى رمز الحقائق: ۱۷۷/۲] (فتح)
And his purchase is restricted to the equivalent of the value, and the increase of the agent for absolute sale—i.e., the purchase of the agent—is restricted etc.: that is, if a man appoints an agent for absolute purchase without restriction to a price, the agent may buy something at the equivalent of the value, and he may buy it at an increase that people overlook (yatasabun) in that increase. And he guarded against if he buys it at an increase that is not overlooked, then it is not permissible; because suspicion is actualized in it—perhaps he bought it for himself, and when it did not suit him he attributed it to someone else. And restriction to the equivalent of the value is by consensus. And the meaning of 'tasabun' is deception. Their saying 'people do not overlook it' means they do not deceive each other due to its excessiveness, and their saying 'people overlook it' means they deceive each other due to its slightness. (Fath) And it is what enters etc.: i.e., the slight overpayment (ghabn yasir) which contracts of people are not free from. (Ayni) That is, slight overpayment: what enters under the valuation of the valuators, in that one valuer evaluates it at ten, another at eight, another at seven, so what is between ten and seven enters under the valuation of the valuators, and what does not enter under their valuation is excessive overpayment (ghabn fahish). And it is said: the limit of excessive overpayment in goods (urud) is half of a tenth of the value; in animals, a tenth of the value; in real estate, a fifth of the value; and in dirhams, a quarter of a tenth of the value. And this is when its price is not known among people and requires valuation by valuators. As for when it is known, like bread, meat, bananas, and cheese, overpayment is not excused even if little, even if it is one fils, and that is the fatwa. (Ramooz al-Haqa'iq: 2/177) (Fath)
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage discusses the purchase authority of an agent with absolute authority to buy. Unlike sale, purchase is suspect because the agent might overpay to benefit the seller or buy for himself and then attribute the bad deal to the principal. Therefore, the agent's purchase is restricted to the market price (mithl al-qima) or to a slight overpayment (ghabn yasir) that people ordinarily tolerate. If the overpayment is excessive (ghabn fahish), the purchase is invalid because the suspicion of self-dealing is confirmed. The restriction to market price is by consensus. The text then defines ghabn yasir (slight overpayment) and ghabn fahish (excessive overpayment) using the concept of valuation by experts (taqwim al-muqawwimin). If valuators differ, the range of their estimates (e.g., 7 to 10) constitutes ghabn yasir; anything beyond that is fahish. The text also gives specific percentage limits for different asset types: goods (arud) 5%, animals 10%, real estate 20%, and dirhams 2.5%. These limits apply when the market price is not commonly known. For commodities with known prices (bread, meat, etc.), any deviation, even one fils, is not excused, and that is the relied-upon fatwa. This distinction protects the agent from being held liable for slight fluctuations while preventing abuse.

Key Terms
غبن فاحش (ghabn fahish): Excessive overpayment that people do not normally accept; varies by property type.
غبن يسير (ghabn yasir): Slight overpayment that falls within the range of valuation experts' estimates.
تقويم المقومين (taqwim al-muqawwimin): Valuation by expert appraisers; used to determine acceptable overpayment range.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an agent for purchase buys a sheep for 110 dirhams when the market value is 100 dirhams, what is the ruling?
A) Valid, because 10% is within the limit for animals.
B) Invalid, because any overpayment on animals is excessive.
C) Valid, because the agent has absolute authority.
D) Invalid, because the agent cannot pay more than 100.

Key Takeaway
An agent with absolute purchase authority must buy at market value or within the range of slight overpayment (ghabn yasir) defined by expert valuation; excessive overpayment (ghabn fahish) invalidates the purchase due to suspicion of self-dealing.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Agent Selling Half of a Slave
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent selling half of a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an agent selling half of a slave and identify the mufta bihi position with its justification.

Core Idea
When an agent sells half of a slave, it is invalid due to partnership harm unless he later buys the other half.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding the validity of an agent selling only half of a slave when authorized to sell the whole. The disagreement revolves around whether absolute authorization covers partial sale, and the harm of partnership that partial sale creates. Understanding this dispute illustrates the Hanafi school's methodology of applying istihsan alongside qiyas.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify what Abu Hanifa rules regarding an agent selling half of a slave when authorized to sell the whole. He holds it is valid.
His basis is the absoluteness of the authorization (itlaq al-tawkil).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the sale is invalid because it causes harm of partnership (darar al-sharika), which is a defect reducing value.
They argue that such partial sale is not covered by the absolute command.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Sahibayn's dalil: Selling half creates a partnership, which is detrimental and not intended by the principal, so it cannot be included under the absolute authorization.
They use the principle that the agent must not exceed what is customary or beneficial.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to the opponent
The text provides an exception: if the agent purchases the remaining half before any dispute arises, then the original sale becomes valid as a means to compliance (tariq al-imitithal).
This exception reconciles the need for partial sale with the harm of partnership.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical consequence: the sale is invalid unless the agent later buys the other half. The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view, based on istihsan, as stated by the three later scholars.
Abu Hanifa's view is qiyas; the relied-upon view is istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وكله ببيع عبد، فباع نصفه صح. ولو وكله وضع المسألة في بيع العبد؛ لأن الخلاف بين الإمام وصاحبيه في الجواز وعدمه مقيد بما يتعيب بالشركة، وكل شيء لا يتعيب بالشركة يجوز بيع نصفه اتفاقاً. عيني فتح صح: أي البيع؛ لأن اللفظ مطلق ومتفرقا. (عيني) أي عند أبي حنيفة؛ لإطلاق التوكيل، وعندهما لا يصح؛ لأن فيه ضرر الشركة، وهي عيب تنقص به القيمة فلا يدخل تحت الأمر المطلق، فلا يجوز، إلا أن النصف الآخر قبل أن يختصما؛ لأن يبيع النصف قد يقع وسيلة إلى الامتثال بأن لا يجد من يشتريه جملة، فيحتاج إلى التفريق، ويتبين ذلك ببيع الباقي بعده، وبقولهما قالت الثلاثة، وهو استحسان والقياس ما قاله أبو حنيفة.
And if he authorized him to sell a slave, and he sold half of it, it is valid. And if he authorized him, placement of the issue in selling the slave; because the disagreement between the Imam and his two companions regarding permissibility and its negation is restricted to what is impaired by partnership, and everything that is not impaired by partnership, it is permissible to sell half of it by agreement. 'Ayni and Fath say 'valid': i.e., the sale; because the wording is absolute and it is permissible collectively and separately. ('Ayni) i.e., according to Abu Hanifa; because of the absoluteness of the authorization, and according to them (the two) it is not valid; because there is harm of partnership, which is a defect that diminishes value, so it is not included under the absolute command, so it is not permissible, except that the other half before they dispute; because selling half may occur as a means to compliance by not finding someone to buy it in bulk, so he needs to divide, and that becomes clear by selling the remaining after it, and according to them the three gave fatwa, and it is istihsan, and the qiyas is what Abu Hanifa said.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of an agent selling only half of a slave when authorized to sell the entire slave. The matn states that selling half is valid, but the commentary explains that this is only according to Abu Hanifa, who argues that the absolute authorization (tawkil mutlaq) allows the agent to sell any part. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) hold that it is not valid because selling half creates a harmful partnership (sharika), which is a defect that reduces the value and thus is not covered by the absoluteness of the command. They argue that the agent's action only becomes valid if he later purchases the remaining half before any dispute arises, as this demonstrates that the partial sale was a means to complete the transaction. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the Sahibayn, as the later scholars ('the three') gave fatwa on it, and it is based on istihsan, while Abu Hanifa's view is based on strict qiyas.

Key Terms
شركة (sharika): Partnership in ownership; here, the agent selling half creates a partnership between the principal and the buyer.
ضرر الشركة (darar al-sharika): Harm of partnership; a defect that reduces value.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; the method used by Sahibayn to override strict qiyas.
وكيل (wakil): Agent, the person authorized to act on behalf of another.
موكل (muwakkil): Principal, the person who authorizes the agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi), when is it valid for an agent to sell half of a slave he is authorized to sell?
A) Always valid because the authorization is absolute.
B) Valid only if the agent also buys the remaining half before any dispute.
C) Valid only if the principal explicitly permits partial sale.
D) Never valid because it causes partnership.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi on an agent selling half is that it is invalid unless the agent later acquires the remaining half, based on istihsan to avoid partnership harm.

Agent’s Purchase of Half a Slave
Ruling on an agent buying half a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent’s purchase of half a slave and explain the legal rationale based on tuhma and the difference between purchase and sale.

Core Idea
An agent’s purchase of half a slave is suspended until the remaining half is bought to prevent self-dealing.

When an agent is authorized to buy a slave but buys only half, the contract is not immediately effective. The Hanafi school rules that such a purchase is suspended (tawaqquf) until the agent acquires the other half. This ruling protects the principal from potential self-interest by the agent and reflects a key distinction between sale and purchase in agency law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is an agent buying half a slave when authorized to buy a whole slave. The ruling is that the purchase is suspended (tawaqquf) – it is not immediately binding on the principal.
Suspension means the contract awaits a condition to become effective.
2
Understand the Distinction between Sale and Purchase
In sale (the agent selling the principal’s property), the agent has authority over the principal’s property, so the sale is immediately binding. In purchase, the agent deals with another’s property, raising suspicion of self-interest, so the purchase is suspended.
The command to sell encounters the principal’s ownership; purchase encounters a third party’s ownership.
3
Analyze the Rationale: Tuhma (Suspicion)
Abu Hanifa argues that when an agent buys half, there is a suspicion that he might act for his own benefit (e.g., buy the other half cheaply). Thus the contract is suspended until the agent eliminates this suspicion by buying the remaining half.
Suspicion is not present in sale because the agent is selling the principal’s own property.
4
Apply the Ruling to Scenarios
If the agent buys the remaining half before any dispute arises, the suspension ends and the purchase becomes binding on the principal. This applies whether the slave is specifically identified or not.
If the agent fails to buy the rest before dispute, the purchase is binding on the agent himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الشراء يتوقف إلخ : أي لو وكله في الشراء بأن يشتري له عبداً ، فاشترى الوكيل نصف العبد، يتوقف شراؤه ما لم يشتر النصف الباقي، والفرق لأبي حنيفة: أن في الشراء يتحقق التهمة دون البيع، ولأن الأمر بالبيع يصادف ملكه فيعتبر فيه إطلاقه، والأمر بالشراء صادف ملك الغير، فلم يصح ، فلم يعتبر فيه الإطلاق، ولا فرق بين التوكيل بشراء عبد بعينه أو بغير عينه، فإن اشترى باقيه قبل أن يختصما لزم الموكل؛ لأن شراء البعض قد يقع وسيلة إلى الامتثال بأن يكون موروثاً بين جماعة، فيحتاج إلى شرائه شقصاً شقصاً، فإذا اشترى الباقي قبل رد الأمر البيع تبين أنه وسيلة فينفذ على الموكل بالاتفاق، وإلا لزم الوكيل. (جوهري، فتح)
And regarding purchase, it is suspended, etc.: i.e., if he authorized him to purchase by buying a slave for him, and the agent bought half of the slave, his purchase is suspended until he buys the remaining half. The distinction according to Abu Hanifa: that in purchase, suspicion is realized without sale, and because the command to buy encounters the property of another, so it is not valid, so absoluteness is not considered in it. There is no difference between authorization to buy a specific slave or an unspecified one. If he buys the rest before they dispute, it is binding on the principal; because buying a part may occur as a means to compliance, such as when an inheritance is among a group, so there is need to purchase it piece by piece. If he buys the rest before rejecting the sale, it becomes clear that it was a means, so it is effective on the principal by agreement; otherwise, it is binding on the agent. (Jawhari, Fath)
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when an agent authorized to buy a slave purchases only half. According to Abu Hanifa, the purchase is suspended (tawaqquf) until the agent buys the remaining half. The reason for the difference between sale and purchase is that in purchase there is a suspicion (tuhma) of self-interest, and the command to buy involves acquiring property from a third party, so the absolute authorization is not considered as effective. However, if the agent buys the remaining half before any dispute, it becomes a means to complete the transaction and is binding on the principal. This applies whether the slave is specifically identified or not.

Key Terms
توقف (tawaqquf): Suspension; the purchase is not immediately effective but awaits a condition.
تهمة (tuhma): Suspicion; here, the suspicion that the agent might act for his own benefit.
وكالة (wakala): Agency; a contract authorizing another to act on one's behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the status of an agent's purchase of half a slave when authorized to buy a slave?
A) The purchase is immediately valid and binding on the principal.
B) The purchase is suspended until the agent buys the remaining half.
C) The purchase is void and never becomes valid.
D) The purchase is valid only if the principal ratifies it later.

Key Takeaway
An agent’s purchase of half a slave is suspended until the remaining half is bought, preventing self-dealing (tuhma) and allowing the agent to complete the transaction as a valid means.

Joint Agency: Acts Permissible for One Agent Alone
Lists four exceptions to joint agency and explains the ra'y principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four actions where one of two joint Hanafi agents may act alone and explain the underlying principle (no need for ra'y).

Core Idea
An agent can act alone when no opinion (ra'y) is needed—like returning a deposit or paying a debt.

In Hanafi agency law, two agents appointed jointly typically must act together. However, the school recognizes exceptions: actions that do not require independent judgment (ra'y) can be performed by one agent alone. This lesson introduces the four such actions and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four actions listed in the matn
Read the matn: divorce without compensation, emancipation without compensation, returning a deposit, paying a debt.
2
Understand the condition 'without compensation' (bila badal)
Compensation involves evaluation and opinion; its absence makes the action straightforward.
This condition applies only to divorce and emancipation.
3
Grasp the concept of ra'y (independent judgment)
Ra'y is required when the action involves valuation, deliberation, or choice. If ra'y is needed, joint action is required.
Returning a deposit and paying a debt do not require ra'y.
4
Compare with actions that require joint action
For example, taking possession of a deposit or collecting a debt require ra'y because the principal has an interest in dual custody.
The text contrasts returning with taking possession.
5
Note the practical application
A single agent can validly return a borrowed item or pay a debt from the principal's funds without the other agent's involvement.
If he acts outside these exceptions, the act may be invalid or he becomes a guarantor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة
تصرفات الوكيل
وطلاق وعتاق بلا بدل وردّ وديعة وقضاء دين
وطلاق وعتاق بلا بدل: [ أي وفي طلاق أيضاً يجوز تصرف أحدهما منفردا. (عيني)] وإنما قيدهما بغير عوض؛ لأنه إذا كان التوكيل للاثنين في الطلاق ببدل أو العتاق يبدل، لا يجوز إنفراد أحدهما وحده؛ لأنه يحتاج فيه إلى الرأي. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۷۹/۲] والمراد بالطلاق والعتاق أن يكونا منجزين بأن قال: طلقاها أو أعتقاه، وأن يكون المرأة والعبد معينين فلو وكلهما، وقال: طلقاها إن شئتما، أو قال أمرها بأيديكما لا ينفرد أحدهما بالطلاق، وكذا بالعتاق، وكذا لو وكلهما بطلاق واحدة بغير عينها، أو بعتق عبد غير معين لزم اجتماعهما؛ لأنه يحتاج إلى الرأي، بخلاف ما إذا قال: طلقاها أو أعتقاه إن شئتما؛ لأنه تعليق بمشيتهما، فلا ينزل الطلاق والعتاق عند مشية وكذا في جعل الأمر بأيديهما؛ لأنه تفويض لا تنجيز وكذا لو قال: طلقاها جميعاً، ليس لأحدهما أن يطلقها وحده، ولو قال: طلقاها جميعاً ثلاثاً، فطلقها أحدهما طلقة، والآخر طلقتين، لا يقع شيء. (فتح) وعتاق: أي وفي عتاق أيضاً يجوز لأحدهما الانفراد بالتصرف. (عيني) بلا بدل يرجع إلى الطلاق والعتاق جميعا. (عيني) وردّ وديعة [ أي فيه أيضا يجوز الانفراد، وكذا رد العارية والمغصوب والمبيع الفاسد. (ط عيني)] قيد بردها؛ لأنه إذا وكل رجلين بقبض الوديعة، ليس لكل واحد منهما أن ينفرد بالقبض، وإذا قبض بدون الآخر، صار ضامناً لكله؛ لأنه قبض بغير إذن المالك؛ إذ أمره تناولهما مجتمعين لا منفردين، فلم يكن مأموراً في حالة الانفراد بقبض شيء، ولو قال المصنف : "ورد" عين" لكان أولى، فإنه لا فرق بين رد الوديعة والعارية والمغصوب والمبيع فاسداً، حيث يجوز الانفراد في ردها؛ لأنه لا يحتاج فيه إلى الرأي، بخلاف قبضها؛ لأن للموكل فيه غرضاً صحيحاً؛ لأن حفظ اثنين خير من حفظ واحد. (عيني، فتح) وقضاء دين أي لو وكل رجلين بأن يقضيا دين الموكل إلى الدائن يجوز أن ينفرد أحدهما بأداء الدين، فإنه مثل رد الوديعة لا يحتاج إلى الرأي، بخلاف قبض الدين، فإن حكمه مثل حكم قبض الوديعة، والوجه فيه ما هو الوجه فيها، والوكيل بقضاء الدين إذا صرف مال الموكل في حاجة نفسه، ثم قضى بمال نفسه دين الموكل، يكون متبرعاً في قضاء دين الموكل. (محشي، فتح)
Book of Agency. Actions of the Agent. And divorce and emancipation without compensation, returning a deposit, and paying a debt. And divorce and emancipation without compensation: [meaning that in divorce as well, it is permissible for one of them to act alone. ('Ayni)] And they are restricted to being without compensation because if the agency for two is in divorce with a compensation or emancipation with a compensation, it is not permissible for one of them alone because it requires opinion. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 179/2] And what is meant by divorce and emancipation is that they are immediate, by saying: 'Divorce her' or 'Emancipate him', and that the woman and the slave are specified. If he appointed two and said: 'Divorce her if you wish' or 'The matter is in your hands', one of them may not divorce alone, and similarly for emancipation. Similarly, if he appointed two to give one divorce without specifying her, or to emancipate a non-specific slave, their joint action is necessary because it requires opinion. Unlike if he said: 'Divorce her or emancipate him if you wish', because it is a condition upon their will, so divorce and emancipation do not occur upon the will; similarly when he makes the matter in their hands, because it is delegation not immediate action. Similarly, if he said: 'Divorce her both together', one may not divorce her alone. And if he said: 'Divorce her three times together', and one gave one divorce and the other two, nothing occurs. (Fath) And emancipation: meaning in emancipation too, one of them may act alone. ('Ayni) 'Without compensation' refers to both divorce and emancipation. ('Ayni) And returning a deposit [meaning in this also, acting alone is permissible, and likewise returning a borrowed item, a usurped item, and a corrupt sale item. (T, 'Ayni)] He restricted it to returning because if he appoints two to take possession of a deposit, it is not permissible for each to take possession alone; if he takes possession without the other, he becomes a guarantor for all of it because he took possession without the owner's permission, since the order applied to both together not individually, so he was not ordered in the state of solitude to take possession of anything. And if the author had said: 'redd 'ayn' it would have been better, because there is no difference between returning a deposit, a borrowed item, a usurped item, and a corrupt sale item, where acting alone is permissible in returning because it does not require opinion, unlike taking possession because the principal has a valid interest in it, as the preservation by two is better than by one. ('Ayni, Fath) And paying a debt: meaning if he appoints two to pay the debt of the principal to the creditor, it is permissible for one of them to discharge the debt alone, because it is like returning a deposit, not requiring opinion, unlike collecting a debt, whose ruling is like that of taking possession of a deposit, and the reasoning is the same. And the agent for paying a debt, if he spends the principal's wealth on his own needs and then pays the principal's debt with his own wealth, he is a volunteer in paying the principal's debt. (Mahshi, Fath)
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on the actions of two agents appointed jointly. The matn lists four cases where one agent may act alone without the other: divorce and emancipation without compensation, returning a deposit, and paying a debt. The commentary, drawn from 'Ayni, Fath, and others, explains the reasoning and conditions.

The key principle is that acting alone is permitted when the action does not require independent opinion (ra'y). Divorce and emancipation without compensation are purely verbal and do not require evaluation, so one may act. However, if compensation is involved, joint action is required because compensation involves valuation and opinion. Similarly, if the divorce or emancipation is conditional or the subject is unspecified, joint action is needed.

Returning a deposit, a borrowed item, a usurped item, or a corrupt sale item does not require opinion; it is a simple physical act of giving back. Hence, one agent may return it alone. In contrast, taking possession (qabd) of a deposit requires joint action because the principal had a valid interest in dual custody for safety.

Paying a debt is like returning a deposit—no opinion needed—so one may pay alone. Collecting a debt, however, is like taking possession and requires joint action.

The passage also notes that if an agent for paying a debt uses the principal's money for himself and then pays the debt from his own money, he is a volunteer (mutabarri') and not entitled to reimbursement.

Key Terms
بلا بدل (bila badal): Without compensation or consideration, referring to divorce and emancipation that do not involve payment.
الرأي (al-ra'y): Independent judgment or opinion that requires deliberation and evaluation, such as assessing compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following actions CANNOT be performed by one of two joint agents alone?
A) Divorce without compensation
B) Taking possession of a deposit
C) Returning a borrowed item
D) Paying a debt

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi agency, solitary action by one of two joint agents is permissible only for acts that do not require independent judgment (ra'y), such as returning an item or paying a debt.

Joint Agency: Conditions for Solitary Action in Divorce and Emancipation
Details conditions for solitary divorce/emancipation by one of two joint agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which one of two joint agents may validly divorce or emancipate alone, and distinguish cases that require joint action.

Core Idea
Divorce or emancipation by one agent is valid only if immediate, specified, and without compensation.

While the general principle permits solitary action for divorce and emancipation without compensation, the Hanafi jurists impose additional conditions: the pronouncement must be immediate (munajjaz), the spouse or slave must be specified, and the wording must not imply delegation. This lesson explores these nuances from the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for valid solitary divorce/emancipation
From the text: must be immediate (munajjaz) and the woman/slave must be specified ('ayn).
2
Understand why conditional or delegation wording invalidates solitary action
Saying 'if you wish' or 'the matter is in your hands' introduces an element of opinion (ra'y), thus requiring joint deliberation.
The text distinguishes tawkil (agency) from tafwid (delegation).
3
Examine cases where the subject is unspecified
If the principal says 'divorce one of my wives' without specification, joint action is required because choice is involved.
4
Analyze the case of 'divorce her both together'
Even without compensation, if wording indicates collective action, one agent cannot act alone.
The text gives an example of 'together' and also of different numbers of divorces.
5
Apply the ra'y principle to these variations
All these conditions essentially revolve around whether the action requires independent judgment (ra'y). If it does, joint action is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة
تصرفات الوكيل
وطلاق وعتاق بلا بدل وردّ وديعة وقضاء دين
وطلاق وعتاق بلا بدل: [ أي وفي طلاق أيضاً يجوز تصرف أحدهما منفردا. (عيني)] وإنما قيدهما بغير عوض؛ لأنه إذا كان التوكيل للاثنين في الطلاق ببدل أو العتاق يبدل، لا يجوز إنفراد أحدهما وحده؛ لأنه يحتاج فيه إلى الرأي. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۷۹/۲] والمراد بالطلاق والعتاق أن يكونا منجزين بأن قال: طلقاها أو أعتقاه، وأن يكون المرأة والعبد معينين فلو وكلهما، وقال: طلقاها إن شئتما، أو قال أمرها بأيديكما لا ينفرد أحدهما بالطلاق، وكذا بالعتاق، وكذا لو وكلهما بطلاق واحدة بغير عينها، أو بعتق عبد غير معين لزم اجتماعهما؛ لأنه يحتاج إلى الرأي، بخلاف ما إذا قال: طلقاها أو أعتقاه إن شئتما؛ لأنه تعليق بمشيتهما، فلا ينزل الطلاق والعتاق عند مشية وكذا في جعل الأمر بأيديهما؛ لأنه تفويض لا تنجيز وكذا لو قال: طلقاها جميعاً، ليس لأحدهما أن يطلقها وحده، ولو قال: طلقاها جميعاً ثلاثاً، فطلقها أحدهما طلقة، والآخر طلقتين، لا يقع شيء. (فتح) وعتاق: أي وفي عتاق أيضاً يجوز لأحدهما الانفراد بالتصرف. (عيني) بلا بدل يرجع إلى الطلاق والعتاق جميعا. (عيني) وردّ وديعة [ أي فيه أيضا يجوز الانفراد، وكذا رد العارية والمغصوب والمبيع الفاسد. (ط عيني)] قيد بردها؛ لأنه إذا وكل رجلين بقبض الوديعة، ليس لكل واحد منهما أن ينفرد بالقبض، وإذا قبض بدون الآخر، صار ضامناً لكله؛ لأنه قبض بغير إذن المالك؛ إذ أمره تناولهما مجتمعين لا منفردين، فلم يكن مأموراً في حالة الانفراد بقبض شيء، ولو قال المصنف : "ورد" عين" لكان أولى، فإنه لا فرق بين رد الوديعة والعارية والمغصوب والمبيع فاسداً، حيث يجوز الانفراد في ردها؛ لأنه لا يحتاج فيه إلى الرأي، بخلاف قبضها؛ لأن للموكل فيه غرضاً صحيحاً؛ لأن حفظ اثنين خير من حفظ واحد. (عيني، فتح) وقضاء دين أي لو وكل رجلين بأن يقضيا دين الموكل إلى الدائن يجوز أن ينفرد أحدهما بأداء الدين، فإنه مثل رد الوديعة لا يحتاج إلى الرأي، بخلاف قبض الدين، فإن حكمه مثل حكم قبض الوديعة، والوجه فيه ما هو الوجه فيها، والوكيل بقضاء الدين إذا صرف مال الموكل في حاجة نفسه، ثم قضى بمال نفسه دين الموكل، يكون متبرعاً في قضاء دين الموكل. (محشي، فتح)
Book of Agency. Actions of the Agent. And divorce and emancipation without compensation, returning a deposit, and paying a debt. And divorce and emancipation without compensation: [meaning that in divorce as well, it is permissible for one of them to act alone. ('Ayni)] And they are restricted to being without compensation because if the agency for two is in divorce with a compensation or emancipation with a compensation, it is not permissible for one of them alone because it requires opinion. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 179/2] And what is meant by divorce and emancipation is that they are immediate, by saying: 'Divorce her' or 'Emancipate him', and that the woman and the slave are specified. If he appointed two and said: 'Divorce her if you wish' or 'The matter is in your hands', one of them may not divorce alone, and similarly for emancipation. Similarly, if he appointed two to give one divorce without specifying her, or to emancipate a non-specific slave, their joint action is necessary because it requires opinion. Unlike if he said: 'Divorce her or emancipate him if you wish', because it is a condition upon their will, so divorce and emancipation do not occur upon the will; similarly when he makes the matter in their hands, because it is delegation not immediate action. Similarly, if he said: 'Divorce her both together', one may not divorce her alone. And if he said: 'Divorce her three times together', and one gave one divorce and the other two, nothing occurs. (Fath) And emancipation: meaning in emancipation too, one of them may act alone. ('Ayni) 'Without compensation' refers to both divorce and emancipation. ('Ayni) And returning a deposit [meaning in this also, acting alone is permissible, and likewise returning a borrowed item, a usurped item, and a corrupt sale item. (T, 'Ayni)] He restricted it to returning because if he appoints two to take possession of a deposit, it is not permissible for each to take possession alone; if he takes possession without the other, he becomes a guarantor for all of it because he took possession without the owner's permission, since the order applied to both together not individually, so he was not ordered in the state of solitude to take possession of anything. And if the author had said: 'redd 'ayn' it would have been better, because there is no difference between returning a deposit, a borrowed item, a usurped item, and a corrupt sale item, where acting alone is permissible in returning because it does not require opinion, unlike taking possession because the principal has a valid interest in it, as the preservation by two is better than by one. ('Ayni, Fath) And paying a debt: meaning if he appoints two to pay the debt of the principal to the creditor, it is permissible for one of them to discharge the debt alone, because it is like returning a deposit, not requiring opinion, unlike collecting a debt, whose ruling is like that of taking possession of a deposit, and the reasoning is the same. And the agent for paying a debt, if he spends the principal's wealth on his own needs and then pays the principal's debt with his own wealth, he is a volunteer in paying the principal's debt. (Mahshi, Fath)
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on the actions of two agents appointed jointly. The matn lists four cases where one agent may act alone without the other: divorce and emancipation without compensation, returning a deposit, and paying a debt. The commentary, drawn from 'Ayni, Fath, and others, explains the reasoning and conditions.

The key principle is that acting alone is permitted when the action does not require independent opinion (ra'y). Divorce and emancipation without compensation are purely verbal and do not require evaluation, so one may act. However, if compensation is involved, joint action is required because compensation involves valuation and opinion. Similarly, if the divorce or emancipation is conditional or the subject is unspecified, joint action is needed.

Returning a deposit, a borrowed item, a usurped item, or a corrupt sale item does not require opinion; it is a simple physical act of giving back. Hence, one agent may return it alone. In contrast, taking possession (qabd) of a deposit requires joint action because the principal had a valid interest in dual custody for safety.

Paying a debt is like returning a deposit—no opinion needed—so one may pay alone. Collecting a debt, however, is like taking possession and requires joint action.

The passage also notes that if an agent for paying a debt uses the principal's money for himself and then pays the debt from his own money, he is a volunteer (mutabarri') and not entitled to reimbursement.

Key Terms
منجز (munajjaz): Immediate, unconditional, not suspended on a condition.
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of authority where the agents have discretion, as opposed to tawkil (agency with specific instructions).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two agents are told: 'Divorce one of my wives without compensation.' Can one agent act alone?
A) Yes, because no compensation is involved.
B) No, because the subject is unspecified and requires opinion.
C) Yes, because the agent can choose.
D) No, because divorce always needs two witnesses.

Key Takeaway
For solitary divorce/emancipation to be valid, the action must be immediate, the subject specified, and no opinion required—otherwise joint action is necessary.

Sub-Agency in Hanafi Law
Ruling on sub-agency with proof and inter-madhhab comparison.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on sub-agency and apply the conditions under which a sub-agent's actions are valid.

Core Idea
An agent cannot delegate without permission; the principal's trust is personal, not transferable.

In Hanafi law, the relationship between principal and agent is built on personal trust. This lesson explores when an agent may appoint a sub-agent and what happens if he does so without permission. We will also compare the Hanafi view with other schools.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi default rule
Identify the general prohibition: the agent may not appoint a sub-agent without the principal's permission or a general delegation (tafwid).
Key phrase: 'ليس له ذلك إلا أن يكون له إذن الموكل أو قال له الموكل : اعمل برأيك'.
2
Understand the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The principal was satisfied with the agent's opinion, not another's. The agent was entrusted to act, not to delegate.
Hanafi proof: trust is specific to the person.
3
Identify exceptions and conditions
If the principal says 'act on your opinion' (tafwid) or explicitly permits, sub-agency is allowed. Also, if the price is fixed, the agent may contract in absentia.
Tafwid gives full discretion, including delegation.
4
Compare with other schools
Ahmad permits sub-agency freely; Shafi'i and Malik allow only if the agent cannot act personally. The thamarah (practical difference) is validity of sub-agent's actions.
Hanafi: sub-agent's actions are invalid unless ratified.
5
Determine the need for ratification
Even if the sub-agent acts in the presence of the first agent, most Hanafis require explicit ratification (ijazah) by the principal or first agent. Mere presence is insufficient according to the majority.
Exception: divorce and emancipation are not ratified by presence due to their conditional nature.

Source Text

ولا يوكل وكيل [ أي ليس له أن يوكل فيما وكل فيه (ط) عيني)] وقال أحمد: للوكيل أن يوكل بلا إذن الموكل وعندنا: ليس له ذلك إلا أن يكون له إذن الموكل أو قال له الموكل : اعمل برأيك"؛ لأنه رضي برأيه دون رأي غيره، فلا يملكه بدون التفويض، ولأن المفوض إليه التصرف دون التوكيل به إلا إذا قدر له الثمن فإنه يجوز عقده في غيبته، وعند الشافعي ومالك: ليس له التوكيل بهذين الوجهين أيضاً إلا أن يعجز الوكيل عن مباشرته بنفسه، فإذا فوض الموكل إليه ووكل الوكيل رجلا آخر كان الثاني وكيلاً عن الموكل، حتى لا يكون للأول أن يعزله، ولا ينعزل بموته، وينعزلان بموت الموكل، وهو نظير استخلاف القاضي، حيث لا يملكه إلا بإذن الخليفة .
