إلا بإذن أو باعمل برأيك"، فإن وكل بلا إذن الموكل، فعقد بحضرته، أو باع من الموكل أو قال له الموكل الوكيل الثاني الوكيل الأول أجنبي، فأجاز، صح، وإن زوج عبد أو مكاتب أو كافر صغيرته الحرة المسلمة، أو الوكيل الأول بيع الأجنبي باع لهـ لها أو اشترى، لم يجز.
أي للصغيرة المسلمة لها بمالها أي زوج مكاتب ذمي أو حربي
= ثم لا ينعزل الثاني بعزل القاضي الأول ولا بموته وينعزلان بعزل الخليفة لهما، لكن لا ينعزلان بموت الخليفة، والفرق: أن الخليفة عامل للمسلمين فلا ينعزل الذي ولاه هو أو ولاه القاضي بإذنه، والموكل عامل لنفسه فينعزل وكيله بموته؛ لبطلان حقه رمز الحقائق: ۱۷۹/۲] (فتح، معدن) صح
:: أي لو وكل الوكيل بلا إذن الموكل، فعقد الوكيل الثاني بحضرة الوكيل الأول، أو باع أجنبي، فأجاز الوكيل الأول بيع الأجنبي، صح في الوجهين؛ لأن المقصود حضور رأيه، وقد حصل، وترجع الحقوق إلى الوكيل الثاني في الأصح؛ لأنه هو العاقد، وقيل: إلى الأول؛ لأن الموكل رضي بلزوم العهدة للأول، وظاهر كلامه الاكتفاء بالحضرة، وهو قول البعض، والعامة على أنه لابد من إجازة الوكيل أو الموكل، وأن حضرة الوكيل الأول لا تكفي، وقيد بالعقد احترازاً عن الطلاق والعتاق؛ لأنهما يقبلان التعليق بالشرط، فكان الموكل علقه بلفظ الأول، فلا تطلق بتطليق الوكيل الثاني بحضرة الأول، بخلاف البيع فإنه لا يقبل التعليق. (عيني، فتح) لم يجز : [ أي كل واحد من التزويج والبيع والشراء، والمعنى لا يجوز تصرف هؤلاء في مالها. (معدن)] أي التزويج والتصرف في مالها بالبيع أو الشراء؛ لأنه لا ولاية لهؤلاء، ألا ترى أن العبد لا يملك النكاح لنفسه، فكيف يملك إنكاح غيره، وكذا الكافر لا ولاية له على المسلم، ولا فرق بين أن يكون ذمياً أو حربيّاً أو مستأمناً، وأما المرتد إذا زوج صغيرته الحرة المسلمة أو تصرف في مال الصغير ومات على الردة لم يجز تصرفه، وقبل الموت تصرفه على ولده موقوف إجماعاً.
بخلاف تزوجه بنفسه، حيث لا يجوز وإن أسلم بعد ذلك؛ لأن جواز النكاح يعتمد الملة، ولا ملة للمرتد، فلا يتوقف؛ إذ لا مجيز له في الحال، وشرط التوقف أن يكون له مجيز في الحال، وتزويجه أولاده الصغار يتوقف؛ لأن له مجيزا في الحال، وهو الولي أو القاضي، فإن أسلم نفذ وصح النكاح، وإن مات بطل، والمكاتب عبد ما بقي عليه فحكمه كالعبد لا يملك التصرف، ولو قال المصنف : "أو اشترى لها بمالها" لكان أولى؛ لأنه إذا اشترى لها بمال نفسه كان مشتريا لنفسه، وذلك جائز لا محالة وعدم الجواز فيما إذا اشترى لها بمالها. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
And the agent does not appoint a sub-agent [meaning he may not appoint someone in what he was appointed to do (T, 'Ayni)]. Ahmad said: The agent may appoint a sub-agent without the principal's permission, while according to us (Hanafi) he may not do so unless he has the principal's permission or the principal said to him: 'Act on your own opinion', because he was satisfied with his opinion not the opinion of another, so he does not possess it without delegation; also because what is delegated to him is the action itself, not the appointment of a sub-agent, except if the price is set for him, then he may contract in absentia. According to al-Shafi'i and Malik, he may not appoint a sub-agent even in these two ways unless the agent is unable to perform the action himself. If the principal delegated to him, and the agent appointed another man, then the second becomes an agent of the principal, so that the first cannot dismiss him, and he is not dismissed by the death of the first, and both are dismissed by the death of the principal. This is analogous to the substitution of a qadi, who cannot do it except by the permission of the caliph. 
= 
Except with permission or with 'act on your own opinion'. If the agent appointed a sub-agent without the principal's permission, and he (the sub-agent) contracted (or sold) in the presence of the first agent, or sold to a stranger, and the first agent ratified it, then it is valid. And if a slave, or a mukatib, or a disbeliever marries off his free Muslim minor daughter, or the first agent sells the sale of the stranger, or buys for her, it is not valid. [Meaning the free Muslim minor daughter, with her wealth; i.e., a mukatib, dhimmi, or harbi marries off.] 
= Then the second (sub-agent) is not dismissed by the dismissal of the first agent nor by his death, and both are dismissed by the dismissal of the caliph (concerning the trustee of a waqf?), but they are not dismissed by the death of the caliph. The difference is that the caliph is an agent for the Muslims, so the one he appointed or the qadi appointed with his permission is not dismissed, while the principal is an agent for himself, so his agent is dismissed by his death due to the termination of his right. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 179/2) (Fath, Madan) Sahh (valid). 
:: That is, if the agent appointed a sub-agent without the principal's permission, and the second agent contracted in the presence of the first agent, or a stranger sold, and the first agent ratified the sale of the stranger, then it is valid in both cases because the purpose—the presence of his opinion—has been achieved. The rights go back to the second agent according to the most correct view because he is the contractor; it is also said they go back to the first because the principal consented to remain liable to the first. The apparent meaning of his statement is that mere presence suffices, which is the view of some, but the general view is that ratification by the agent or the principal is necessary, and the presence of the first agent alone is not sufficient. He restricted it to 'contract' to exclude divorce and emancipation, because they are subject to condition, so the principal attached them to the first's wording, so the wife is not divorced by the divorce of the second agent in the presence of the first, unlike sale which is not subject to condition. ('Ayni, Fath) 'Lam yajuz' [it is not valid]: meaning each of marriage, sale, and purchase; the meaning is that the action of these persons in her wealth is not valid. (Madan) I.e., marriage and disposal of her wealth by sale or purchase, because they have no authority. Do you not see that the slave does not have the right to marry himself, so how can he have the right to marry off others? Similarly, the disbeliever has no authority over a Muslim, and there is no difference whether he is a dhimmi, harbi, or musta'min. As for the apostate, if he marries off his free Muslim minor daughter or disposes of the minor's wealth and dies upon apostasy, his action is not valid; before death, his action regarding his child is suspended (mawquf) by consensus. Unlike his own marriage, which is not valid even if he later becomes Muslim, because the validity of marriage depends on the religion (millah), and the apostate has no religion, so it is not suspended because there is no validator (mujiz) at present. The condition for suspension is that there be a validator at present. His marrying off his minor children is suspended because there is a validator at present (the guardian or judge). If he becomes Muslim, it is effective and the marriage is valid; if he dies, it is void. The mukatib is a slave as long as anything remains of his kitabah, so his ruling is like a slave; he does not have the right to dispose. And if the author had said: 'or bought for her with her wealth', it would have been better, because if he buys for her with his own wealth, he is purchasing for himself, and that is undoubtedly permissible; the impermissibility is when he buys for her with her wealth. ('Ayni, Fath, 'Inayah)
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage covers two main rulings: (1) the permissibility of sub-agency, and (2) the validity of actions by certain agents (slave, mukatib, disbeliever, apostate).

First, the Hanafi position: an agent may not appoint a sub-agent unless the principal explicitly permitted him or told him to 'act on your own opinion' (i'mal bi-ra'yika). The reason is that the principal trusted the agent's judgment, not that of another. If the agent appoints a sub-agent without permission, the sub-agent's actions are not valid unless the first agent (or principal) ratifies them, provided the sub-agent acted in the presence of the first agent or with his attendance. However, the majority view requires explicit ratification, not mere presence. The comparison with the caliph and qadi illustrates the principle: when the delegator (principal) dies, the agency ends; when the delegator is an institution like the caliph, the deputy's term continues beyond the delegator's death.

Second, the passage addresses individuals who lack legal capacity to act as agents for marriage or financial transactions. A slave, mukatib, or disbeliever cannot act as a guardian to marry off a free Muslim minor girl or dispose of her wealth because they lack the requisite authority (wilayah). An apostate's actions regarding his children are suspended pending his return to Islam or death. The distinction is made between his own marriage (which is invalid and cannot become valid later) and his marriage of his children (which can be validated if he returns to Islam). A mukatib is still a slave regarding capacity, so he cannot act as an agent for such matters.

Throughout, the commentary draws from 'Ayni, Fath, and other Hanafi authorities, reinforcing the principle that agency requires capacity and proper delegation.

Key Terms
توكيل (tawkil): The act of appointing a sub-agent; delegating the agency to another.
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of authority; in this context, giving the agent full discretion including ability to delegate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when can an agent appoint a sub-agent without the principal's explicit permission?
A) When the agent is too busy to perform the task.
B) When the principal says 'act on your own opinion'.
C) When the agent is unable to act personally.
D) When the sub-agent is also the principal's friend.

Key Takeaway
An agent may not delegate without permission; if delegated (tafwid), the sub-agent becomes the principal's agent directly.

Validity of Acts by Incapable Agents
Rulings on the validity of marriage and financial acts by incapable persons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which persons lack capacity to act as agents or guardians and explain the rulings on their actions.

Core Idea
Slaves, mukatibs, disbelievers, and apostates cannot validly act as guardians for free Muslim minors.

Not everyone has the legal capacity to marry off a minor or dispose of their wealth. This lesson examines the rulings for specific categories: the slave, the mukatib, the disbeliever (dhimmi, harbi, musta'min), and the apostate. Understanding these limitations prevents invalid contracts and protects the rights of minors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories discussed
The text mentions: slave ('abd), mukatib (writing contract), disbeliever (kafir: dhimmi, harbi, musta'min), and apostate (murtadd).
Each has a different level of incapacity.
2
Determine the act in question
The act is marrying off a free Muslim minor girl (tazwij al-saghirah al-hurrah al-muslimah) or disposing of her wealth (bai'/shira).
The ruling is about guardianship (wilayah), not personal actions.
3
Apply the rule for each category
For slave and mukatib: they lack wilayah over others because they do not even own themselves. For disbeliever: they have no authority over a Muslim. For apostate: his actions are suspended (mawquf) if there is a validator; his own marriage is invalid.
Apostate's actions regarding children are suspended pending return to Islam or death.
4
Understand the suspension (tawaqquf) vs invalidity
The apostate's marriage of his minor children is suspended because there is a validator (the guardian or judge) who can ratify if he returns to Islam. His own marriage is invalid and cannot become valid later.
Condition for suspension: there must be a validator at the present time.
5
Distinguish between purchase with own wealth vs minor's wealth
If a person buys for the minor with his own money, it is his own purchase and valid. The impermissibility is when he buys with the minor's wealth.
The author suggests a clearer formulation to avoid confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يوكل وكيل [ أي ليس له أن يوكل فيما وكل فيه (ط) عيني)] وقال أحمد: للوكيل أن يوكل بلا إذن الموكل وعندنا: ليس له ذلك إلا أن يكون له إذن الموكل أو قال له الموكل : اعمل برأيك"؛ لأنه رضي برأيه دون رأي غيره، فلا يملكه بدون التفويض، ولأن المفوض إليه التصرف دون التوكيل به إلا إذا قدر له الثمن فإنه يجوز عقده في غيبته، وعند الشافعي ومالك: ليس له التوكيل بهذين الوجهين أيضاً إلا أن يعجز الوكيل عن مباشرته بنفسه، فإذا فوض الموكل إليه ووكل الوكيل رجلا آخر كان الثاني وكيلاً عن الموكل، حتى لا يكون للأول أن يعزله، ولا ينعزل بموته، وينعزلان بموت الموكل، وهو نظير استخلاف القاضي، حيث لا يملكه إلا بإذن الخليفة .
= 
إلا بإذن أو باعمل برأيك"، فإن وكل بلا إذن الموكل، فعقد بحضرته، أو باع من الموكل أو قال له الموكل الوكيل الثاني الوكيل الأول أجنبي، فأجاز، صح، وإن زوج عبد أو مكاتب أو كافر صغيرته الحرة المسلمة، أو الوكيل الأول بيع الأجنبي باع لهـ لها أو اشترى، لم يجز.
أي للصغيرة المسلمة لها بمالها أي زوج مكاتب ذمي أو حربي
= ثم لا ينعزل الثاني بعزل القاضي الأول ولا بموته وينعزلان بعزل الخليفة لهما، لكن لا ينعزلان بموت الخليفة، والفرق: أن الخليفة عامل للمسلمين فلا ينعزل الذي ولاه هو أو ولاه القاضي بإذنه، والموكل عامل لنفسه فينعزل وكيله بموته؛ لبطلان حقه رمز الحقائق: ۱۷۹/۲] (فتح، معدن) صح
:: أي لو وكل الوكيل بلا إذن الموكل، فعقد الوكيل الثاني بحضرة الوكيل الأول، أو باع أجنبي، فأجاز الوكيل الأول بيع الأجنبي، صح في الوجهين؛ لأن المقصود حضور رأيه، وقد حصل، وترجع الحقوق إلى الوكيل الثاني في الأصح؛ لأنه هو العاقد، وقيل: إلى الأول؛ لأن الموكل رضي بلزوم العهدة للأول، وظاهر كلامه الاكتفاء بالحضرة، وهو قول البعض، والعامة على أنه لابد من إجازة الوكيل أو الموكل، وأن حضرة الوكيل الأول لا تكفي، وقيد بالعقد احترازاً عن الطلاق والعتاق؛ لأنهما يقبلان التعليق بالشرط، فكان الموكل علقه بلفظ الأول، فلا تطلق بتطليق الوكيل الثاني بحضرة الأول، بخلاف البيع فإنه لا يقبل التعليق. (عيني، فتح) لم يجز : [ أي كل واحد من التزويج والبيع والشراء، والمعنى لا يجوز تصرف هؤلاء في مالها. (معدن)] أي التزويج والتصرف في مالها بالبيع أو الشراء؛ لأنه لا ولاية لهؤلاء، ألا ترى أن العبد لا يملك النكاح لنفسه، فكيف يملك إنكاح غيره، وكذا الكافر لا ولاية له على المسلم، ولا فرق بين أن يكون ذمياً أو حربيّاً أو مستأمناً، وأما المرتد إذا زوج صغيرته الحرة المسلمة أو تصرف في مال الصغير ومات على الردة لم يجز تصرفه، وقبل الموت تصرفه على ولده موقوف إجماعاً.
بخلاف تزوجه بنفسه، حيث لا يجوز وإن أسلم بعد ذلك؛ لأن جواز النكاح يعتمد الملة، ولا ملة للمرتد، فلا يتوقف؛ إذ لا مجيز له في الحال، وشرط التوقف أن يكون له مجيز في الحال، وتزويجه أولاده الصغار يتوقف؛ لأن له مجيزا في الحال، وهو الولي أو القاضي، فإن أسلم نفذ وصح النكاح، وإن مات بطل، والمكاتب عبد ما بقي عليه فحكمه كالعبد لا يملك التصرف، ولو قال المصنف : "أو اشترى لها بمالها" لكان أولى؛ لأنه إذا اشترى لها بمال نفسه كان مشتريا لنفسه، وذلك جائز لا محالة وعدم الجواز فيما إذا اشترى لها بمالها. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
And the agent does not appoint a sub-agent [meaning he may not appoint someone in what he was appointed to do (T, 'Ayni)]. Ahmad said: The agent may appoint a sub-agent without the principal's permission, while according to us (Hanafi) he may not do so unless he has the principal's permission or the principal said to him: 'Act on your own opinion', because he was satisfied with his opinion not the opinion of another, so he does not possess it without delegation; also because what is delegated to him is the action itself, not the appointment of a sub-agent, except if the price is set for him, then he may contract in absentia. According to al-Shafi'i and Malik, he may not appoint a sub-agent even in these two ways unless the agent is unable to perform the action himself. If the principal delegated to him, and the agent appointed another man, then the second becomes an agent of the principal, so that the first cannot dismiss him, and he is not dismissed by the death of the first, and both are dismissed by the death of the principal. This is analogous to the substitution of a qadi, who cannot do it except by the permission of the caliph. 
= 
Except with permission or with 'act on your own opinion'. If the agent appointed a sub-agent without the principal's permission, and he (the sub-agent) contracted (or sold) in the presence of the first agent, or sold to a stranger, and the first agent ratified it, then it is valid. And if a slave, or a mukatib, or a disbeliever marries off his free Muslim minor daughter, or the first agent sells the sale of the stranger, or buys for her, it is not valid. [Meaning the free Muslim minor daughter, with her wealth; i.e., a mukatib, dhimmi, or harbi marries off.] 
= Then the second (sub-agent) is not dismissed by the dismissal of the first agent nor by his death, and both are dismissed by the dismissal of the caliph (concerning the trustee of a waqf?), but they are not dismissed by the death of the caliph. The difference is that the caliph is an agent for the Muslims, so the one he appointed or the qadi appointed with his permission is not dismissed, while the principal is an agent for himself, so his agent is dismissed by his death due to the termination of his right. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 179/2) (Fath, Madan) Sahh (valid). 
:: That is, if the agent appointed a sub-agent without the principal's permission, and the second agent contracted in the presence of the first agent, or a stranger sold, and the first agent ratified the sale of the stranger, then it is valid in both cases because the purpose—the presence of his opinion—has been achieved. The rights go back to the second agent according to the most correct view because he is the contractor; it is also said they go back to the first because the principal consented to remain liable to the first. The apparent meaning of his statement is that mere presence suffices, which is the view of some, but the general view is that ratification by the agent or the principal is necessary, and the presence of the first agent alone is not sufficient. He restricted it to 'contract' to exclude divorce and emancipation, because they are subject to condition, so the principal attached them to the first's wording, so the wife is not divorced by the divorce of the second agent in the presence of the first, unlike sale which is not subject to condition. ('Ayni, Fath) 'Lam yajuz' [it is not valid]: meaning each of marriage, sale, and purchase; the meaning is that the action of these persons in her wealth is not valid. (Madan) I.e., marriage and disposal of her wealth by sale or purchase, because they have no authority. Do you not see that the slave does not have the right to marry himself, so how can he have the right to marry off others? Similarly, the disbeliever has no authority over a Muslim, and there is no difference whether he is a dhimmi, harbi, or musta'min. As for the apostate, if he marries off his free Muslim minor daughter or disposes of the minor's wealth and dies upon apostasy, his action is not valid; before death, his action regarding his child is suspended (mawquf) by consensus. Unlike his own marriage, which is not valid even if he later becomes Muslim, because the validity of marriage depends on the religion (millah), and the apostate has no religion, so it is not suspended because there is no validator (mujiz) at present. The condition for suspension is that there be a validator at present. His marrying off his minor children is suspended because there is a validator at present (the guardian or judge). If he becomes Muslim, it is effective and the marriage is valid; if he dies, it is void. The mukatib is a slave as long as anything remains of his kitabah, so his ruling is like a slave; he does not have the right to dispose. And if the author had said: 'or bought for her with her wealth', it would have been better, because if he buys for her with his own wealth, he is purchasing for himself, and that is undoubtedly permissible; the impermissibility is when he buys for her with her wealth. ('Ayni, Fath, 'Inayah)
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage covers two main rulings: (1) the permissibility of sub-agency, and (2) the validity of actions by certain agents (slave, mukatib, disbeliever, apostate).

First, the Hanafi position: an agent may not appoint a sub-agent unless the principal explicitly permitted him or told him to 'act on your own opinion' (i'mal bi-ra'yika). The reason is that the principal trusted the agent's judgment, not that of another. If the agent appoints a sub-agent without permission, the sub-agent's actions are not valid unless the first agent (or principal) ratifies them, provided the sub-agent acted in the presence of the first agent or with his attendance. However, the majority view requires explicit ratification, not mere presence. The comparison with the caliph and qadi illustrates the principle: when the delegator (principal) dies, the agency ends; when the delegator is an institution like the caliph, the deputy's term continues beyond the delegator's death.

Second, the passage addresses individuals who lack legal capacity to act as agents for marriage or financial transactions. A slave, mukatib, or disbeliever cannot act as a guardian to marry off a free Muslim minor girl or dispose of her wealth because they lack the requisite authority (wilayah). An apostate's actions regarding his children are suspended pending his return to Islam or death. The distinction is made between his own marriage (which is invalid and cannot become valid later) and his marriage of his children (which can be validated if he returns to Islam). A mukatib is still a slave regarding capacity, so he cannot act as an agent for such matters.

Throughout, the commentary draws from 'Ayni, Fath, and other Hanafi authorities, reinforcing the principle that agency requires capacity and proper delegation.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has a contract (kitabah) with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; a legal act that is pending validation upon fulfilment of a condition, such as the apostate's return to Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a slave marrying off a free Muslim minor girl?
A) Valid because the slave is a relative.
B) Invalid because the slave has no capacity to marry himself.
C) Valid only if the master permits.
D) Suspended until the slave is freed.

Key Takeaway
Slaves, mukatibs, disbelievers, and apostates lack the capacity to act as guardians for free Muslim minors; apostate's actions may be suspended if there is a validator.

Rulings on the Second Agent and Invalid Agencies
States conditions for validity of second agent's contract and invalid agencies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a second agent's contract is valid and identify which individuals cannot act as agents for a Muslim minor in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
A second agent's contract is only valid if the first agent is present or ratifies; slaves and disbelievers cannot act for Muslim minors.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rules on agency when a first agent appoints a second agent without the principal's permission. It also covers who may not serve as an agent for a free Muslim minor, such as slaves, disbelievers, and apostates, and explains the consequences of their actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for second agent
Determine when a second agent appointed without permission can validly contract: either in the presence of the first agent or with subsequent ratification.
The passage contrasts two views: mere presence may suffice (some scholars) vs. explicit ratification is required (the general view).
2
List the invalid agents for Muslim minors
Identify the categories of individuals whose agency for a free Muslim minor is invalid: slave, mukatib, disbeliever (dhimmi, harbi, musta'min), and apostate after death.
The common reason: they lack legal authority (wilayah) over a Muslim due to their own incapacity or status.
3
Understand the ruling on apostate's agency
Distinguish between the apostate's own marriage (void immediately) and his agency for his minor children (suspended, valid if he returns to Islam, void if he dies in apostasy).
The difference lies in whether there is a current ratifier (guardian/judge) for the children's case.
4
Apply the rules to removal of agents
Note that the second agent is not removed by removal/death of the first agent, but both are removed by removal of the caliph who appointed them, not by the caliph's death.
The caliph is an agent for the Muslims, so his death does not terminate his appointees' authority; the principal's death does terminate his agent's authority.

تصرفات الوكيل 

 إلا بإذن أو باعمل برأيك، فإن وكل بلا إذن الموكل، فعقد بحضرته، أو باع من الموكل أو قال له الموكل الوكيل الثاني الوكيل الأول أجنبي، فأجاز، صح، وإن زوج عبد أو مكاتب أو كافر صغيرته الحرة المسلمة، أو الوكيل الأول بيع الأجنبي باع لهـ لها أو اشترى، لم يجز. 

 أي للصغيرة المسلمة لها بمالها أي زوج مكاتب ذمي أو حربي 

 ثم لا ينعزل الثاني بعزل القاضي الأول ولا بموته وينعزلان بعزل الخليفة لهما، لكن لا ينعزلان بموت الخليفة، والفرق: أن الخليفة عامل للمسلمين فلا ينعزل الذي ولاه هو أو ولاه القاضي بإذنه، والموكل عامل لنفسه فينعزل وكيله بموته؛ لبطلان حقه. 

 :: أي لو وكل الوكيل بلا إذن الموكل، فعقد الوكيل الثاني بحضرة الوكيل الأول، أو باع أجنبي، فأجاز الوكيل الأول بيع الأجنبي، صح في الوجهين؛ لأن المقصود حضور رأيه، وقد حصل، وترجع الحقوق إلى الوكيل الثاني في الأصح؛ لأنه هو العاقد، وقيل: إلى الأول؛ لأن الموكل رضي بلزوم العهدة للأول، وظاهر كلامه الاكتفاء بالحضرة، وهو قول البعض، والعامة على أنه لابد من إجازة الوكيل أو الموكل، وأن حضرة الوكيل الأول لا تكفي، وقيد بالعقد احترازاً عن الطلاق والعتاق؛ لأنهما يقبلان التعليق بالشرط، فكان الموكل علقه بلفظ الأول، فلا تطلق بتطليق الوكيل الثاني بحضرة الأول، بخلاف البيع فإنه لا يقبل التعليق. 

 لم يجز : [ أي كل واحد من التزويج والبيع والشراء، والمعنى لا يجوز تصرف هؤلاء في مالها. ] أي التزويج والتصرف في مالها بالبيع أو الشراء؛ لأنه لا ولاية لهؤلاء، ألا ترى أن العبد لا يملك النكاح لنفسه، فكيف يملك إنكاح غيره، وكذا الكافر لا ولاية له على المسلم، ولا فرق بين أن يكون ذمياً أو حربيّاً أو مستأمناً، وأما المرتد إذا زوج صغيرته الحرة المسلمة أو تصرف في مال الصغير ومات على الردة لم يجز تصرفه، وقبل الموت تصرفه على ولده موقوف إجماعاً. بخلاف تزوجه بنفسه، حيث لا يجوز وإن أسلم بعد ذلك؛ لأن جواز النكاح يعتمد الملة، ولا ملة للمرتد، فلا يتوقف؛ إذ لا مجيز له في الحال، وشرط التوقف أن يكون له مجيز في الحال، وتزويجه أولاده الصغار يتوقف؛ لأن له مجيزا في الحال، وهو الولي أو القاضي، فإن أسلم نفذ وصح النكاح، وإن مات بطل، والمكاتب عبد ما بقي عليه فحكمه كالعبد لا يملك التصرف، ولو قال المصنف : "أو اشترى لها بمالها" لكان أولى؛ لأنه إذا اشترى لها درهم بمال نفسه كان مشتريا لنفسه، وذلك جائز لا محالة وعدم الجواز فيما إذا اشترى لها بمالها.
The acts of the agent. Except with permission or if he said 'act according to your opinion'. If he appointed without the principal's permission, and the [second] agent made a contract in the presence of [the first agent], or sold to the principal, or the principal said to him [the second agent], or an outsider sold and the first agent ratified, it is valid. But if a slave, a mukatib, or a disbeliever marries his [the principal's] free Muslim minor daughter, or the first agent sold the outsider's sale, or buys for her, it is not valid. That is, for the free Muslim minor [girl] with her property, i.e., a mukatib dhimmi or harbi married [her]. Then, the second [agent] is not removed by the removal of the first [agent] by the judge nor by his death, and both are removed by the removal of the caliph who appointed them both, but they are not removed by the death of the caliph. The difference is that the caliph is an agent for the Muslims, so the one whom he appointed or whom the judge appointed with his permission is not removed, whereas the principal is an agent for himself, so his agent is removed by his death due to the nullification of his right. Meaning: If the agent appointed without the principal's permission, and the second agent made a contract in the presence of the first agent, or an outsider sold and the first agent ratified the outsider's sale, it is valid in both cases, because the aim is the presence of his opinion, and that has been achieved. The rights revert to the second agent in the most correct view, because he is the contractor; and it is said [they revert] to the first, because the principal consented to the liability binding the first. The apparent [meaning] of his statement is that mere presence suffices, which is the view of some. The general [view] is that the ratification of the agent or principal is necessary, and the presence of the first agent alone is not sufficient. He restricted [the ruling] to contracts to exclude divorce and manumission, because they accept conditional suspension, so the principal suspended them by the words of the first [agent]; thus divorce does not occur by the divorce of the second agent in the presence of the first, contrary to sale, which does not accept conditional suspension. 'It is not valid' means each of marriage, sale, and purchase, i.e., the acts of these [individuals] regarding her property are not valid. That is, marriage and disposal of her property by sale or purchase, because these have no authority. Do you not see that a slave does not possess marriage for himself, so how can he possess marrying others? Likewise, a disbeliever has no authority over a Muslim, and there is no difference whether he is a dhimmi, a harbi, or a musta'min. As for an apostate, if he marries his free Muslim minor daughter or disposes of the property of a minor and dies in apostasy, his disposition is not valid. Before death, his disposition over his child is suspended by consensus, contrary to his own marriage, which is not valid even if he later accepts Islam, because the validity of marriage depends on [having a] religion, and an apostate has no religion, so it is not suspended since there is no one to ratify it in the present. The condition for suspension is that there is someone who can ratify it in the present. His marriage of his minor children is suspended because there is someone to ratify it in the present, namely the guardian or the judge; if he accepts Islam it takes effect and the marriage is valid; if he dies it is void. The mukatib is a slave as long as anything remains [of his kitabah], so his ruling is that of a slave: he does not possess the right to dispose. If the author had said 'or buys for her with her own property' it would have been better, because if he buys for her a dirham with his own property, he is buying for himself, and that is undoubtedly permissible; the non-permissibility is when he buys for her with her own property.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents rulings on the actions of an agent (wakil). It opens by stating the general rule: an agent's act is valid only if permitted by the principal. The text then discusses a scenario where the first agent appoints a second agent without the principal's permission. The key point is that the second agent's contract is valid only if made in the presence of the first agent or if the first agent subsequently ratifies it. The passage clarifies that mere presence of the first agent may not be sufficient according to the general view; explicit ratification is required. The ruling is specific to contracts like sale, and does not apply to divorce or manumission because those can be made conditional, so the principal's words are considered as having suspended the effect. The passage then addresses invalid agencies: a slave, a mukatib (slave under contract of manumission), or a disbeliever cannot validly act as an agent for a free Muslim minor in matters of marriage or property disposal because they lack legal authority over Muslims. An apostate's agency is governed by special rules: his acts are suspended before death, and invalid after death if he died in apostasy, except his own marriage which is void ab initio. The passage also notes the difference between agent removal by the principal's death versus the caliph's death, due to the nature of their agency. Throughout, the text relies on commentary from 'Ayni, Fath, and Ma'din, reflecting the Hanafi school's detailed fiqh.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakil): The agent appointed by the principal (al-muwakkil) to perform a specific legal act on his behalf.
الموكل (al-muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent to act on his behalf.
المكاتب (al-mukatib): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitabah) with his master, paying in installments; he remains a slave until fully paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, when is the contract of a second agent appointed without the principal's permission valid?
A) Only if the principal gives explicit permission
B) If made in the presence of the first agent or ratified by him
C) If the second agent is a slave
D) If the contract is for marriage

Key Takeaway
A second agent's contract is valid if the first agent is present or ratifies; slaves, mukatibs, disbelievers, and apostates (after death) cannot act as agents for a free Muslim minor.

Agency for Litigation and Receiving: Scope of Authority
Explains the scope of agency for litigation and receiving with intra-madhhab differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on whether an agent for litigation has authority to receive debts and whether an agent for receiving has authority to litigate, explain the proofs for each position, and apply the custom-based exception.

Core Idea
A litigation agent does not automatically have receiving authority—custom determines the scope.

When you appoint someone to sue on your behalf, does that automatically give them the power to collect the money? The Hanafi school has a nuanced answer. In this lesson, we explore the debate between Zufar and the three scholars (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) and the relied-upon position that hinges on local custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Issues
The passage deals with two questions: (1) Does an agent appointed for litigation (khusuma) have the right to receive payment? (2) Does an agent appointed to receive a debt have the right to litigate?
2
State the Positions
For the first issue, Zufar says no; the three scholars say yes. For the second, Abu Hanifa says yes; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say no. The mufta bihi follows Zufar on issue one and Abu Hanifa on issue two.
3
Analyze the Proofs
The three scholars reason: agency for a thing includes its completion, and litigation is completed only by receipt. Zufar counters: betrayal is common; one trusted with litigation may not be trusted with money; consent to litigation is not consent to receiving.
Zufar's argument is based on 'ghalabat al-khiyana' (prevalence of betrayal).
4
Note the Custom Exception
The fatwa is conditioned: if local custom among merchants is that the litigator also receives, then the litigation agent does have receiving authority. Otherwise, he does not.
This shows the role of 'urf (custom) in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Apply to the Reverse Issue
For an agent for receiving, Abu Hanifa holds he can litigate because receiving implies taking ownership, making him a litigant. The Sahibayn disagree, saying receiving is not litigation and trust in handling money does not imply skill in litigation.
The disagreement is limited to creditor-appointed agents; judge-appointed agents do not litigate by consensus.

Source Text

باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض 

 أي في بيان أحكامها أي قبض الديون 

 الوكيل بالخصومة والتقاضي لا يملك القبض، وبقبض الدين يملك الخصومة 

 أي طلب الدين عند زفر وعليه الفتوى أي الوكيل بقبض إلخ 

 باب إلخ: [ وهي قول يستعمل في إظهار الحق. ] أخر الوكالة بالخصومة عن الوكالة بالبيع والشراء؛ لأن الخصومة تقع باعتبار ما يجب استيفاؤه ممن هو في ذمته، وذلك في الأغلب يكون لمطالبة المبيع أو الثمن؛ أو لأن الخصومة مهجورة شرعاً، فاستحقت التأخير مما ليس بمهجور. الوكيل بالخصومة إلخ: وهو تفاعل من قضى دينه، أو اقتضيت منه ديني، وتقاضيت ديني أي أخذت وفي العرف هو المطالبة لا القبض. لا يملك القبض: وهذا مذهب زفر وعليه الفتوى، ولهذا اختاره في المتن تبعاً لاختيار السرخسي، لكن الفتوى على أنه ينظر إن كان التوكيل بالتقاضي في بلدة العرف فيها بين التجار أن المتقاضي هو الذي يقبض الدين كان الوكيل بالتقاضي وكيلا بالقبض، وإلا فلا وبقول زفر قالت الثلاثة أيضاً؛ لظهور الخيانة في الوكلاء، وقد يؤتمن على الخصومة من لا يؤتمن على المال، ولأنه رضي بخصومته لا القبض وكما لا يملك القبض لا يملك الصلح إجماعاً. 

 ورسول التقاضي يملك القبض لا الخصومة إجماعاً، ولا يملك الخصومة والقبض وكيل الملازمة كما لا يملك الخصومة وكيل الصلح، وعند علمائنا الثلاثة: الوكيل بالخصومة يملك القبض عيناً كان أو ديناً؛ لأن الوكيل بالشيء وكيل بإتمامه، وإتمام الخصومة والتقاضي يكون بالقبض، وما لم يقبض فالخصومة قائمة فيه؛ لتوهم الإنكار بعد ذلك، وتعذر الإثبات بعارض من موت القاضي أو المطل أو الإفلاس، وما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب 

 عن 

 يملك الخصومة عند أبي حنيفة، حتى لو أقام المدعى عليه البينة أن رب الدين استوفى منه الدين أو أبرأه عن الدين، تقبل بينته عنده؛ لأن ما يقبضه الوكيل ملك للمطلوب، فيكون الأمر بقبضه أمراً للتمليك، فينتصب الوكيل خصماً، كالوكيل بقبض الشفعة، وقالا : لا يكون خصماً، وهو رواية عنه، وبه قال زفر والثلاثة إلا في قول للشافعي ورواية أحمد؛ لأن القبض غير خصومة وليس كل من يؤتمن على المال يهتدي في الخصومات، فلم يكن الرضا بالقبض رضا بالخصومة، ولأن التوكيل يتناول قبض الدين لا غير ولأبي حنيفة أنه وكله بالتملك؛ لأن الديون تقضى بأمثالها؛ إذ قبض الدين نفسه لا يتصور إلا أنه جعل استيفاء لعين حقه من وجه فأشبه الوكيل بأخذ الشفعة فيكون وكذا إذا جحد الغريم وأقام الوكيل البينة عليه تقبل عنده، خلافاً لهما. ثم اعلم أن الخلاف بين الإمام وصاحبيه في أن وكيل القبض يملك الخصومة أو لا مقيد بما إذا كان وكيل الدائن، أما إذا وكله القاضي بقبض مال الغائب فلا يكون وكيلاً بالخصومة اتفاقاً، بخلاف وكيل القسمة والأخذ بالشفعة، والرجوع في الهبة والرد بالعيب، فإنه يملك الخصومة مع القبض اتفاقاً.
Chapter of Agency for Litigation and Receiving [Debts]. That is, explaining its rulings, i.e., receiving debts. The agent for litigation and taking legal action does not possess [the right of] receiving [payment], and [the agent] for receiving debt possesses litigation. That is, [the right to] demand the debt, according to Zufar, and upon it is the fatwa, i.e., the agent for receiving etc. The chapter: litigation is a statement used to manifest the right. He delayed the agency for litigation after agency for sale and purchase because litigation occurs regarding what is due from someone in his liability, and that is mostly for demanding the sold item or price; or because litigation is detested in Shari'ah, so it deserved to be delayed after what is not detested. The agent for litigation: it is from 'qada daynahu' or 'iqtaḍaytu minhu dayni' meaning I took, and in custom it is demanding, not receiving. He does not possess receiving: This is the madhhab of Zufar, and upon it is the fatwa; for this reason the author chose it following the choice of al-Sarakhsi. But the fatwa is that it is considered: if the agency for litigation was in a town where the custom among merchants is that the litigator is the one who receives the debt, then the agent for litigation is an agent for receiving; otherwise not. By Zufar's saying, the three also spoke, because of the prevalence of betrayal among agents, and sometimes one is trusted with litigation who is not trusted with wealth, and because he consented to his litigation not to receiving. Just as he does not possess receiving, he does not possess compromise (sulh) by consensus. The messenger for litigation possesses receiving but not litigation by consensus. The agent of continuous attendance does not possess litigation and receiving, just as the agent for compromise does not possess litigation. According to our three scholars: the agent for litigation possesses receiving, whether it is a specific item or a debt, because the agent for a thing is an agent for its completion, and the completion of litigation is by receiving; as long as he does not receive, the litigation remains concerning it, due to the possibility of denial afterwards and difficulty of proof due to an impediment like death of the judge, delay, or bankruptcy; and what is necessary for the completion of an obligation is itself obligatory. Concerning [the reverse]: He possesses litigation according to Abu Hanifa, so that even if the defendant brings evidence that the creditor received the debt from him or discharged him from it, his evidence is accepted according to him [Abu Hanifa], because what the agent receives becomes the property of the defendant, so the order to receive it is an order to transfer ownership; thus the agent becomes a litigant, like the agent for receiving pre-emption. The two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: he is not a litigant, and this is a transmission from him [Abu Hanifa], and this is what Zufar and the three [scholars] said, except for a saying of al-Shafi'i and a narration of Ahmad, because receiving is not litigation, and not everyone trusted with wealth is skilled in litigations, so consent to receiving is not consent to litigation, and because the agency includes only receiving the debt. For Abu Hanifa, he appointed him to take ownership because debts are paid by their equivalents; since receiving the debt itself is inconceivable, it is considered as taking possession of his right in a way, so it resembles the agent for taking pre-emption; thus he [the agent] becomes a litigant. Likewise, if the debtor denies and the agent brings evidence against him, it is accepted according to him, contrary to the two [Sahibayn]. Then know that the disagreement between the Imam and his two companions on whether the agent for receiving possesses litigation or not is restricted to when he is an agent of the creditor. If the judge appointed him to receive the property of an absent person, he is not an agent for litigation by agreement, contrary to the agent for division, taking pre-emption, revocation of gifts, and return due to defect, for he possesses litigation along with receiving by agreement.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on Agency for Litigation and Receiving (bāb al-wakāla bil-khuṣūma wal-qabḍ). It opens by establishing the general rule that an agent appointed solely for litigation does not have the right to receive payment, and conversely, an agent appointed to receive a debt does have the right to litigate (according to Zufar and the fatwa). The text then explains why the chapter on litigation is placed after agency for sale: because litigation is discouraged in Shari'ah and usually follows from sale. The central ruling is that an agent for litigation does not automatically have authority to receive; this is the position of Zufar, and it is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi), as chosen by the author following al-Sarakhsi. However, the fatwa is nuanced: if local custom among merchants deems that the litigator also receives, then the agent for litigation does have receiving authority. Zufar's view is supported by the fact that betrayal is common, and one may be trusted with litigation but not with money. By consensus, such an agent cannot make a compromise (sulh). The text then contrasts: a mere messenger for litigation can receive but not litigate, and an agent who is constantly present (wakīl al-mulāzama) has neither. In opposition to Zufar, the three scholars (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold that an agent for litigation does have the right to receive, because agency for a thing includes its completion, and litigation is only completed by receipt. The passage then discusses the reverse: whether an agent for receiving debt has the right to litigate. According to Abu Hanifa, he does, because receiving involves taking ownership, making him a litigant like an agent for pre-emption. The Sahibayn disagree, and their view is also attributed to Zufar and other schools. The disagreement is limited to cases where the agent is appointed by the creditor; if appointed by a judge for an absent person's property, he has no litigation authority by consensus. Exceptions include agents for division, pre-emption, gift revocation, and return due to defect, who do have litigation authority by agreement.

Key Terms
الخصومة (al-khusumah): Litigation or legal disputation, including suing and defending in court.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession or receipt, especially of a debt or property.
التقاضي (al-taqadi): The process of taking legal action, including demanding payment in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in this text, does an agent appointed for litigation have the right to receive the debt?
A) Yes, always
B) No, never
C) No, unless local custom among merchants indicates otherwise
D) Yes, but only if the agent is also a messenger

Key Takeaway
An agent for litigation does not automatically have receiving authority; custom determines the scope, and an agent for receiving does have litigation authority by the mufta bihi.

6 min
Agent for Litigation Does Not Have Power to Receive (Zufar's View)
States the ruling that agent for litigation lacks power to receive, with fatwa based on custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling that an agent for litigation lacks power to receive and identify when local custom overrides this default.

Core Idea
An agent for litigation cannot receive payment unless custom empowers him.

This lesson examines a crucial distinction in Hanafi agency law: the line between the authority to litigate and the authority to take possession. You will see that the default rule—based on Zufar's view—is that an agent appointed only to sue does not have the power to collect. However, as the text shows, local commercial custom can shift this default, reflecting the dynamic relationship between ruling and 'urf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The agent appointed specifically for litigation (al-wakīl bi al-khuṣūmah) does not possess the power to receive (qabḍ) the debt or item from the debtor. This is the stated default.
This applies even if the agent wins the case; receipt is a separate legal act.
2
Understand the rationale (dalīl 'aqlī)
The text gives two reasons: (1) prevalence of betrayal (khiyānah) among agents—a person trusted to argue may not be trusted with money; (2) the principal's consent (riḍā) was for litigation, not receipt. These are rational proofs for the default.
The Hanafi school considers these grounds sufficient to restrict the agent's authority.
3
Note the exception based on custom (urf)
If the local custom among merchants (in the town where the agency is executed) is that the litigant also receives the debt, then the agent for litigation is also considered an agent for receipt. Custom can override the default.
The fatwa (operative ruling) is given on Zufar's view, but the mufti must investigate the 'urf.
4
Recognize the broader consequence: no power to compromise
Just as the agent cannot receive, he cannot compromise (ṣulḥ) the claim by consensus. This reinforces the narrow scope of the agent's authority.
Ṣulḥ is treated as a form of disposal of the right, similar to receipt.

Source Text

باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض
أي في بيان أحكامها
أي قبض الديوا
س م ز ف ك د. الوكيل بالخصومة والتقاضي لا يملك القبض، وبقبض الدين يملك الخصومة
أي طلب الدين عند زفر وعليه الفتوى أي الوكيل بقبض إلخ
باب إلخ: [وهي قول يستعمل في إظهار الحق. (عناية) أخر الوكالة بالخصومة عن الوكالة بالبيع والشراء؛ لأن الخصومة تقع باعتبار ما يجب استيفاؤه ممن هو في ذمته، وذلك في الأغلب يكون لمطالبة المبيع أو الثمن؛ أو لأن الخصومة مهجورة شرعاً، فاستحقت التأخير مما ليس بمهجور. (عناية) الوكيل بالخصومة إلخ: وهو تفاعل من قضى دينه، أو اقتضيت منه ديني، وتقاضيت ديني أي أخذت وفي العرف هو المطالبة لا القبض. لا يملك القبض: وهذا مذهب زفر وعليه الفتوى، ولهذا اختاره في المتن تبعاً لاختيار السرخسي، لكن الفتوى على أنه ينظر إن كان التوكيل بالتقاضي في بلدة العرف فيها بين التجار أن المتقاضي هو الذي يقبض الدين كان الوكيل بالتقاضي وكيلا بالقبض، وإلا فلا وبقول زفر قالت الثلاثة أيضاً؛ لظهور الخيانة في الوكلاء، وقد يؤتمن على الخصومة من لا يؤتمن على المال، ولأنه رضي بخصومته لا القبض وكما لا يملك القبض لا يملك الصلح إجماعاً.
Chapter of Agency for Litigation and Receipt. Meaning: explaining its rulings. Meaning: receipt of debt. s m z f k d. The agent for litigation and claiming does not possess receipt, and by receiving debt he possesses litigation. Meaning: claiming the debt according to Zufar, and upon it is the fatwa, meaning the agent for receipt etc. Chapter etc.: [It is a statement used to manifest truth. (Inayah) He delayed the agency for litigation after agency for sale and purchase because litigation occurs regarding what must be collected from the one in whose liability it is, and that mostly is for claiming the sold item or the price; or because litigation is legally abandoned, so it deserved delay from what is not abandoned. (Inayah) The agent for litigation etc.: It is tafa''ul from qada daynahu, or iqtadaytu minhu dayni, and taqadaytu dayni meaning I took, and in custom it is claiming, not receipt. He does not possess receipt: This is the madhhab of Zufar and upon it is the fatwa, and for this he chose it in the matn following the choice of al-Sarakhsi, but the fatwa is that it is considered: if the authorization for litigation was in a town where the custom among merchants is that the litigant is also the one who receives the debt, then the agent for litigation is also an agent for receipt. Otherwise, he is not. And with Zufar's statement, the three also said: due to the prevalence of betrayal among agents, and sometimes the one trusted with litigation is not trusted with property, and because the principal was satisfied with his litigation, not receipt, and just as he does not possess receipt, he does not possess compromise (sulh) by consensus.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq opens the chapter on agency for litigation and receipt. It first defines key terms: 'khusuma' (litigation) is derived from qada (to fulfill a debt), but in legal custom it means claiming, not actual receipt. The matn then states two rulings: (1) an agent appointed specifically for litigation and claiming (taqadi) does not have the power to receive the debt or item; (2) conversely, an agent appointed to receive a debt does have the power to litigate (i.e., to claim it). The commentary clarifies that the first ruling is the madhhab of Imam Zufar, and it is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). The matn author (al-Nasafi) chose it following al-Sarakhsi. However, the fatwa is nuanced: if the local custom among merchants is that the litigant is also the one who receives payment, then an agent for litigation in that locale is also an agent for receipt. The reason given for Zufar's view is the prevalence of betrayal (khiyanah) among agents; a person may be trustworthy for litigation but not for handling money. Also, the principal's consent to appoint an agent for litigation does not imply consent to receipt. Furthermore, just as the agent cannot receive, he cannot compromise (sulh) the claim. This ruling is presented as part of the Hanafi tradition, with the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad) also reportedly agreeing with Zufar on this point.

Key Terms
الوكيل بالخصومة (al-wakīl bi al-khuṣūmah): An agent appointed specifically to conduct litigation and claim rights on behalf of the principal.
القبض (al-qabḍ): Physical or legal taking possession of a debt or item from the debtor or hand.
الخصومة (al-khuṣūmah): Legal litigation or claiming a right before a judge or authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in this passage, what is the default ruling regarding an agent appointed only for litigation (wakīl bi al-khuṣūmah)?
A) He has the power to both litigate and receive the debt/item.
B) He has the power to litigate but not to receive, unless local custom says otherwise.
C) He has the power to receive but not to litigate.
D) He has neither power until confirmed by a judge.

Key Takeaway
An agent for litigation does not automatically have the power to receive; custom or explicit authorization is required for that power.

Agent for Specific Object Cannot Litigate
Ruling on agent's lack of litigation authority with rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an agent for taking possession of a specific object (wakīl bi qabḍ al-‘ayn) does not have litigation authority, and identify the cross-madhhab difference with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
An agent for a specific object is a messenger, not a litigant.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqā’iq establishes a critical distinction in agency law. The matn tersely states: 'And by taking possession of a specific object, no.' The commentary explains that an agent appointed to collect a specific physical item is merely a messenger and trustee—he cannot sue or be sued. This ruling contrasts with the agent for debt collection, who does have litigation authority. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly structuring agency agreements in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of agents
Locate in the source the two categories: agent for taking possession of a specific object (wakīl bi qabḍ al-‘ayn) and agent for taking possession of a debt (wakīl bi qabḍ al-dayn).
2
State the ruling for each
According to the matn and commentary, the agent for a specific object does NOT have litigation authority; the agent for debt DOES have litigation authority. Note that the ruling on the specific object is agreed upon (ittifāqan) within the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the consequence
The agent for a specific object is a mere messenger (rasūl) and trustee (amīn); he cannot represent the principal in court. The agent for debt is an agent for ownership (wakīl bi al-tamalluk) and becomes a litigant (khaṣm).
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
While the Hanafi school distinguishes based on the subject matter, the Shafi'i school holds that any agent has implicit litigation authority. This difference has practical implications when drafting agency contracts.
This will be elaborated in the al-mukhtalaf-fih section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض وبقبض العين لا. وبقبض العين لا: أي الوكيل بقبض العين لا يملك الخصومة اتفاقاً، والفرق بين الوكيل بقبض الدين والوكيل بقبض العين عند أبي حنيفة أن الوكيل بقبض الدين وكيل بالتملك؛ لأن المقبوض ليس بملك للموكل، بل هو بدل حقه؛ لأن الديون تقضى بأمثالها لا بأعيانها فانتصب خصماً، بخلاف الوكيل بقبض العين، فإنه وكيل باستيفاء عين حقه، فلم يكن وكيلاً بالمبادلة، فصار رسولاً وأميناً محضاً، فلهذا لا ينتصب خصماً.
Chapter of agency for litigation and taking possession. And by taking possession of a specific object, not: meaning the agent for taking possession of a specific object does not own the litigation by agreement. And the difference between the agent for taking possession of a debt and the agent for taking possession of a specific object according to Abu Hanifa is that the agent for taking possession of a debt is an agent for ownership; because what is taken is not the property of the principal, but rather a substitute for his right; because debts are paid with their equivalents, not with their specific objects, so he becomes a litigant. Contrary to the agent for taking possession of a specific object, for he is an agent for receiving the specific object of his right, so he is not an agent for exchange, so he becomes merely a messenger and trustee; therefore he does not become a litigant.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with rational explanation. The matn states: 'And by taking possession of a specific object, no.' The commentary explains: The agent appointed specifically to collect a physical item (a 'ayn) does not have the authority to represent the principal in litigation, by agreement of the school. The reason according to Imam Abu Hanifa is that the agent for collecting a debt is an agent for ownership (wakil bil-tamlik) because the money collected is not the principal's property but a substitute for his right; debts are paid with equivalents, so the agent takes ownership on behalf of the principal and thereby becomes a litigant. In contrast, the agent for collecting a specific object is merely a messenger and trustee, receiving what is already the principal's property; he has no authority to exchange or litigate, so he cannot assume the role of a litigant.

Key Terms
وكيل بقبض العين (wakīl bi qabḍ al-‘ayn): An agent appointed to take possession of a specific, identifiable object (a ‘ayn) that is already owned by the principal.
وكيل بقبض الدين (wakīl bi qabḍ al-dayn): An agent appointed to collect a debt owed to the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the agent for debt have litigation authority while the agent for a specific object does not?
A) Because the agent for debt is a closer relative.
B) Because debts are fungible and the agent becomes an owner; specific objects are already owned by the principal.
C) Because the agent for debt is appointed by a judge.
D) Because the Hanafi school does not recognize agency for specific objects.

Key Takeaway
An agent for a specific object is a mere messenger and cannot litigate; an agent for a debt is an owner and can litigate.

The Rationale: Why Debt Collection Agents Can Litigate but Specific Object Agents Cannot
Explanatory rationale for the difference in litigation authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the legal reasoning (talil) behind the Hanafi distinction between agents for debt and agents for specific objects in terms of litigation authority, and apply this reasoning to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Debt is fungible; the agent becomes an owner. Specific object is owned; the agent is a messenger.

Having established the ruling, this lesson delves into the 'why'. The commentary provides a clear talil (rationale) from Imam Abu Hanifa: debts are paid with equivalents (amthāl), so the agent collecting a debt takes ownership on behalf of the principal; in contrast, a specific object is already the principal's property, so the agent merely receives what is already owned. This rationale explains the difference in litigation authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of agent roles
From the commentary, distinguish between an agent for ownership (wakīl bi al-tamalluk) and an agent for taking possession of a specific object (wakīl bi qabḍ al-‘ayn).
2
Analyze the nature of debt
Understand that debts are not specific objects but are fungible (mālī). When a debt is collected, what is received is a substitute for the right, not the principal's own property.
The text says 'because debts are paid with their equivalents'.
3
Analyze the nature of a specific object
A specific object (‘ayn) is already owned by the principal. The agent is simply receiving what is already the principal's property; there is no exchange (mubādala).
4
Derive the consequence for agency
Because the debt agent takes ownership (tamalluk), he becomes a litigant (khaṣm) because he has a stake. The specific object agent does not take ownership, so he is a messenger and trustee—no litigation authority.
5
Apply the talil to new cases
Consider: if an agent is appointed to receive a specific car, the same ruling applies. If an agent is appointed to collect money (which is fungible), the ruling of debt collection applies.
This is the practical application of the rationale.

Source Text

باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض وبقبض العين لا. وبقبض العين لا: أي الوكيل بقبض العين لا يملك الخصومة اتفاقاً، والفرق بين الوكيل بقبض الدين والوكيل بقبض العين عند أبي حنيفة أن الوكيل بقبض الدين وكيل بالتملك؛ لأن المقبوض ليس بملك للموكل، بل هو بدل حقه؛ لأن الديون تقضى بأمثالها لا بأعيانها فانتصب خصماً، بخلاف الوكيل بقبض العين، فإنه وكيل باستيفاء عين حقه، فلم يكن وكيلاً بالمبادلة، فصار رسولاً وأميناً محضاً، فلهذا لا ينتصب خصماً.
Chapter of agency for litigation and taking possession. And by taking possession of a specific object, not: meaning the agent for taking possession of a specific object does not own the litigation by agreement. And the difference between the agent for taking possession of a debt and the agent for taking possession of a specific object according to Abu Hanifa is that the agent for taking possession of a debt is an agent for ownership; because what is taken is not the property of the principal, but rather a substitute for his right; because debts are paid with their equivalents, not with their specific objects, so he becomes a litigant. Contrary to the agent for taking possession of a specific object, for he is an agent for receiving the specific object of his right, so he is not an agent for exchange, so he becomes merely a messenger and trustee; therefore he does not become a litigant.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with rational explanation. The matn states: 'And by taking possession of a specific object, no.' The commentary explains: The agent appointed specifically to collect a physical item (a 'ayn) does not have the authority to represent the principal in litigation, by agreement of the school. The reason according to Imam Abu Hanifa is that the agent for collecting a debt is an agent for ownership (wakil bil-tamlik) because the money collected is not the principal's property but a substitute for his right; debts are paid with equivalents, so the agent takes ownership on behalf of the principal and thereby becomes a litigant. In contrast, the agent for collecting a specific object is merely a messenger and trustee, receiving what is already the principal's property; he has no authority to exchange or litigate, so he cannot assume the role of a litigant.

Key Terms
وكيل بقبض العين (wakīl bi qabḍ al-‘ayn): An agent appointed to take possession of a specific, identifiable object (a ‘ayn) that is already owned by the principal.
وكيل بقبض الدين (wakīl bi qabḍ al-dayn): An agent appointed to collect a debt owed to the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the talil, why does the agent for debt become a litigant?
A) Because he is a relative of the principal.
B) Because the debt is fungible and he takes ownership of the substitute.
C) Because he is a judge.
D) Because the principal is unavailable.

Key Takeaway
The difference in litigation authority hinges on whether the agent takes ownership (debt) or merely receives what is already owned (specific object).

Third Party Proof Alters Agent's Possession
Ruling on third-party proof staying agent's possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on third-party proof against an agent and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas in this case.

Core Idea
Istihsan restricts the agent's hand without deciding ownership until the principal appears.

In this passage, we examine a nuanced ruling where a third party claims to have bought an object from the principal, while the principal is absent. The agent, appointed to take possession, faces a dilemma. The Hanafi school employs istihsan to partially accept the third party's proof, preventing the agent from taking possession but not finalizing the sale. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize the principal (موكل), agent (وكيل بالقبض), and third-party possessor (ذو اليد). Understand their roles.
2
Understand the default qiyas
By strict analogy (qiyas), the third-party's proof should be rejected entirely because it is against the agent, who is a litigant representative of the principal.
Qiyas gives full rejection.
3
Apply istihsan
Istihsan overrides qiyas here: accept the proof partially—only to restrict the agent's hand (qaṣr al-yad), not to prove the sale itself.
Partial acceptance is the key.
4
Analyze the reason
The agent is only a litigant regarding the hand (yad), not the underlying right (haqq). Hence, the proof affects the agent's authority but not ownership.
Distinction between yad and haqq.
5
Determine the thamarah
Practical outcome: the matter is stayed (wuquf) until the principal arrives; the agent cannot take possession.
This prevents hasty transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو برهن ذو اليد على الوكيل بالقبض أن الموكل باعه، وقف الأمر حتى يحضر الغائب. باعه من ذي اليد: أي باع الشيء الذي يدعي الوكيل قبضه. وقف الأمر إلخ: أي إذا وكل رجل رجلاً بقبض عبد له وغاب، فأقام ذو اليد بينة أنه اشتراه من الذي وكله بالقبض، لم تقبل بينته في إثبات الشراء، وتقبل في حق منع يد الوكيل عن قبض العين، ويتوقف حتى يحضر الموكل استحساناً، والقياس أن يدفع إلى الوكيل؛ لأن بينة ذي اليد قامت على خصم، فلم تعتبر، وجه الاستحسان: أن الوكيل خصم في قصر يده؛ لقيامه مقام الموكل في القبض، فإذا حضر الموكل أمر الخصم بإعادة البينة على ما ادعى به؛ لأن البينة قامت على نفس الحق وعلى قصر اليد، والوكيل خصم في حق اليد فحسب، فتقبل في حقه و تقصر يده عنه، كما إذا أقام الخصم البينة أن الموكل عزله عن الوكالة، فإنها تقبل في حق قصر اليد، لا في حق ثبوت العزل.
So if the possessor brings proof against the agent for possession that the principal sold it to him, the matter is stayed until the absent person arrives. 'He sold it from the possessor': meaning he sold the thing that the agent claims to have taken possession of. 'The matter is stayed etc.': meaning if a man appoints another to take possession of a slave of his and then disappears, and the possessor brings evidence that he bought it from the one who appointed the agent for possession, his evidence is not accepted to establish the purchase, but it is accepted to prevent the agent's hand from taking possession of the specific object, and it is stayed until the principal arrives by istihsan. By qiyas, it would be handed over to the agent because the possessor's evidence was brought against a litigant, so it is not considered. The basis of istihsan: the agent is a litigant in restricting his hand because he stands in the place of the principal in taking possession; when the principal arrives, the opponent is ordered to re-present the evidence for what he claimed, because the evidence was brought against the right itself and against the restriction of the hand. The agent is a litigant only in respect of the hand, so it is accepted regarding him and his hand is restricted, just as if the opponent brought evidence that the principal dismissed him from the agency; then it would be accepted for restricting the hand, not for establishing the dismissal.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof using istihsan. The matn states: 'If the possessor brings proof... the matter is stayed.' The commentary explains the scenario: A principal (absent) appoints an agent to collect a specific object (e.g., a slave). A third party (possessor) claims to have bought the object from the principal and provides evidence. Under strict qiyas, the evidence would be rejected because it is against the agent who is a representative. However, by istihsan, the evidence is accepted partially: it is not accepted to prove the sale itself (which would require the principal's presence), but it is accepted to prevent the agent from taking possession, thus staying the matter until the principal returns. The agent is considered a litigant only regarding the hand, not the underlying right. An analogy is drawn: if the possessor proves the agent was dismissed, that would also restrict the hand without proving dismissal.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): A juristic preference that overrides analogy (qiyas) to achieve a more equitable result.
قصر اليد (qaṣr al-yad): Restriction of the agent's physical control over the object; preventing him from taking possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario of a third party claiming to have bought the object from the principal, what is the effect of their proof on the agent?
A) The agent immediately takes possession and the third party must sue the principal.
B) The agent is prevented from taking possession until the principal arrives, by istihsan.
C) The proof is fully accepted and the agent loses the right to take possession permanently.
D) The proof is rejected entirely and the agent takes possession immediately.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan allows partial acceptance of third-party proof: restricts the agent's hand without deciding ownership, pending the principal's return.

Same Rule for Manumission and Divorce Agency
Extension of earlier ruling to agency for manumission and divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting evidence in agency for manumission or divorce and explain the distinction between restricting the agent's hand and adjudicating the substantive act.

Core Idea
Evidence restricting the agent's hand is accepted by istihsan, but the substantive ruling awaits the principal.

This lesson extends a key procedural principle to agency for manumission and divorce. When an agent is appointed to carry out an act like freeing a slave or divorcing a wife, and a third party brings evidence that the principal already performed the act, strict analogy (qiyas) would reject the evidence. However, by juristic preference (istihsan) and consensus, the evidence is accepted to prevent the agent from acting—but without making a final judgment against the absent principal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
An agent is appointed to effect manumission (transferring a slave to the master) or divorce (transferring a woman to her husband). A third party brings evidence that the principal already performed the act.
2
Understand the qiyas ruling
By strict qiyas, the evidence is rejected because the agent is not a proper litigant regarding the underlying right (manumission/divorce). The evidence is not brought against a specific opponent.
3
Learn the istihsan exception
By istihsan, the evidence is accepted to restrict the agent's hand (qasr yad al-wakil). The agent loses the ability to execute the act, but the substantive act (e.g., manumission) is not adjudicated against the absent principal.
The agent is considered a litigant only regarding his own authority, not the underlying right.
4
Note the consensus
This ruling is established by consensus (ijma') within the Hanafi school. The evidence is accepted only for the purpose of restricting the agent, not for anything else.
5
Apply the distinction
Distinguish between the immediate effect (agent cannot act) and the final effect (substantive ruling postponed until principal appears). This prevents the agent from wrongly executing an act that may have already occurred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك العتاق والطلاق: أي إذا وكل رجل رجلاً بنقل العبد أو الأمة إلى المولى أو بنقل المرأة إلى زوجها، وأراد الوكيل ما أمر به فأقام المملوك البينة على الإعتاق أو من هو بيده على الارتمان من الموكل، أو أقامت المرأة البينة على أن زوجها طلقها ثلاثاً، لا تقبل هذه البيئة قياساً؛ لقيامها لا على خصم، وتقبل استحساناً في قصر يد الوكيل دون القضاء بالعتق والرهن والطلاق ومن ضرورة ذلك قصر يد الوكيل، والوكيل ليس بخصم في أحد الأشياء المذكورة، وهو إثبات العتق والطلاق ولكنه خصم في قصر يده وليس من ضرورة قصر اليد القضاء بالعتق على الغائب، فقبلناها في القصر فقط دون غيره، وهذا بالإجماع.
And similarly manumission and divorce: meaning if a man appoints a man to transfer the male slave or female slave to the master, or to transfer the woman to her husband, and the agent wants to do what he was commanded, then the slave brings evidence of the manumission, or the one in whose hand he is brings evidence of the pledge from the principal, or the woman brings evidence that her husband divorced her three times; this evidence is not accepted by qiyas because it is brought against no litigant, but it is accepted by istihsan to restrict the agent's hand, without adjudicating the manumission, pledge, or divorce. From that necessity comes the restriction of the agent's hand. The agent is not a litigant in any of the mentioned things—that is, establishing manumission and divorce—but he is a litigant in restricting his hand, and it is not necessary from restricting the hand to adjudicate manumission against the absent person. So we accept it only for restriction, not for anything else, and this is by consensus.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage extends the previous ruling to agency for effecting manumission ('itaq) or divorce (talaq). The agent appointed to carry out these acts (e.g., transferring a slave to the master or a woman to her husband) faces a similar situation: if a third party brings evidence that the principal already manumitted the slave or divorced the wife, by strict qiyas the evidence is rejected because the agent is not a proper litigant regarding the underlying right. However, by istihsan, the evidence is accepted to prevent the agent from executing the act (restricting his hand), but not to establish the manumission or divorce as a final judgment. This is by consensus of the school. The agent is only a litigant concerning his own authority (yad), not the substantive right.

Key Terms
قصر يد الوكيل (qaṣr yad al-wakīl): Restricting the agent's ability to act (e.g., not allowing him to execute manumission or divorce) based on evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a slave brings evidence that the principal already manumitted him, how is this evidence treated regarding the agent for manumission?
A) It is fully accepted, and the manumission is declared effective immediately.
B) It is rejected entirely because the agent is not a proper opponent.
C) It is accepted to restrict the agent from carrying out the manumission, but not to establish the manumission itself.
D) It is accepted only after the agent agrees.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, evidence that the principal has already performed an act restricts the agent from doing it, but does not establish the act as final against the absent principal—a distinction based on istihsan and consensus.
