Agent's Acknowledgment in Court: Valid but Not Enforceable
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent for litigation's acknowledgment and explain why it is valid but not enforceable.

Core Idea
An agent's acknowledgment in court is valid as a response but not enforceable because it exceeds his mandate.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a key question: if an agent appointed for litigation acknowledges a debt in court, does it bind the principal? The Hanafi school distinguishes between validity and enforceability. The acknowledgment is considered valid as a statement, but it does not become binding on the principal because the agent was authorized only to litigate, not to admit liability. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this nuanced position and the intra-Hanafi variants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Exceptions
Read the matn: 'If the agent for litigation acknowledges before the judge, it is valid.' Note the exception for hudud and qisas (invalid due to shubha).
The ruling has two parts: general validity, and specific exclusion for hudud/qisas.
2
Understand the Distinction: Valid vs. Enforceable
The commentary explains that the acknowledgment is valid (sahih) as a judicial statement, but not enforceable (la yanfadh) because the agent acted beyond his mandate. Grasp this key distinction.
Enforceability here means the principal is bound.
3
Trace the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Identify the three positions: the 'three' (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf final, Muhammad), Abu Yusuf's earlier view (enforceable), and Zufar's view (invalid). Map each to their reasoning.
The author sides with the 'three' as the mufta bihi.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Proof (Dalil)
The agent's appointment includes 'absolute response' (jawaab mutlaq), and 'khusumah' is metaphorically used for any response. Explain this usul-based reasoning.
The proof is istihsan-based, not strict qiyas.
5
Apply to a Practical Scenario
Consider a case: agent for defendant acknowledges debt in court. Principal repudiates. What is the Hanafi ruling? The acknowledgment is valid but not enforceable; principal can deny.
This thamarah al-khilaf distinguishes Hanafi from Shafi'i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أقر الوكيل بالخصومة عند القاضي، صح: أي لو أقر الوكيل بالخصومة سواء كان من قبل المدعي أو المدعى عليه على موكله بالقبض أو الإبراء إن من جانب المدعي، وبلزوم المال إن كان من جانب المدعى عليه في مجلس القضاء، يصح إقراره عليه، وقال ولا ينفذ؛ لأنه أتى بغير المأمور؛ إذ هو مأمور بالخصومة عنه في مجلس القاضي، وما أتى به من الإقرار جواب، فلا يصح، ح، وبه قالت الثلاثة، وهو قول أبي يوسف أولاً وهو القياس. كان زفر : لا يصح. ولنا: أن التوكيل صحيح، فيدخل تحته ما يملكه الموكل وهو مطلق الجواب إقرارا كان أو إنكاراً، ويراد بالخصومة مطلق الجواب عرفاً مجازاً؛ لأنها سبب له، فذكر السبب وإرادة المسبب شائع، وقيد الوكيل بالخصومة؛ للاحتراز عن الوكيل بغيرها، كالوكيل بالصلح حيث لا يصح إقراره مطلقاً، ومسألة الكتاب يقيد بغير الحد فلا يصح إقرار الوكيل على موكله بالحد والقود للشبهة.
And if the agent for litigation acknowledges before the judge, it is valid: meaning if the agent for litigation acknowledges, whether he is from the plaintiff's side or the defendant's side, concerning his principal regarding taking possession or release if from the plaintiff's side, and regarding liability for wealth if from the defendant's side, in the court of the judge, his acknowledgment against him is valid. And it is said: it is not executed because he did something other than what he was ordered; for he was ordered to litigate on his behalf in the judge's court, and what he brought of acknowledgment is a response, so it is not valid. This is the opinion of the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), and it is Abu Yusuf's first opinion and it is the qiyas. Zufar said: it is not valid. Our proof: the appointment of agency is valid, so under it enters what the principal possesses, which is absolute response, whether acknowledgment or denial. And 'khusumah' (litigation) is used metaphorically for absolute response in custom, because it is a cause for it; so mentioning the cause and intending the effect is common. And the 'agent for litigation' is qualified to exclude other agents, like the agent for reconciliation, whose acknowledgment is not valid at all. And the issue of the book is qualified by other than hadd (fixed punishment), so the agent's acknowledgment against his principal for hadd and qisas (retaliation) is not valid due to doubt.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the validity and effect of an agent's acknowledgment in court. The matn states: 'If the agent for litigation acknowledges before the judge, it is valid.' The commentary explains: The acknowledgment is valid (sahih) as a statement, but it does not take effect (la yanfadh) because the agent was commanded to litigate, not to acknowledge. Abu Yusuf initially held that it is valid and enforceable, but later agreed with Abu Hanifa and Muhammad that it is valid but not executable. Zufar argued it is not valid at all. The Hanafi position (the 'three') is that the acknowledgment is valid because the agency for litigation includes the authority to respond, whether by acknowledgment or denial, as 'khusumah' is customarily used for any responsive statement. However, it is not enforceable because the agent overstepped his mandate. An exception is made for acknowledgments concerning hudud and qisas, which are invalid due to doubt (shubha).

Key Terms
الوكيل بالخصومة (al-wakīl bi al-khuṣūmah): An agent appointed specifically to represent the principal in litigation before a judge.
جواب مطلق (jawāb muṭlaq): Absolute response; any response in litigation, whether acknowledgment or denial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on an agent for litigation who acknowledges a debt on behalf of his absent principal in court?
A) The acknowledgment is invalid and has no effect.
B) The acknowledgment is valid and the principal is bound to pay.
C) The acknowledgment is valid but not enforceable; the agent may have acted beyond his mandate.
D) The acknowledgment is valid only if the principal had given permission to acknowledge.

Key Takeaway
An agent for litigation's acknowledgment in court is valid as a response but does not bind the principal because it exceeds the agent's mandate.

Agent's Acknowledgment Outside Court: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on agent's acknowledgment outside court

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi position on an agent's acknowledgment outside court, explain the reasoning of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and apply the ruling to cases where agency excludes acknowledgment.

Core Idea
An agent's acknowledgment outside court is invalid; judicially recognized responses are only in the judge's court.

In Hanafi fiqh, litigation (khusumah) has specific procedural requirements. One such requirement is that an agent's response—including acknowledgment (iqrar)—must occur in the judge's court (majlis al-qada) to be legally effective. This passage presents a dispute within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that acknowledgment outside court is invalid, while Abu Yusuf considers it valid because the agent is a deputy. The passage also examines a nuance: if the principal explicitly excludes acknowledgment from the agency, Muhammad distinguishes between claimant and defendant. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify when an agent's actions bind the principal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal context
We are dealing with an agent (wakil) appointed for litigation (khusumah). The act is the agent's acknowledgment (iqrar) of the claim outside the judge's court. Determine whether this acknowledgment is valid and what effect it has.
The agent's authority is metaphorical (majaz) for responding in court.
2
State the majority position and its proof
The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that acknowledgment outside court is invalid. Their reasoning: the agent's authority is 'by way of metaphor' for the opponent's response, and the only response that counts in judgment is the one given in the judge's court.
The text says 'at al-tarafayn' indicating the dominant view.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf argues that the agent is a deputy (na'ib) of the principal, so his acknowledgment is like the principal's own, which is valid anywhere. Court presence is not a condition.
This is a qiyas on the principal's own capacity.
4
Examine the rebuttal and tarjih
The two (and the relied-upon position) reject Abu Yusuf's analogy because the agent's role is specifically for court proceedings. The text favors the majority, as indicated by 'at al-tarafayn'.
The authority weight is zahir al-riwayah.
5
Apply the sub-issue: excluding acknowledgment from agency
If the principal explicitly prohibits the agent from acknowledging, Abu Yusuf says the agency is invalid entirely because 'denial-only' agency is not possible. Muhammad, however, distinguishes: the claimant (who is not forced to litigate) can validly appoint an agent for partial tasks, while the defendant (who is forced) cannot do so if it harms the claimant.
This distinction shows how legal capacity (compulsion) affects agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلا لا: أي وإن لم يقر الوكيل عند القاضي، بل أقر في غير مجلس القضاء، لا يصح عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف: يصح، ولا يشرط حضور مجلس الحكم؛ لأنه نائبه فصار إقراره كإقراره ينفذ أينما وجد، ولهما: أنه وكيل بجواب الخصم بطريق المجاز، والجواب المعتبر في الحكم هو الجواب في مجلس القاضي، لا في غيره، ولو استثنى الموكل بالخصومة الإقرار، فعن أبي يوسف: لا يصح؛ لأنه يكون وكيلاً بالإنكار فقط، وهو لا يملك ذلك عينا، التوكيل به، وعن محمد أنه فرق بين الطالب والمطلوب أي صححها في الطالب دون المطلوب، ووجه الفرق: أن الطالب لا يجبر على الخصومة، فله أن يوكل في شيء دون شيء على ما يختار، والمطلوب يجبر على الخصومة، فلا يملك التوكيل بما فيه إضرار للطالب.
And if not: meaning if the agent did not acknowledge before the judge, but rather acknowledged outside the court, it is not valid according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). And Abu Yusuf said: it is valid, and the attendance of the court is not a condition because he is his deputy, so his acknowledgment becomes like his own acknowledgment, enforceable wherever found. And for the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): he is an agent for the opponent's response by way of metaphor, and the response that is considered in judgment is the response in the judge's court, not elsewhere. And if the principal for litigation excludes acknowledgment, then according to Abu Yusuf: it is not valid because he becomes an agent only for denial, and that is not something he can own, so agency for it is not valid. And according to Muhammad, he distinguished between the claimant and the defendant: he corrected it in the case of the claimant but not the defendant. The basis of the distinction: the claimant is not forced to litigate, so he may appoint an agent for some things and not others as he chooses, while the defendant is forced to litigate, so he cannot appoint an agent in a way that harms the claimant.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage continues the dispute on the location of acknowledgment. The matn states: 'And if not, no.' The commentary explains: If the agent acknowledges outside the judge's court, the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold it invalid because the agent's authority is metaphorically for response in court; court response is judicially recognized. Abu Yusuf disagrees, saying the agent is a deputy and his acknowledgment is valid anywhere. The commentary then discusses two sub-issues: (1) If the principal explicitly excludes acknowledgment from the agency, Abu Yusuf holds the agency invalid (since denial-only agency is not valid); Muhammad distinguishes between claimant (valid) and defendant (invalid) because the defendant is compelled to respond in court.

Key Terms
مجلس القضاء (majlis al-qaḍā): The judge's court where judicial proceedings take place.
وكيل بالخصومة (wakīl bi al-khuṣūmah): An agent appointed specifically for litigation, with authority to respond in court.
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment or admission of a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is valid and enforceable because the agent is a deputy.

C) It is valid only if the principal is present.

Key Takeaway
An agent's acknowledgment outside the judge's court is invalid according to the relied-upon Hanafi position; the judicially recognized response is only in the court.

Why the Agency of a Surety for Wealth is Void
Ruling on the nullity of agency when the agent is the surety.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that agency is void when the agent has a personal interest in the subject matter, and explain the rationale.

Core Idea
A surety cannot be an agent to collect the guaranteed debt because he acts for himself, not the principal.

In Hanafi fiqh, agency (wakalah) requires that the agent acts on behalf of the principal. If the appointed agent is the surety of the debt, collecting it would free him from his own liability, making him act for himself. This lesson examines the ruling that such an agency is void from the start.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and transaction
Zayd is owed by Amr; Bakr is surety for Amr. Zayd appoints Bakr as agent to collect from Amr. The question is whether this agency is valid.
2
State the ruling and its basis
The text rules the agency void. The reason: the agent (Bakr) would be acting for himself (to discharge his surety), not for Zayd. The rukn of agency – acting for another – is missing.
This is a rational proof (talil) derived from the definition of wakalah.
3
Analyze the objection and response
An objection: if a creditor appoints the debtor to release himself, it is valid. The response distinguishes that as tamlik, not wakalah. This reinforces the principle.
We will explore this distinction in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطل توكيل الكفيل بمال: صورته: لو كان لزيد على عمرو مال فكفل به بكر فوكل زيد بكرا لقبض المال من عمرو، لا يكون بكر وكيلا في ذلك أبداً، ولم يصح التوكيل؛ لأن الوكيل هو الذي يعمل لغيره، ولو صححنا هذه الوكالة صار عاملاً لنفسه ساعياً في براءة ذمته فانعدم الركن، فبطل الوكالة. فإن قلت: الدائن إذا وكل المديون بإبراء نفسه عن الدين يصح وإن كان عاملا لنفسه ساعيا في براءة ذمته، قلت: هذا ليس بتوكيل، إنما هو تمليك كما في قوله لامرأته: "طلقي نفسك" فإنه تمليك حتى تقيد بالمجلس لا توكيل، وإذا بطلت الوكالة في الصورة المذكورة، فلو قبض الوكيل المال من المديون وهلك في يده لم يهلك على الطالب، بل يهلك من مال المديون ولو أبرأه عن الكفالة لا تنقلب الوكالة صحيحة لوقوعها باطلة ابتداء، كما لو كفل عن غائب؛ فإنه يقع باطلا، ثم إذا بلغه فأجازه لم يجز، وقيد بقوله : " بمال"؛ لأنه يجوز توكيل الكفيل بالنفس بالخصومة؛ لأن الواحد يقوم بهما.
And the agency of the surety for wealth is void: its form: if Zayd has wealth owed by Amr, and Bakr stands surety for it, and Zayd appoints Bakr as agent to collect the wealth from Amr, Bakr is never an agent in that, and the agency is not valid because the agent is one who acts for another. If we validate this agency, he would become an agent for himself, working to free his own liability, so the pillar (rukn) is missing, thus the agency is void. If you say: a creditor may appoint the debtor as agent to release himself from the debt, and it is valid even though he acts for himself, working to free his liability, I say: that is not agency; it is a form of giving ownership (tamlik), like one saying to his wife: 'Divorce yourself' – that is tamlik, even if restricted to the court, not agency. And if the agency in the mentioned form is void, then if the agent collects the money from the debtor and it perishes in his hand, it does not perish from the creditor's account, but rather from the debtor's account. And if he releases him from the suretyship, the agency does not become valid because it was void from the start, just as if one stands surety for an absent person, it is void; if it later reaches him and he ratifies it, it is not valid. And the author restricted it by 'for wealth' because it is permissible to appoint a surety for the person (nafs) as agent for litigation, because one person can handle both.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with rational explanation on the invalidity of an agency where the agent is also the surety (kafil) for the debt he is to collect. The scenario: Zayd is owed money by Amr; Bakr is surety for Amr. Zayd appoints Bakr as agent to collect the debt from Amr. This agency is void because Bakr would be acting for himself (collecting to free his own liability as surety), not for Zayd; thus the essential condition of agency (acting for another) is missing. An objection is raised: a creditor can appoint the debtor himself as agent to release the debtor from the debt, which is valid even though the debtor acts for himself. The response: that is not agency but tamlik (granting ownership), like saying to a wife 'divorce yourself,' which is a delegation of power, not agency. Consequently, if the agent-surety mistakenly collects the money and it is lost, the loss falls on the debtor, not the creditor. The invalidity is initial (batala ibtida'an), and later ratification does not cure it. The restriction 'for wealth' indicates that if the surety is for the person (kafil bi al-nafs), he can be appointed as agent for litigation, since one person can handle both responsibilities.

Key Terms
كفيل بمال (kafīl bi māl): A surety who guarantees payment of a debt; the surety becomes liable for the debt if the debtor defaults.
تمليك (tamlīk): Granting ownership or authority to another, as in telling the debtor to free himself from debt, which is not agency but a form of empowerment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the agency of a surety (kafil bi al-mal) to collect the debt void?
A) Because the surety is already the debtor’s agent.
B) Because the surety would be acting for himself, not for the principal, violating the essence of agency.
C) Because the debt is not yet due.
D) Because the surety is a minor.

Key Takeaway
Appointing a surety to collect the guaranteed debt results in a void agency because the agent's personal interest violates the fundamental requirement of acting for the principal.

Distinguishing Tamlik from Wakalah: The Debtor as Agent
Clarification of the distinction between tamlik and wakalah in the context of appointing a debtor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a valid tamlik (empowerment) and a void wakalah, and apply the distinction to scenarios where the appointee has a personal interest in the outcome.

Core Idea
When a creditor tells a debtor to free himself, it is tamlik, not agency — the action is delegated for the debtor's own benefit, not on behalf of the creditor.

A common objection to the void agency ruling is that a creditor can appoint the debtor to release himself from the debt, which seems to involve self-interest. The Hanafi jurists clarify that this is not agency but tamlik – a grant of authority to act for one's own benefit. This lesson explores that distinction and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
An objector argues: if a creditor appoints a debtor to discharge his own debt, it is valid even though the debtor acts for himself. This seems to contradict the earlier ruling.
The example is 'I appoint you to free yourself from my debt'.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response: that is tamlik, not wakalah. Tamlik transfers a right to the appointee to act for his own benefit, like telling a wife 'divorce yourself'.
Tamlik is a distinct legal concept: the appointee becomes the owner of the action.
3
Apply the distinction to agency
If the appointment is truly tamlik, the rules of agency (like acting for another) do not apply. The consequence: the money collected by the surety-as-agent perishes from the debtor's account, not the creditor's, because the collection was void.
The passage also clarifies that subsequent ratification cannot cure the void agency.

Source Text

Debtor's Confirmation of Agency: Must He Pay?
Ruling on agency claim and debtor's confirmation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a person claims to be an agent for an absent debtor and the debtor confirms, and distinguish between the matn's position and the view of the three imams.

Core Idea
Debtor's confirmation of agency does not necessarily compel payment according to the Hanafi majority.

This passage addresses a common scenario: someone claims to be the agent of an absent creditor, and the debtor confirms the agency. Should the debtor be forced to pay? The matn says yes, but the three founding imams of the Hanafi school disagree. We will unpack the reasoning and the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
A person claims to be the agent (wakil) of an absent creditor to collect a debt from the debtor. The debtor confirms that the claimant is indeed the agent.
2
State the Matn's Ruling
The matn rules: 'He is ordered to pay it to him.' The commentary clarifies this means the debtor is compelled to pay the debt to the claimant-agent.
Reason: the debtor's confirmation is an admission (iqrar) against himself.
3
Understand the Rationale
Debts are fungible (repayable in kind). By confirming the agency, the debtor acknowledges that paying the agent is equivalent to paying the principal. Therefore, he cannot later claim he paid the principal.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
The commentary states: 'And according to the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), he is not ordered to pay.' This means the majority view of the Hanafi school opposes the matn's ruling.
The three imams likely require additional evidence beyond mere confirmation.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The commentary sides with the three, so the relied-upon position is that the debtor is not compelled to pay based solely on confirmation. The matn's ruling may be from a weaker transmission or an earlier view.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah (the three's position).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ادعى أنه وكيل الغائب في قبض دينه فصدقه الغريم أمر بدفعه إليه. فصدقه الغريم: الذي عليه الدين أنه وكيله أمر بدفعه إليه المراد بالأمر أن الغريم يجبر على دفع الدين إلى مدعي الوكالة عن الغائب؛ لأنه أقر على نفسه؛ إذ الديون تقضى بأمثالها، فيكون مقراً بوجوب دفع ماله إليه، حتى لو ادعى أنه أوفى الدين إلى الطالب لا يصدق؛ لأنه لزمه الدفع إلى الوكيل بإقراره، فلا يثبت الإيفاء بمجرد دعواه وعند الثلاثة لا يؤمر بالدفع.
And whoever claims that he is the agent of the absent person to collect his debt, and the debtor confirms him, he is ordered to pay it to him. 'The debtor confirms him': the one who owes the debt [confirms] that he is his agent; he is ordered to pay it to him. The meaning of 'ordered' is that the debtor is compelled to pay the debt to the person claiming agency for the absent one, because he has acknowledged against himself; for debts are repaid with their equivalents, so he becomes acknowledging the obligation to give his money to him, to the extent that even if he claims that he had already paid the debt to the principal, he is not believed, because he has made it necessary to pay to the agent by his admission; thus the payment is not established by his mere claim. And according to the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), he is not ordered to pay.
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on a situation where a person claims to be the agent of an absent creditor to collect a debt, and the debtor confirms that the claimant is indeed the agent. According to the matn, the debtor is ordered to pay the debt to the claimant-agent. The commentary explains: The debtor's confirmation is an admission that binds him, because debts are fungible and paying the agent is equivalent to paying the creditor. Thus, the debtor cannot later claim that he already paid the principal. However, the commentary notes that according to the three major Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), the debtor should not be ordered to pay; there is an intra-school dispute. The text seems to present the matn's ruling as a starting point, with the commentary noting the disagreement.

Key Terms
الغريم (al-gharīm): The debtor who owes the debt.
الوكيل (al-wakīl): The agent appointed to collect the debt.
الغائب (al-ghāʾib): The absent creditor not present to claim the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The debtor must pay immediately because of his confirmation.
B) The debtor is not ordered to pay; the confirmation alone is insufficient.
C) The debtor must pay only if the agent produces a written document.
D) The debtor can choose whether to pay or not.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that a debtor is not compelled to pay based solely on his confirmation of a claimant's agency; the absent creditor's rights require stronger proof.

Agent's Confession in Litigation: Validity and Restrictions
Commentary on the general ruling of agent's confession in litigation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent's confession in litigation, identify the restriction for hudud and qisas, and explain the proof using istihsan based on custom.

Core Idea
An agent's confession in litigation is valid for financial claims but invalid for hudud and qisas due to legal doubt.

When you appoint someone to represent you in court, what can they admit on your behalf? The Hanafi school draws a clear line: the agent's confession is valid for debts and property, but not for fixed criminal punishments or retaliation. This lesson unpacks that ruling from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, showing how the term 'litigation' is understood broadly by custom, and why hudud and qisas are excluded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What legal act is being discussed? The agent's confession (iqrar) made during litigation. According to the text, it is valid (sahih) when the agent is appointed for litigation, but with exceptions.
The confession is a response (jawab) that binds the principal
2
Locate the dalil
What proof supports the validity? The commentator uses istihsan based on custom (urf): the term 'litigation' (khusuma) is customarily understood to include any response, confession or denial, because litigation is the cause for such responses.
Cause and effect reasoning: mention of cause implies the effect
3
Identify the restrictions
The text introduces two restrictions: (1) If the agent is appointed for reconciliation (sulh), his confession is invalid absolutely. (2) For hudud and qisas, the agent's confession is invalid due to legal doubt (shubha).
These restrictions limit the scope of the general ruling
4
Understand the basis for the exceptions
Why are hudud and qisas excluded? Because they are considered religious duties (ibaadaat) and require strict proof; agency for such matters is not recognized due to the gravity and the need for direct personal accountability.
Shubha (ambiguity) prevents enforcement of severe penalties through indirect confession

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة س ٥٠٦ باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض وإلا لا، وبطل توكيل الكفيل بمال، ومن ادعى أنه وكيل الغائب في قبض دينه فصدقه الغريم أمر بدفعه إليه، زفة أي لا يصح إقراره دينه، والقود، الدين مدعي الوكالة أي المدعي . ولنا: أن التوكيل صحيح، فيدخل تحته ما يملكه الموكل وهو مطلق الجواب إقرارا كان أو إنكاراً، ويراد بالخصومة مطلق الجواب عرفاً مجازاً؛ لأنها سبب له، فذكر السبب وإرادة المسبب شائع، وقيد الوكيل بالخصومة؛ للاحتراز عن الوكيل بغيرها، كالوكيل بالصلح حيث لا يصح إقراره مطلقاً، ومسألة الكتاب يقيد بغير الحد فلا يصح إقرار الوكيل على موكله بالحد والقود للشبهة رمز الحقائق: ۱۸۱/۲]
Book of Agency. Page 506. Chapter on Agency for Litigation and Taking Possession. And if not, no; and the appointment of the guarantor for money is void; and whoever claims that he is the agent of an absent person to collect his debt, and the debtor confirms him, he is ordered to pay it to him. [Garble] meaning: his confession is not valid. The debt and the retaliation; the debt of the claimant of agency, meaning the claimant. And for us: the appointment is valid, so what the principal owns enters under it, which is absolute response, whether confession or denial. And by litigation is meant absolute response by custom metaphorically, because it is a cause for it. Mentioning the cause and intending the effect is common. And the restriction of the agent to litigation is to avoid the agent for other matters, like the agent for reconciliation where his confession is not valid absolutely. And the issue of the book is restricted to other than fixed punishments (hadd) and retaliation (qisas), so the agent's confession against his principal regarding hadd and qisas is not valid due to the ambiguity. (Ramiz al-Haqaiq 2:181).
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq begins the chapter on agency for litigation and taking possession. The author first states that if the agent does not confess in court, it is not valid (explained later). Then he mentions that appointing a guarantor (kafil) as an agent to collect a debt is invalid. Then he discusses the case where someone claims to be the agent of an absent principal to collect a debt: if the debtor confirms his statement, the debtor is ordered to pay him. The commentary then clarifies that the agent's confession (in court) is valid because the principal's absolute response—whether admitting or denying—falls within the scope of the agency. The term 'litigation' (khusuma) in common usage refers to any response, even confession, because litigation is a cause for such a response. The mention of the cause (litigation) to imply the effect (response) is a common rhetorical device. However, the author notes that this general rule is restricted: if the agent is appointed for reconciliation (sulh), his confession is not valid at all. Furthermore, in the matn of the book, the ruling is restricted to cases other than hudud and qisas, because the agent's confession regarding these is not valid due to the legal doubt (shubha) involved. This reflects the Hanafi principle that agency for criminal matters is not recognized for severe corporal penalties, as these require strict proof.

Key Terms
الخصومة (al-khusuma): Litigation; in this context, it is used metonymically to mean any response (jawaab) given by the agent in court, including confession or denial.
الحد والقود (al-hadd wa al-qisas): Fixed corporal punishments (hudud) and retaliation (qisas). The agent's confession regarding these is not valid because of the legal doubt (shubha) involved.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the agent's confession regarding hudud and qisas?
A) It is valid if the agent was appointed for litigation.
B) It is invalid due to legal doubt (shubha).
C) It is valid only if the principal explicitly authorized it.
D) It is valid only if the confession is made in court.

Key Takeaway
An agent's confession in litigation is valid for financial claims but invalid for hudud and qisas because severe penalties require direct personal accountability and strict proof.

Agent's Confession Outside Court and Excluding Confession
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agency in litigation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of an agent's confession outside the courtroom and explain when a principal may validly exclude confession from the agency.

Core Idea
An agent's confession only binds the principal if made in court; a claimant may restrict confession, but a defendant may not.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents two related intra-Hanafi disputes concerning the scope of an agent's authority in litigation. The first asks whether an agent's confession is valid if made outside the courtroom. The second asks whether the principal can exclude confession from the agent's mandate. The school's relied-upon answers draw a sharp distinction between the roles of claimant and defendant, rooted in the nature of litigation and the coercion to respond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disputes
The passage addresses two distinct issues: (1) the validity of the agent's confession when made outside court, and (2) whether the principal can exclude confession from the agent's authority. Note the positions of Abu Hanifa's two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
The matn 'wa illa la' gives the Zahir al-Riwayah ruling on the first issue.
2
Analyse the first dispute: location of confession
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that confession outside court is invalid. Abu Yusuf alone holds it valid anywhere. Understand the dalil: the Sahibayn argue that agency is metaphorically for court response; Abu Yusuf argues the agent is a deputy and the principal's own confession is valid anywhere.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view.
3
Analyse the second dispute: excluding confession
Abu Yusuf says excluding confession is invalid because the agent becomes only a denier, which is not a valid object of agency. Muhammad distinguishes: a claimant (not forced to litigate) may exclude confession; a defendant (forced) may not, because it would harm the claimant.
Muhammad's distinction is the relied-upon fatwa.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if an agent confesses outside court per the mufta bihi, the confession is void and the principal can deny the debt. If the principal restricts confession, only a claimant-principal can do so; a defendant-principal's restriction is ineffective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلا لا: أي وإن لم يقر الوكيل عند القاضي، بل أقر في غير مجلس القضاء، لا يصح عند الطرفين، وقال أبو يوسف: يصح، ولا يشرط حضور مجلس الحكم؛ لأنه نائبه فصار إقراره كإقراره ينفذ أينما وجد، ولهما: أنه وكيل بجواب الخصم بطريق المجاز، والجواب المعتبر في الحكم هو الجواب في مجلس القاضي، لا في غيره، ولو استثنى الموكل بالخصومة الإقرار، فعن أبي يوسف: لا يصح؛ لأنه يكون وكيلاً بالإنكار فقط، وهو لا يملك ذلك عينا، التوكيل به، وعن محمد أنه فرق بين الطالب والمطلوب أي صححها في الطالب دون المطلوب، ووجه الفرق: أن الطالب لا يجبر على الخصومة، فله أن يوكل في شيء دون شيء على ما يختار، والمطلوب يجبر على الخصومة، فلا يملك التوكيل بما فيه إضرار للطالب. رمز الحقائق: ۱۸۱/۲]
And if not, no: meaning if the agent did not confess before the judge, but confessed outside the courtroom, it is not valid according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf said: it is valid, and presence in the court is not a condition, because he is his deputy, so his confession becomes like his confession, effective wherever it occurs. And for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): he is an agent for the opponent's response by way of metaphor, and the response considered in legal judgment is the response in the court of the judge, not elsewhere. And if the principal excluded confession from the litigation agency, there is a report from Abu Yusuf that it is not valid, because he would be an agent only for denial, and he does not possess that specifically as an object of agency. And from Muhammad: he distinguished between the claimant and the defendant, meaning he validated it for the claimant but not for the defendant. The basis of the distinction: the claimant is not forced into litigation, so he may appoint an agent for some matters and not others as he chooses; the defendant is forced into litigation, so he cannot appoint an agent in a way that harms the claimant. (Ramiz al-Haqaiq 2:181).
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage addresses two intra-Hanafi disputes regarding the agent's confession in litigation. First, the matn states 'وإلا لا' (and if not, no), meaning if the agent confesses outside the courtroom, it is invalid. The commentator clarifies that this is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). Abu Yusuf, however, disagrees: he holds that the agent's confession is valid wherever made, because the agent is the principal's deputy, and one's own confession is valid anywhere. The Sahibayn argue that the agent is only appointed for responding in court (the metaphorical sense of litigation), and only the response in court is legally recognized. Thus, they restrict validity to the courtroom. Second, the passage discusses whether the principal can exclude confession from the agent's authority. There are two reports: from Abu Yusuf, it is not valid at all to restrict the agent to denial only, because denial is not a substantive action that can be the object of agency. From Muhammad, he distinguishes: if the principal is the plaintiff (claimant), he may exclude confession because he is not compelled to litigate and can tailor the agent's authority. If the principal is the defendant, he cannot exclude confession because he is forced to respond, and restricting the agent to denial would harm the plaintiff by denying the claim without a proper defense. The relied-upon position likely follows the Sahibayn on the first issue and Muhammad on the second.

Key Terms
الطالب والمطلوب (al-talib wa al-matlub): Claimant (plaintiff) and defendant; used in the context of agency for litigation.
وكيل بالخصومة (wakil bi al-khusumah): Agent appointed specifically for litigation, to respond to claims in court.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical; here, the term 'response' (jawaab) is used metaphorically for the formal court response.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, when can a principal validly exclude confession from the agent's authority?
A) When the principal is a defendant.
B) When the principal is a claimant.
C) It is never valid.
D) It is always valid.

Key Takeaway
An agent's confession only binds the principal if made in court; a claimant may restrict the agent from confessing, but a defendant may not.
